Special
The Experts: The No. 1 Thing Investors Shouldn't Be Worried About But Are

1,364 words
6 June 2013
01:26
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

What is the No. 1 thing investors shouldn't be worried about over the next few years but are? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry, academic and other thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887324216004578483382771090240.html" that discussed how to face your investment fears and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Wednesday, June 5.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Investing in Funds & ETFs Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts stream, recent interactive videos and other exciting online content at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html?mg=inert-wsj"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/1444jcw" three of The Experts Vanguard Chief Investment Officer Gus Sauter, Bankrate.com's Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") and Garrett Planning Network founder Sheryl Garrett ("https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") as they discuss ways to brace for risks that could rock your investment portfolio, in a video chat that aired Monday, June 3.

Charles Rotblut: Washington Isn't the Problem for Now

I would say there are two things: government and interest rates.

I know there is much frustration with the continuing partisan battles occurring in Washington, but the economy has continued to grow despite the legislative stagnation. There is no doubt that the federal debt and future spending on both entitlement programs and defense programs will need to be addressed, but they are long-term issues. I don't see them significantly impacting market direction over the next few years.

Predictions about when the Federal Reserve will start to raise interest rates have been premature for four years. Eventually, those predictions will be right. But given expectations for slow economic growth both domestically and globally, I don't foresee it as a problem over the next few years. Plus, if interest rates do start to rise, they will likely be in reaction to stronger economic growth, which will, in turn, boost earnings growth.

Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

Frank Holmes: China's Imperfect, but So What?

Investors shouldn't worry about China's growth. Many mainstream reports fail to take into account the fact that the world has never experienced a population and urbanization boom like China. As the most populous country in the world, its leaders are focused on helping its residents achieve a higher standard of living. Along the way, there will be missteps. But highlighting China's economic imperfections is akin to saying the U.S. is a poor nation because impoverished areas exist.

I believe China's new leaders, President Xi Jinping and Premier Li Keqiang, will focus on reforms that will provide residents with opportunities, social security, increasing incomes and higher standards of living.

Frank Holmes is chief executive and chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors Inc.

Rick Ferri: The Bond Bubble Isn't as Frightening as It Looks

The biggest investment concern today is the so-called bond bubble. Interest rates are at an all-time low and this is causing an unprecedented and in my view, unwarranted bond-market scare. There is a reason interest rates are low. It's because inflation is low and falling, and the global economy is meandering along at best. Don't expect changes soon.

Besides, there's a good reason to own bonds in a portfolio. They provide protection against a certain type of risk the worst type of risk a risk than can permanently devastate a retirement account. A risk that is building as the stock market hits new highs. A risk that ultimately crushes many investors during steep bear-market declines.

A bond allocation helps protect a portfolio from its owner. People who maintain a stable bond allocation are less likely to panic in a bear stock market. Those who ignore prudence and over-allocate in a bull market tend to have bad endings as they panic sell in a bear market. This is five times more costly to a portfolio than always holding a prudent allocation to stocks and bonds and weathering through the storms. Don't let bond-bubble talk get in the way of good judgment.

Rick Ferri is founder of Portfolio Solutions LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Matt Hougan: Go Ahead and Worry. Up to a Point.

It's always good to worry. Just don't panic. Mostly, the answer is in the middle.

Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

Larry Zimpleman: The Ups and Downs Are Par for the Course

In general, I think most investors are more worried about volatility than they should be. Look at the 2008-2009 period and the financial crisis. How many articles did we see then talking about how it had wiped out the savings of an entire generation of people. And, here we are only four years later and the market is reaching new highs virtually every day. If you keep in mind that, in general, markets are tied to corporations and you feel comfortable that you are invested in companies that can grow their businesses and earnings over time, then the volatility that the market delivers is only an issue if you need to draw down the assets in the near future. If you know you won't need to draw on the assets for at least the next five years, volatility is not something that should keep you up at night.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

Michelle Perry Higgins: The Correction Is Coming. Relax.

The next big correction! Let me officially go on the record by predicting that another major market adjustment is just around the corner. It could happen a month from now or be three years away. No one can say when with absolute certainty. The stock market has a good track record of completing its cycles and I encourage investors to recognize that and stop worrying about the inevitable pullbacks. Instead of wasting energy with apprehension and fear, I would advise prudent investors to plan and educate themselves.

I employ a two-step planning process:

1. Have a well-diversified portfolio with a strong defensive barrier (cash and fixed income) to carry you through the down years.

2. Educate yourself on the historical cycles of the stock market. "History doesn't repeat itself, but it does rhyme." Mark Twain

See related graphic: "http://www.advisorperspectives.com/dshort/charts/markets/secular-bull-bear-markets.html?SP-Composite-secular-trends.gif"

Michelle Perry Higgins ("https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Meir Statman: Don't Worry About Market Timing

The No. 1 thing investors shouldn't be worried about but are is whether to get into the stock market now or get out. If you got out of the stock market in October 2007, good for you. If you got out the stock market in March 2009, too bad. Now, as always, is a good time to buy and hold a diversified portfolio.

Meir Statman is the Glenn Klimek professor of finance at Santa Clara University, and author of "What Investors Really Want."

Greg McBride: If Stocks Fall, Get Ready to Hang In There

The continued risk aversion of investors is predicated on their fear of a sharp pullback in the equity market. I'm not saying it won't happen it almost certainly will. But for investors with the discipline to hang in there, keep reinvesting their dividends and capital-gains distributions and the fortitude to buy more on a downdraft, they'll end up ahead when markets inevitably rebound, as the last six years have illustrated. But sitting on the sidelines or hunkering down in conservative investments won't get you to your financial goals.

Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Bilderberg event unites protesters against the unknown

By Hannah Kuchler in Watford 
566 words
6 June 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A former Labour minister, a Ukip MEP and a US arch conspiracy theorist gathered in a field just outside Watford on Thursday with one shared objective: to get the minutes of a meeting taking place inside the luxury hotel on the horizon.

Two hundred protesters were penned in by police and G4S guards on the edge of the estate in the English countryside where the Bilderberg group of political and business leaders has begun its secretive annual three-day event.

Fleets of Mercedes cars with blacked-out windows sped past, possibly transporting any of the 140 people named on the guest list. Invitees ranged from George Osborne, British chancellor, to Jos Manuel Barroso, head of the European Commission, and Eric Schmidt, executive chairman of Google, to Peter Sutherland, chairman of Goldman Sachs International.

"Scum!", "Maggots!" and "Pay your taxes!", demonstrators shouted at the cars, despite not being able to identify the passengers. Some refused to call themselves protesters, saying they objected to the lack of transparency, but could not protest against decisions they did not know about.

Michael Meacher, a Labour backbencher and former environment minister, was among the throng. He said the secrecy surrounding the meeting was "utterly anti-democratic". He has asked the speaker of the House of Commons for an urgent question to demand Mr Osborne reveals any plans made in the red-brick hotel.

"This is arguably the most important meeting of the leaders of western finance capitalism this year," he said. "I don't think they attend for just the tea and coffee."

Mr Meacher was on unlikely shared ground with Gerard Batten, a Ukip MEP for London, who suspected that decisions about Europe would be made during the meeting. "If they want to fix the world's problems, they do have to bring the people with them," he said.

Founded in 1954, Bilderberg was designed to foster dialogue between Europe and North America during the cold war. This year, it will discuss topics including growth, US foreign policy, cyber warfare, big data, Africa and the Middle-East.

Once cloaked in a shroud of secrecy, it has been slowly opening up over the past decade: it now has a website and publishes an agenda and a list of participants. It insists no resolutions are proposed and no votes are taken.

But that assurance has not been enough for protesters. Placards connecting the event with everything from eugenics to paedophilia show how the silence from inside the building has led to speculation outside.

"Bilderberg ate my hamster," reads one large banner draped across the metal barriers.

"We are assaulting the Bilderberg group with the search lights of publicity. Like Count Dracula!" bellowed Alex Jones, an American radio host and conspiracy theorist, from the middle of a swarm of supporters. His cornucopia of theories have included a plot to kill 80 per cent of the world's population and a plan to combine the US and the EU into a single super state.

Other campaigners connected the meeting with real world events, drawing parallels between the lack of transparency at Bilderberg and the lobbying scandal which has hit British politics in the past couple of weeks.

Hanging up at the heavily-guarded entrance to the field of protesters, one sardonic sign read: "Lobbying register: all corporate lobbyists please sign in here."

Andrew Lamb to retire as head of CME Clearing Europe

By Philip Stafford 
288 words
6 June 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Andrew Lamb is to retire as chief executive of CME Group's European clearing house, bringing to an end a 35-year career in London's market structure industry.

Mr Lamb, who joined CME Clearing Europe in 2009, was previously chief executive of LCH.Clearnet and had served for 15 years at the Bank of England, with four years as senior adviser, derivatives and commodity markets.

He was also head of risk and deputy chief executive at London Clearing House for a decade until 2003. He will retire on July 31, according to a memo circulated at CME last week.

Mr Lamb oversaw the establishment of CME's European business and the opening of its London office in May 2011. The world's largest futures operator began clearing off-exchange interest rate swaps earlier this year to challenge the market incumbent LCH.Clearnet for a share of the $500tn business. CME has also applied to become a futures exchange in the UK capital, a move that will put it into competition with Deutsche Brse's Eurex and NYSE Liffe.

He will be replaced by Lee Betsill, currently chief operating officer and managing director of CME Clearing Europe. Tina Hasenpusch will assume the role of chief operating officer, while continuing as head of business development. The appointments of Mr Betsill and Ms Hasenpusch will be subject to regulatory approval.

The memo also noted CME Clearing Europe has hired Marc Vial from JPMorgan to fill a new role as chief risk officer. Mr Vial, who was previously executive director, head of global risk strategy and market analytics group at the US bank, began working in early June.

Markets
Crude Oil Advances As Stockpiles Drop

By Dan Strumpf 
553 words
5 June 2013
21:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Oil prices rose after a government report showed U.S. crude stockpiles pulled back from an 82-year high last week, but gains were kept in check because of plentiful supplies.

Light, sweet crude for July delivery gained 43 cents, or 0.5%, to $93.74 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange on Wednesday.

About 6.3 million barrels were drawn from crude-oil stockpiles last week, the Energy Information Administration reported, outpacing the 400,000-barrel decline projected by analysts in a Dow Jones Newswires survey. It was the biggest drop in stockpiles this year.

"The market did not expect such a large crude draw," said Tony Headrick, energy-market analyst at CHS Hedging. "But the market did not shoot higher because supplies are [still] ample."

Oil stockpiles had been steadily rising, reaching their highest level since May 1931 in the prior week. Last week's pickup in demand from refineries and a 7% drop in crude-oil imports helped reverse the trend.

In a sign of the shifting U.S. oil landscape, the volume of crude oil produced in the U.S. last week outpaced oil imports for the first time since January 1997. Domestic production surged to 7.3 million barrels a day in the week ended May 31, helped by surging flows from shale fields, while oil imports were at their lowest since September 2008.

Rising domestic production is helping to cushion the market against higher prices.

The price of Nymex crude, the U.S. benchmark, has risen 2.1% since the start of the year, while Brent crude is down 7.3%.

Oil stockpiles now stand at 391.3 million barrels, 1.7% above last year's level.

"Crude-oil inventories are still exorbitantly high and it's a big cost of capital having all that crude just sitting there," said Kyle Cooper, managing partner at IAF Advisors in Houston.

Refineries are processing more crude now that the summer driving season is under way. Refineries used 88.4% of their total capacity last week, the EIA reported, up two percentage points from the previous week. Analysts had expected just a half-percentage-point rise.

Gasoline stockpiles last week fell 400,000 barrels, according to the EIA.

Inventories of distillates, including heating oil and diesel, rose 2.6 million barrels, as demand for those products declines with the warmer weather across the U.S.

Oil-market watchers are likely to turn their attention on Friday to the monthly U.S. nonfarm-payrolls report for clues about the health of the job market in the world's biggest oil consumer. High unemployment in the U.S. has kept fuel demand restrained with fewer motorists on the road.

Earlier Wednesday, Automatic Data Processing and Moody'sAnalytics reported 135,000 new private-sector jobs in May, below the 170,000 expected. The ADP report is seen as a preview to Friday's employment report.

Front-month July reformulated gasoline blendstock, or RBOB, rose 0.48 cent, or 0.2%, to settle at $2.8230 a gallon. Front-month July heating oil fell 0.95 cent, or 0.3%, to $2.8554 a gallon.

Sarah Jacob contributed to this article.

Write to Dan Strumpf at "mailto:daniel.strumpf@dowjones.com"

The Experts: Are 529 Plans the Right Choice for All Families Saving to Send Their Kids to College?

1,692 words
5 June 2013
14:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Are 529 plans the right choice for all families saving to send their kids to college? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry, academic and other thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887324767004578487301380805758.html" that discussed websites that aim to make it easy for family and friends to add to an account with a state-sponsored 529 college-savings plan and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Tuesday, June 4.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Investing in Funds & ETFs Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts stream, recent interactive videos and other exciting online content at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html?mg=inert-wsj"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/1444jcw" three of The Experts Vanguard Chief Investment Officer Gus Sauter, Bankrate.com's Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") and Garrett Planning Network founder Sheryl Garrett ("https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") as they discuss ways to brace for risks that could rock your investment portfolio, in a video chat that aired Monday, June 3.

Greg McBride: These Plans Are Great, if Not in Every Situation

I'm a big fan of 529 college-savings plans because of the tax-free growth and withdrawals for money used for college expenses. There are plenty of low-cost, direct-sold plans available, and the menu of available investment options has become further diversified in recent years. I hesitate to characterize anything as right for everybody, and one instance where the benefits of a 529 college-savings plan may not have time to accrue is for those that are looking at very short investment horizons such as one year before beginning to take withdrawals. The benefit of tax-free growth is very limited over such a short period of time in low-return, no-risk investments, and could be offset by investment expenses or plan fees in the short run.

Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for "http://www.bankrate.com/", providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Charles Rotblut: The Key: Start Early

Qualifying for college aid is dependent on several variables and I'll defer to my peers in this column on whether 529 plans are the best vehicle for college savings.

What I will say is that parents' top concern should not be the type of account, but rather that they are regularly contributing to college. Just as is the case with retirement, the earlier one starts saving and the longer they continually contribute to a properly allocated portfolio, the greater the chance they will have enough in savings once the child enters college.

Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

Michelle Perry Higgins: Not a Slam Dunk for Every Family

A 529 savings plan is definitely a top contender in the college-savings choices. But this plan is not a slam dunk for all families. Before opening a 529 plan or any college-savings account, it's important to evaluate the pros and cons of each plan and then decide which plan, if any, best aligns with your family goals. Let's review a few of the advantages and disadvantages of the 529 plan.

Some of the advantages of a 529 plan are:

 "The earnings that accumulate are tax-deferred, withdrawals are exempt from federal income tax and are also generally exempt from state tax for "qualified higher education expenses" (books, supplies, tuition, fees, room and board). Note: This does NOT include a new car or a shopping spree for the beneficiary.

 "The beneficiary and account holder must be identified when opening the account. After that, friends and family members can also contribute to the plan.

 "If the beneficiary does not use all the funds or decides not to attend college, the account holder can change the beneficiary to another family member (i.e. sibling, parent).

 "Funds may be used at any eligible higher-education institution in the U.S. as well as some colleges overseas.

 "The minimum contribution is typically very low, thus making it more affordable for families to get started.

 "The account holder has control of the funds, not the beneficiary.

Some of the disadvantages of the 529 plan are:

 "Plans will vary from state to state, which is a little more challenging for families. Therefore, you need to do your due diligence on the sales charges, fees, and investment choices by the plan administrator.

 "When a withdrawal is taken but it is not used for "qualified higher education expenses," then the earnings may be subject to income tax plus a 10% penalty tax.

 "A 529 account may reduce your beneficiary's ability to receive financial aid.

Visit the Internal Revenue Service website for more details:

"http://www.irs.gov/uac/529-Plans:-Questions-and-Answers"

Sources: "http://www.collegesavings.org/", "http://www.savingforcollege.com/", "http://www.scholarshare.com/". Back Room Technician.

Michelle Perry Higgins ("https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Matt Hougan: Yes, for the Vast Majority of People

For all families? No. The world is a complex place.

But for the vast majority of people? Absolutely. Assuming you get a low-cost plan stocked with low-cost index funds or ETFs and those are big assumptions a 529 is a great choice.

Just remember: You have to save vastly more money than you think you should. College prices and price inflation are insane.

Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

George Papadopoulos: 529 Plans Are Not My First Choice

If your goal is to save for your kids' college education, before deciding where to put the money, you must save the money beginning right now! 529 plans are still a great choice for most parents, but I must admit that I am not a big fan. I find the restrictions in changing investments, the limited investment options and the steep penalties if the money is not used for college to be substantial drawbacks. I prefer to first maximize the contributions to a Coverdell Education Savings Account, which can be held at any discount broker and which give you complete freedom to invest the funds as you see fit. Unfortunately, that maximum contribution is only $2,000 per year; it is obvious that even the best financial outcome will not cover more than a year or two of college costs. After that, I prefer to invest in low-cost Vanguard ETFs in taxable accounts so I can be in control of my own destiny while hoping that my two kids will shock me by receiving full rides to their preferred schools. If that happens, the money in taxable accounts can be added to our retirement nest eggs.

The 529 plan I like the most is the Utah state plan with its lower fees and an adequate number of all Vanguard investment fund choices.

George Papadopoulos ("https://twitter.com/feeonlyplanner") is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families.

Eleanor Blayney: The Best Conditions for a 529 Plan

While a great college-savings tool, 529 plans are not necessarily right for every family.

Generally 529 plans are advantageous under the following conditions:

 "The plan is established for a very young beneficiary. This allows the chief benefit of 529 plans namely, tax-free compounded growth to provide a true advantage over other taxable forms of funding.

 "There is more than one child in the family. The tax-free benefits not used for qualified college expenses by a 529 beneficiary can be transferred to a sibling, and therefore are not lost.

 "The plan is owned by a grandparent or nonparental relative. In this case, the assets in the 529 have no impact on the student's eligibility for financial aid. However, even when a parent owns the 529, the impact is minimal as a result of 2005 changes in financial-aid rules. Only 5.6% of the 529 plan assets are now assumed eligible for college costs, as opposed to 20% for custodial plans. Because of this differential, many parents transfer existing UTMA or trust accounts into 529s.

 "The plan is established in a state that offers residents a credit against state income taxes.

Finding the right 529 plan, given your income and estate-tax situation and your other assets and liabilities, is not easy, however. There are hundreds of 529 plans from which to choose, varying by provider, participation eligibility, investment management, investment options and fees.

Consider, if possible, investing some funds in a 529 prepaid tuition plan. Many states have closed down this option; furthermore, it's usually assumed that this makes sense only if the beneficiary will attend a school in the state providing the plan. But generally you can transfer the money-market equivalent of the prepaid plan to a college in another state. So why not use this option by putting the money you would otherwise put in a money market into a 529 savings plan? That way you keep more windows open for your student, and also diversify the risk of your invested funds.

Eleanor Blayney ("https://twitter.com/EleanorBlayney") is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards.

Rick Ferri: Not All 529 Plans Are Alike

I try never to use the words "never" or "all" when writing about personal finance. Everyone is different, and every situation is different. That being said, most families will find that a 529 plan provides an excellent way to save in order to send kids and grandkids to college. Not all 529 plans are alike. I recommend selecting a plan that has low fees and the ability to invest in low-cost index funds. "http://www.bogleheads.org/wiki/Category:529_Plans" on 529 plans that can help sort through the maze.

Rick Ferri is founder of Portfolio Solutions LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Markets
Dollar Slumps on Weak Jobs Report

By Matthew Walter 
504 words
5 June 2013
21:07
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The dollar weakened after a disappointing report on the U.S. labor market led to increased uncertainty about whether the economy can sustain its pace of recovery.

Private-sector jobs in the U.S. increased by 135,000 last month, according to a report from payroll processor Automatic Data Processing Inc. and forecasting firm Moody'sAnalytics. That fell short of the 170,000 gain expected by economists surveyed by Dow Jones.

Stocks tumbled along with the dollar after the report, which is sometimes seen as a leading indicator of the more closely watched nonfarm payrolls report from the Labor Department, which is due Friday. Investors are concerned that a slowdown in job growth may lead the Federal Reserve to maintain its bond-buying program for longer, keeping interest rates low and weighing on the dollar.

"We've got soft stocks and soft data, and the dollar has taken a hit," said Richard Franulovich, a senior currency strategist at Westpac Banking Corp.

Speculation about when the Fed will begin to taper its bond-buying program has been a key driver for the dollar this year. Central bank officials have publicly discussed their divergent opinions about how quickly the stimulus program should be ended. In the latest comments, Dallas Fed President Richard Fisher reiterated his view Wednesday that the housing market is in a solid rebound, giving the central bank room to begin pulling back from its bond buying sooner rather than later.

In late-afternoon trading, the dollar was at 99.16 from 100.03 late Tuesday. Against the Swiss franc, the dollar was at CHF0.9423 from CHF0.9468.

The Wall Street Journal Dollar Index, which tracks the U.S. dollar against a basket of currencies, was at 74.34 from 74.502.

The move down in the dollar gave more support to the yen, which started gaining overnight after a speech by Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe, in which he laid out his long-awaited growth strategy. Investors said the blueprint fell short of expectations, increasing demand for the yen, which is a popular safe haven.

The euro saw modest gains against the dollar ahead of Thursday's policy meeting at the European Central Bank. Euro-zone policy makers are expected to leave monetary policy unchanged after cutting rates last month.

"The euro remains generally resilient ahead of the ECB meeting tomorrow," said Valentin Marinov, a currency strategist at Citibank. "One reason seems to be investors' anticipation that the ECB may signal a more constructive outlook on the euro-zone economy."

The euro changed hands at $1.3092 compared with $1.3081 late Tuesday. The common currency bought 129.82 from 131.00.

In the U.K., a report showed that a key gauge of activity in the services sector rose to the fastest pace in more than a year in May.

The U.K. pound fetched $1.5401 from $1.5313.

Write to Matthew Walter at "mailto:matthew.walter@dowjones.com"

Carlyle to sell New York skyscraper for $1.3bn

By Anjli Raval in New York 
536 words
2 June 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Carlyle Group has agreed to sell a New York office tower for $1.3bn, the largest deal for a US building since 2010.

The Washington-based private equity group has spearheaded a deal to sell its 600,000 square foot 650 Madison Avenue office and retail block to a joint venture between retail developer Crown Acquisitions and investment firm Highgate Holdings, for almost twice what it paid for it five years ago.

Carlyle led a group of investors that bought the 27-storey glass-fronted skyscraper in one of Manhattan's prime tourist and office areas for $680m in 2008, according to research firm Real Capital Analytics, before refurbishing and leasing out the property.

If the deal goes through, it will the biggest sale of a single building in the US since Google bought the 2.9m square feet 111 Eighth Avenue in December 2010 for $1.8bn.

"This was one of the best office and retail investments made during the financial crisis," said Robert Stuckey, managing director and head of US real estate at Carlyle.

The sale marks the second profitable exit of a 2008 investment. Carlyle teamed up with Crown to buy a majority stake in the retail portion of 666 Fifth Avenue for $524m which it later sold for $1.08bn after introducing new tenants including Japanese retailer Uniqlo, surf-themed clothing brand Hollister and fast fashion chain Zara

The latest deal would equate to $2,167 per sq ft, which would beat the last record price for an office tower in New York of $1,566 per square feet paid by the private equity firm Somerset Partners for the 325,000 square feet 450 Park Avenue tower ahead of the financial downturn in 2007.

Haim Chera, managing principal of Crown, emphasised the difference between 650 Madison and 450 Park, saying the price was largely a reflection of the 75,000 sq ft of ground-floor retail space, which he values at $1bn. The property is located in one of the world's most expensive and highly-coveted retail districts close to the landmark Plaza Hotel and Central Park.

"The property stands at the centre of a 'retail triangle' with Barneys to the north, Bergdorf Goodman to the west and Bloomingdale's to the east making it one of the most desirable retail locations anywhere," Mr Chera said.

Investors have sought after New York trophy assets as haven investments, which has resulted in rising property values. While many office properties have struggled to recover from the financial downturn as corporations downsize or seek cheaper alternatives, retail properties - particularly along the high-profile shopping corridors popular with tourists and luxury customers - have performed better.

Crown and Highgate will borrow close to half the purchase price, meaning they intend to have about $650m of equity in the property. They thwarted rival bids from others including HFZ Capital Group, Vornado Realty Trust, Brookfield Office Properties and General Growth Properties.

The property is 92 per cent leased. 650 Madison serves as Polo Ralph Lauren's international corporate headquarters and has large retail tenants including Crate & Barrel and luxury designer Tod's.

ANALYSIS
The pension gap

By John Authers 
1,046 words
28 May 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
London Ed1
11
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fee Paying more to fund managers does not mean better returns. By John Authers

With its national parks and miles of stunning coastline, the English county of Devon has obvious attractions to pensioners. But there are less obvious attractions, too.

Devon's pension fund pays relatively little in fees to investment managers compared with other English counties - yet the performance of the fund is in line with its peers. Staffordshire's pension fund pays more than three times as much, for a fund that is almost identical.

The difference, revealed by a ground-breaking study based on annual reports of the 100 local authority pension plans in the UK , provided to the FT by Investor Data Services, a London consultancy, was not unusual. The most expensive quarter of funds paid an average of more than four times as much for investment management as the cheapest quarter.

With returns falling, fund managers across the world face complaints about fees and a lack of transparency. Active managers are under pressure to justify their fees in the face of growing competition from cheaper passive funds that merely track benchmark indices. These findings will fuel debate over how UK council pensions should be run and whether funds are sufficiently transparent.

The council's funds are managed by some of the biggest names in global asset management. But it is impossible from public data to ascertain the fees and commissions each charge.

UK council plans, which managed 187.3bn between them at the end of the last financial year, are typical of many across the world - but the UK's public-sector disclosure rules allow a look at fees that might stay hidden.

Neither Staffordshire nor Devon is exceptional; they are an interesting example because they appear similar. They are the same size: Devon had 2.68bn in March 2012, against 2.62bn for Staffordshire. They have a similar spread of assets in fixed income, equities and property, with a little money given to hedge fund managers. Both hold the same top three stocks (Royal Dutch Shell, Vodafone and HSBC) while three fund managers work for both counties. They follow the same public-sector procurement rules.

Yet Staffordshire paid 7.152m for fund management in 2011-12, while Devon paid 2.669m. And Staffordshire's bill for administration came to 2.033m, while Devon paid 1.225m.

Over the preceding eight years, Devon paid 3.9m less in investment fees per year and 850,000 less in administration fees. That means Staffordshire paid 38.2m more than Devon for essentially the same job. In overall return over the past decade, Devon has gained 6 per cent a year and Staffordshire 5.7 per cent - in line with the average council, according to State Street Investment Analytics.

Staffordshire county council said: "Hymans Robertson, the actuarial and investment consultancy, has conducted extensive analysis of fee statistics for local government pension schemes and has confirmed that our level of fees are consistent with those paid by other authorities.

"Fund structures can be very complex, so even if they appear to be superficially the same, without detailed analysis it is very difficult to make a 'like for like' comparison."

Council annual reports do not offer benchmarks for costs. It is only by collating cost data, often buried in footnotes, that discrepancies emerge.

One reason anomalies develop is the length of the value chain for pensions. The final return to investors is stated but it is unclear how much this has been reduced by avoidable fees.

Hymans Robertson, which could not comment on individual cases, said that funds the size of Devon or Staffordshire should be paying fees of 25 to 35 basis points. Anything lower would imply that they were "not fully disclosing fees". This could be because they invest in pooled funds "where the manager fees are charged within the fund and reflected in the unit price rather than paid explicitly" by the council. (Devon had a 6 per cent allocation to pooled funds in its latest report). High charges could stem from "funds of funds".

Officials at Devon said they doubted that a cost benchmark would be useful because strategies vary widely.

Another explanation for the discrepancies is that not all the necessary data are available to the public. The costs that funds' investment managers incur in buying and selling stocks are shown in report footnotes (Staffordshire's managers spent 3.28m on commissions, Devon's 959,000). Commission charges vary hugely across the sector, according to IDS's data.

When Christopher Sier, an independent consultant, built a database on commissions using freedom of information requests, he estimated average portfolio turnover at about 120 per cent a year - which seemed high as many funds were passive. But firm conclusions were impossible because information was redacted, often including the names of both the fund managers and their brokers.

Local authorities are already attacking costs. Some are forming consortiums to award contracts or planning to merge their administration. Concentration of managers is falling, with the top five managers accounting for 38 per cent of externally managed assets, from 56 per cent a decade ago.

They are also moving from active managers to passive index-trackers, which now account for 24 per cent of assets, according to the WM UK Local Authority Fund Annual Review - up from 14 per cent 10 years earlier.

Some are negotiating more aggressively with their fund managers. Linda Selman, of Hymans Robertson, said: "They simply ask managers what fee concessions they can make and explain the environment they are living with. That's had a pretty good level of success. When managers have been put under pressure they've offered maybe 10 per cent of the fee."

Some also negotiate on outcomes. One small council cut its investment management fees to only 0.05 per cent in one year because it had entrusted all its assets with one fund manager, in return for a rebate if performance dropped below an agreed benchmark. The manager failed to meet its three-year benchmark and forfeited fees.

The IDS research suggests pension funds will need to offer far more transparency about their charges - and that fund managers can look forward to even more aggressive negotiations in future.

The dollar is under mild pressure across the board Monday but the moves are small and trading ranges tight across currency markets with the U.K. and U.S. markets closed.

The commodity-linked currencies of Australia, New Zealand and Canada recovered from the losses made in Asia, while the euro marked a fresh day high of $1.2949 against the buck during Monday's European trading session.

But with traders out in London--the world's largest FX dealing hub--volumes were light. And there was little in the way of newsflow.

"There's been no big data out...so there nothing fundamentally driving moves," said Lutz Karpowitz, a senior currency strategist at Commerzbank AG in Frankfurt.

The subdued tone is likely to continue for the rest of Monday, with markets also shut in the U.S. later for Memorial Day.

When traders return to work Tuesday the focus will continue to be on the U.S. as market participants look for signs for when the Federal Reserve might start tapering back its bond purchases.

"With the Fed firmly in data-dependent mode, incoming data and Fed officials' comments will continue to drive the dollar's performance," foreign-exchange strategists at Barclays said in a note to clients, pointing out that consumer confidence data for May due Tuesday and Chicago's PMI reading published Friday are important releases to watch.

Elsewhere, the yen was steady against the dollar, despite a 3.2% fall on the Nikkei Stock Average. With a large part of the FX market out, comments from Bank of Japan Governor Haruhiko Kuroda calling on commercial banks with solid capital positions to be more aggressive in lending, also had little impact on the yen. Similarly, minutes from the Bank of Japan's April 26 meeting published overnight, in which the board appeared to be split over the interpretation of rising government bond yields, failed to stir the Japanese currency.

Elsewhere, the Swedish krona recovered fully from its fall against the euro on weaker-than-expected retail sales data for April. These rose only 2% on the year, compared with a forecast of a 3.2% rise, according to a survey by Dow Jones Newswires.

"Although the data is a bit lower than expected its still positive," said Mr. Karpowitz.

Swedish gross domestic product data for the first-quarter due Wednesday, is likely to be more important for the currency.

"We expect a weak first-quarter GDP, well below the Riksbank's forecast, and if we are right, it will likely strengthen calls for a new rate cut from the Riksbank in July, which is not fully priced in," said Sverre Holbek, a senior analyst at Danske Bank said.

At 1058 GMT, the euro was trading at $1.2946 against the dollar, compared with $1.2925, late Friday in New York, according to trading system EBS. The dollar was at 100.86 against the yen, compared with 101.38, while the euro was at 130.58, compared with 130.97. The pound was trading at $1.5134 against the dollar, compared with $1.5127 late Friday in New York.

The Wall Street Journal Dollar Index, which tracks the U.S. dollar against a basket of major currencies, was at 75.14 from 75.257.

Bay Area -- The Valley: Venture Firms Build Own Software to Gain an Edge

By Evelyn M. Rusli 
701 words
23 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13B
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

A new website named Grove popped up earlier this year to help entrepreneurs find potential recruits, talk to other startups and track rivals' growth.

That is if the entrepreneur is backed by Sequoia Capital, the well-known venture-capital firm, which built the service for the companies it has invested in.

The 41-year-old Sequoia is one of a growing number of Silicon Valley venture-capital firms now creating proprietary software to gain an edge in the increasingly competitive industry.

Some are creating apps to scout potential deals by aggregating data across the Web and their community of companies. Others are building software -- like Grove, named after clusters of sequoia trees -- to help grow companies they have already invested in.

Sequoia is doing a bit of both.

Grove also features an analytics service the firm developed to help track rising startups to invest in, says Bryan Schreier, a general partner at Sequoia. The service, called Rivalytics, tracks traffic for a mobile app based on public download data provided by Apple Inc. and Google Inc. and private data from Sequoia's companies. By matching the data sets and using statistical analysis, Rivalytics can estimate an app's usage.

Mr. Schreier says the firm had previously created services for its startups by using a hodgepodge of applications from its past investments in companies like Google and LinkedIn Corp. and building stand-alone tools. It built the new website to integrate it "all into a 2013 look and feel," he says.

A Sequoia spokesman declined to say which potential investments the software has helped spot.

Meanwhile, Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers has been investing in a similar service: an internal program called Dragnet, named after the famous television police series. Built in 2011, the Dragnet website pulls data from multiple sources such as app stores, news sources and LinkedIn to help the firm's partners identify rising startups. Kleiner also shares data from Dragnet with its companies to help them understand broad market trends.

A Kleiner spokeswoman declined to comment on which investments it helped mine from the website, but says it has been helpful.

The new software comes as venture capitalists battle to differentiate themselves. Some have increased their marketing budgets; others have hired armies of specialists in areas like recruiting.

Josh Kopelman, a managing partner at First Round Capital, says hosting a platform that pools resources and insights from its community increases the value of the firm to its entrepreneurs. Mr. Kopelman describes it as another tool in his toolbox when he is courting new investments.

"We do a reverse pitch, where we can pitch them on our platform," says Mr. Kopelman.

He says First Round Capital has spent more than $1 million building an online service that allows its venture capitalists and portfolio companies to ask questions, search job candidates and compare salaries. The firm hired two engineers to build and manage the service.

Mr. Kopelman declined to disclose the service's traffic but says there are employees from 170 companies on the site and they ping its Q&A service hundreds of times a month. "This is about building the DNA into our firm," says Mr. Kopelman.

At least one venture-capitalist-turned-entrepreneur is trying to sell collaboration software to venture firms that don't want to build their own.

Paul Singh, a former partner at seed investment firm 500 Startups, left the company earlier this year to work on his startup, Dashboard.io, full-time. The service is based on software he built to help companies that 500 Startups invested in to network among themselves. Currently, about 50 firms are using it.

The service is free and Mr. Singh wants to eventually make money through charging for premium features. He is considering a tool that will allow startups to compare metrics, such as how often they deploy code, to anonymous data from other companies using the system.

The company wants to "be the Bloomberg of private startups," says Mr. Singh, referring to the media company's data platform for finance professionals.

Wealth Management Journal
Advisers Coax Investors Off Sidelines

By Murray Coleman 
764 words
22 May 2013
14:10
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The four-year rally in U.S. stocks is proving more resilient than many investors had expected, prompting some portfolio managers to steer their affluent clients away from staying on the sidelines in cash or fixed-income.

To coax clients back into equities, advisers remind them that today's market isn't set up for a fall like the tech bubble of 1999-2000 or the financial crisis of 2008-2009.

"Stocks are probably a little bit overvalued and due for a correction. But we don't see Armageddon coming," says Don Riley, chief investment officer at the Wiley Group in Conshohocken, Pa., which manages $200 million in assets.

Instead of heading into money-market funds, he's advising clients to remain diversified through such exchange-traded funds as the WisdomTree LargeCap Dividend (DLN) and the Vanguard REIT ETF (VNQ).

"This is a very different looking market than we've seen in the past--there's not nearly the same level of speculation taking place," Mr. Riley says, recalling his days as a trader on the floor of the Philadelphia stock exchange during crashes in 1987 and through the tech-wreck years.

While he doesn't suggest that investors try to time market peaks and bottoms, adviser Josh Brown of Fusion Analytics believes they should realize that the market's peak in 1999 capped "one of the greatest market manias in history."

The current run-up, which traces its roots back to early March 2009, appears to Mr. Brown as rather "run of the mill" from a historical perspective.

But some bearish analysts disagree, arguing that investors are paying a lot more for stocks than they probably realize.

One popular metric used in gauging whether a stock is pricey, for instance, is a price-to-earnings ratio advanced by Yale professor Robert Shiller that takes into account a decade's worth of past inflation-adjusted earnings. The idea is to smooth market bumps over the longer term to give investors a better view of current pricing levels.

Advisers like Mr. Brown, whose firm manages more than $350 million in assets, take exception to such alternative valuations. Besides taking into account more garden-variety recessions in its earnings calculations, the Shiller PE measure also includes the market's fall from its 2007 peak through early 2009.

The result is a raising of valuations to "extreme" PE multiples that are "obscenely flawed," suggesting that the market is peaking, Mr. Brown says.

Another selling point supporting the current rally's strength has been its relatively broad base of market participants.

For example, in 1999 less than one-fifth of the S&P 500's sectors were within 5% of their 52-week highs. At the market's top in 2007, about half were at or near peak levels.

Today, around 89% are at those high-water marks, according to S&P Capital IQ data.

"If you're looking for a decline in market participation, you're not going to find it today," says Sam Stovall, chief equity strategist at the New York-based research firm.

Market momentum is much more reminiscent of 1995 than 1999 or 2007, says Hank Smith, chief investment officer at Haverford Trust Co., which manages more than $7 billion in assets. In that year, stocks rose more than 30% after a sluggish 1994, he points out.

"People still doubt this rally; they think it's built on a sugar high fed by the Federal Reserve's easy-money policies," Mr. Smith says. But he's finding that there are key fundamental factors behind the latest bull run that argue against any huge downturn in stocks.

Earnings on S&P 500 companies are running more than double the rate of 1999, Mr. Smith says. So are dividend yields on blue-chip stocks.

Investors aren't paying up as much this time around, either. Mr. Smith observes that investors are facing a traditional PE multiple of 14.5, which is less than half as much as 14 years ago.

Haverford Trust still likes prospects for stock ETFs, such as the iShares Dow Jones International Select Dividend Income Fund (IDV) and the Vanguard FTSE Emerging Markets ETF (VWO), Mr. Smith notes.

"Now that we're more than four years removed from the global financial crisis, it's finally starting to sink in with more people that this market is unique and has to be evaluated on its own merits," Mr. Smith says.

Starwood Capital Goes Shopping for Malls

By Kris Hudson and Craig Karmin 
665 words
22 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Starwood Capital Group is in talks to buy seven U.S. shopping malls from Westfield Group for more than $1 billion, a deal that would mark the latest in a flurry of big-ticket acquisitions of retail property.

Starwood's talks with Westfield aren't yet in the final stages and still could collapse, according to people familiar with the matter.

The discussions come as retail property continues to rebound from the economic downturn while fending off competition from online shopping. In addition, buyers are emboldened by low interest rates and the increasing availability of financing, notably from the resurgent market for securitized mortgages.

In another recent deal, private-equity firm Blackstone Group LP this month agreed to sell its majority stake in 30 U.S. shopping centers to DDR Corp. for $1.46 billion. Meantime, Blackstone also is preparing for an initial public offering or sale of its strip-center owner, Brixmor Property Group, by the end of this year.

Retail was one of the last property sectors to begin recovering from the downturn. Occupancy rates and rents showed little increase as consumers held back, retailers contracted and Internet retail expanded.

But the outlook has improved lately. The average vacancy rate for retail properties in the top 63 U.S. markets was 6.8% in the first quarter, down from a recent high of 7.5% in 2010, according to CoStar Group.

Construction of new retail space remains relatively low, allowing landlords to fill vacancies with restaurants, health clubs and expanding retailers such as Ulta Salon, Cosmetics & Fragrance Inc.

"Sales productivity has come back with a vengeance," said Cedrik Lachance, an analyst with Green Street Advisors Inc. "The occupancy rate at better properties is either back to or in excess of the previous peak in 2007. So you have an industry that is far healthier than is commonly believed."

Commercial-real-estate sales in all categories have been gaining thanks to the strengthening economy, rising values and low interest rates. Last year, deal volume hit $294 billion, compared with $229 billion in 2011 and $66 billion in 2009, according to Real Capital Analytics. Retail deals in 2012 totaled $55 billion, up 25% from 2011.

Buyers of retail property have been taking advantage of debt financing available from the sale of commercial mortgage-backed securities, a market that has been gathering steam. Of the $34.1 billion of commercial mortgages securitized so far this year, 32% have been backed by retail properties, more than any other commercial property type, according to Trepp LLC.

Westfield, based in Sydney, owns stakes in 100 malls world-wide. Since 2010, the company has been focusing on its highest-performing malls, selling its mediocre properties. Last year, Starwood acquired a 90% stake in seven U.S. malls from Westfield for $1.05 billion.

Blackstone's public sale of Brixmor is expected to be one of the largest real-estate IPOs since the financial crisis.

The New York-based private-equity firm values the business around $13 billion, say people familiar with the matter. Brixmor owns 90 million square feet of space at approximately 525 properties, making it the largest wholly owned shopping-center portfolio in the U.S.

Blackstone has spent hundreds of millions of dollars upgrading the shopping centers, much of that to attract anchor tenants, such as Wal-Mart, Safeway and TJ Maxx.

While Blackstone has added about 50 centers to the portfolio and shed about 75, the bulk of Brixmor's assets were acquired in 2011 in a $9.4 billion purchase of Australian landlord Centro Properties Group's U.S. operations.

In that deal, Blackstone assumed $8 billion of debt and eventually refinanced it. Brixmor has reduced some of that debt, but it isn't clear how much debt the company currently has.

Pimco shuns 'grim' high yields

By Nick Reeve 
536 words
20 May 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fund focus

Valuations have climbed too much, writes Nick Reeve

Investors should avoid US shares offering high levels of dividend income because valuations have reached risky levels, says Pimco's equity income team.

Raji Manasseh, senior vice-president for dividend strategies at the California-based investment giant, says the team fears the prospects for many high-yielding sectors are grim.

"Financial repression has driven a thirst for yield," Mr Manasseh says. "Clients have funnelled into a narrow range of dividend stocks and raised values to historic highs."

The team has responded by selling down US high-income shares on its Pimco EqS Dividend global equity strategy, focusing on the telecoms, utilities and real estate investment trusts (Reits) sectors. The strategy, which is available through US and Dublin-based funds, was launched in December 2011 amid a push by Mohamed El-Erian, Pimco's chief executive, to reduce the group's overwhelming focus on fixed income.

"Since 1995, US telecoms have traded in line with the wider market, but now they are at a 40 per cent premium. You have to ask if these companies are worth 19-20 times next year's earnings," says Mr Manasseh.

The team is also concerned about valuations in US utilities . Compared with its historic trading level of roughly 20 per cent cheaper than the wider market, the sector is now also priced at a significant premium, the senior vice-president adds.

The team is further wary about healthcare, in which Pfizer and Johnson & Johnson have booked significant share price gains over the past six months, buoyed by investors' thirst for healthy levels of income.

Pfizer's shares have gained 19.4 per cent while Johnson & Johnson has added 21.7 per cent, with both companies contributing to the 17.4 per cent rise seen on the wider S&P 500 index during that period.

Pimco has deepened its focus on areas such as technology, which is traditionally a growth-orientated sector. News flow in the sector was hijacked by technology company Apple's announcement in April that it was hiking its annual payout by 15 per cent to $3.05 a share. The company's shares now yield roughly 2.7 per cent.

But Mr Manasseh says the team is instead focusing on Apple's main rival Microsoft, on the basis that it offers a more stable dividend model.

He criticises Apple's decision to partially fund its dividend increase with a $17bn (10.9bn) bond issue earlier this month, adding that a number of other US companies had declared dividends in recent months "but not on a scale we like to see".

Pimco's income managers are also considering areas such as infrastructure, with toll road operators in China, Latin America and Europe offering a 5-6 per cent yield at "more attractive valuations", Mr Manasseh says. Elsewhere, the managers are topping up holdings in "selected companies" in the UK, including Imperial Tobacco.

The Dublin-based Pimco EqS Dividend fund has gained 27.2 per cent since launch in December 2011, according to FE Analytics.

Nick Reeve is a senior reporter at Investment Adviser

Managing: Wanted: More Directors Who Are Digital Savvy

By Joann S. Lublin 
739 words
20 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
8
English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns

Nearly every facet of corporate life has gone digital. But many public-company boards remain stuck in analog mode.

That has started to change. Boards worried about their scant digital expertise are scrambling to recruit newcomers who can advise management on strategies for mobile devices, social media and data analytics.

"The avalanche of digital activity is making directors conclude they are increasingly ill-equipped in the boardroom," observes Tuck Rickards, a managing director of recruiters Russell Reynolds Associates Inc.

Digital directors, who are sometimes decades younger than many of their colleagues, weigh in on marketing strategy, business alliances and even recruitment, several company leaders say.

The shift has made digital experts like Jeffrey F. Rayport hot commodities in U.S. boardrooms. Dr. Rayport, an operating partner of private-equity firm Castanea Partners, taught the first digital-strategy course at Harvard Business School in the 1990s and later ran the digital-strategy arm of consultants Monitor Group. He says he now fields more than a dozen feelers a year.

"It's a little overwhelming," says Dr. Rayport, who already holds directorships at Monster Worldwide Inc. and ValueClick Inc.

"I have never seen such an acceleration of demand for specific board expertise," says Clarke Murphy, the chief executive of Russell Reynolds. The search firm handled 10 U.S. hunts for digitally savvy directors last year. Its first such assignment resulted in Facebook Inc. executive Sheryl Sandberg joining Walt Disney Co.'s board in 2010.

Because boards at public companies want members with C-suite experience, the pool of digital candidates is small and usually doesn't include entrepreneurial startup veterans, recruiters say.

As a result, finding a digital director can take six to 12 months, which is twice as long as a typical board search, says Norbert Gottenberg, a solo high-tech recruiter. "Both sides are picky," he adds. Boards vet digital prospects more carefully than they do traditional ones, while qualified candidates "want to be on the right board."

Dr. Rayport, for example, is weighing three other directorship offers at publicly held businesses but says he wants to make sure that he's "not playing the role of window dressing."

Thirty-seven of the 100 biggest U.S. companies lack directors with digital expertise, and only 16 have at least three such members comprising 20% of the board, a Russell Reynolds analysis concludes. "Highly digital" boards include Cisco Systems Inc., Coca-Cola Co. and Allstate Corp.

Mark Vadon received his first public-company board invitation in 2006 but waited years before accepting one. The co-founder, chairman and former CEO of Blue Nile Inc., an online retailer of jewelry that went public in 2004, also co-launched and chairs Zulily Inc. The flash-sale site derives almost 40% of its revenue from smartphones and tablets.

For years, Mr. Vadon spurned feelers because they only came from Internet-related businesses. "It wasn't very different from what I was already doing," he says.

Then Home Depot Inc., a home-improvement chain eager to expand online sales, approached Mr. Vadon in 2012 as part of a push to bolster its board's digital expertise. CEO Frank Blake even flew to Seattle to woo him.

Appointed last September, the 43-year-old entrepreneur is younger and wears his hair longer than the others who sit on Home Depot's board. "Whatever his appearance, he was an incredible addition," says Bonnie Hill, the independent lead director.

Among other things, Mr. Vadon says he has urged executives to tailor ad pitches for mobile devices and enlarge the ranks of mobile developers by training current staffers. A Home Depot spokeswoman agrees that "mobile is a priority" but declined to discuss specific strategies.

At Starbucks Corp., digital director Clara Shih played a pivotal role in a mobile-payments deal forged with startup Square Inc. last August. Ms. Shih, named to the coffee chain's board at age 29 in December 2011, runs Hearsay Social, which helps companies use social media.

Though startup leaders are often less wedded to hierarchy and formal structure, Ms. Shih says she felt the Starbucks directorship offered an "extraordinary opportunity" to learn how big businesses operate.

Square will eventually process credit and debit transactions at about 7,000 U.S. Starbucks stores through Square's smartphone app.

Technology
Pace Picks Up on Tech IPOs --- Large Group of Maturing Silicon Valley Startups Will Test Post-Facebook Waters

By Jessica E. Lessin and Pui-Wing Tam in San Francisco and Telis Demos in New York 
862 words
17 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

A year after Facebook Inc.'s botched initial public offering, Silicon Valley's IPO pipeline is starting to fill up again.

Following a slowdown in technology IPOs after Facebook's offering last May, companies including microblogging service Twitter Inc., peer-to-peer lending site Lending Club Corp. and software-tools startup Atlassian are among those preparing to go public, said executives and people familiar with the matter.

While some of the companies are in the early stages of preparing for an IPO, others are likely to file within the next year to 18 months, they said.

Any consumer technology companies that test the IPO market would be closely watched as bellwethers of demand for such stocks.

The busiest sector continues to be companies selling software to businesses. Marketing-software maker Marketo Inc., and analytics-software company Tableau Software Inc. are expected to begin trading Friday. Network security firm FireEye Inc. and corporate mobile-device management company Good Technology Corp. have hired banks and could list later this year, said people familiar with their plans. Another considering the leap is utility-software company Opower Inc.

A cluster of advertising-technology firms, including Criteo and YuMe Inc., are also eyeing 2013 listings, people familiar with the situation said.

Among consumer-focused companies, sports camera-maker GoPro Inc. is eyeing possibly going public later this year, according to people familiar with its thinking.

The first quarter was relatively quiet for tech IPOs. Overall, six tech or Internet-related companies went public in the first quarter, down from 15 in the same period a year ago, according to Dealogic. Some companies have slowed their IPO march because of the availability of private financing, which allows startups to delay offerings. Others have put off IPOs until they are more confident that their public valuations would well exceed where they had raised money privately.

Others, though, are beginning to stir. There was a burst of offerings in March and April including Marin Software Inc. and Rally Software Development Corp. and it appears the time is ripe for more.

"The number of private companies across Silicon Valley that is mature is at an all-time high," said Venky Ganesan, a venture capitalist at Menlo Ventures. "There's a huge pipeline and backlog of IPOs."

Among the factors spurring the pickup in activity are the soaring stock market, said investors and bankers.

Many tech companies have grown rapidly over the past few years to a public-company scale and are considering taking advantage of the IPO window, they said.

At the same time, rules that went into effect last year with the JOBS Act allow companies to file for an IPO and keep their finances confidential until they are closer to a roadshow. That has prodded some startups that were once leery of public scrutiny to accelerate filing plans, said Jeff Vetter, a partner specializing in IPOs and securities law at law firm Fenwick & West, which helped take Facebook public last year.

The tech IPO business is "much busier," said Mr. Vetter. He estimates Fenwick will work on about 12 tech IPOs this year (including three the firm has already taken public), up from six last year.

Facebook's poor IPO has also helped other potential IPO candidates because investor expectations have deflated, said Renaud Laplanche, chief executive of Lending Club. Mr. Laplanche, who forecasts his San Francisco company will be ready to file for an IPO "by sometime next year," said Facebook's IPO "brought a little more sanity to the market." That encouraged him because "the last thing we want is a stock price that shoots up 200% on the first day and then goes down from there," he said.

One of the most anticipated public offerings is Twitter, which has been valued at more than $10 billion by investors. Company executives have been preparing internally for a public offering, which some investors said privately they expect within a year. A company spokesman did not return requests for comment.

At file-storage service Box Inc., CEO Aaron Levie said going public would give the company a new currency for acquisitions and help it better serve enterprises. While he said an IPO is "unlikely this year," he cited 2014 as a possibility.

"Being a private company gives us a little more flexibility but [being public] is certainly in our sights," Mr. Levie said. He declined to comment on the San Francisco company's financials, except to say revenue rose 150% last year.

Other high-profile tech companies are on a slower IPO track. Box rival Dropbox Inc. is one of those, according to two people familiar with the company's thinking who said the startup has no current plans to go public. Dropbox raised $250 million in 2011 at around a $4 billion valuation and is profitable. Dropbox didn't respond to request for comment.

BNP launches full-service mobile bank

By Scheherazade Daneshkhu in Paris 
518 words
16 May 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

BNP Paribas is to tap rising demand for banking services by mobile phone users with the launch of a full-service dedicated mobile bank across four European countries.

Hello Bank starts with zero customers but aims to have 1.4m by 2017 in Germany, Belgium, France and Italy. The bank launched on Thursday in the first two countries, with France to follow next month and Italy in October.

Banks in France are looking for new customers in the face of stagnant or declining revenue growth in the domestic retail business because of the contraction in the economy and falling demand for loans.

Chris Harvey, global head of financial services at Deloitte, the consultancy, said: "Banks are increasingly looking for new growth avenues. The digital and mobile sector is one promising area that also has the potential to reduce radically the cost base."

Of the 1.4m customers BNP is targeting, up to one-third are likely to be existing BNP clients deciding to transfer their branch based accounts but Franois Villeroy de Galhau, BNP's chief operating officer, said the aim was to attract new customers and to respond to the "explosion" of smartphone usage since 2010.

"In France no one used their mobile for banking services in 2010 - now there are 700,000 people who do and that is growing at 50 per cent a year," he said.

Hello bank is "the first full-range bank specifically designed for mobiles with a rapid and permanent connection", said Mr Villeroy, offering products available in branches. Most other mobile banking services were either payments-based or transfers of technology from the internet to mobiles and tablets, he said.

Some  80m will be invested this year - mainly the cost of acquiring new clients - and Hello bank is expected to break even within four years.

Its accounts and payments will be free - in France, there is usually a charge for debit cards - and its savings rates would be "attractive" said BNP. It will have a back-up staff of 900 for mobile customers, who will have to use the branch network to deposit cash and cheques.

BNP said that Hello bank would entail lower costs than a branch-based network because it needed less paper and fewer employees. But in turn, revenue per customer would be lower.

Banks are looking to cut costs to boost profitability. BNP, which is at the start of a  2bn three-year cost-cutting programme, said this week it expected a "significant" drop in staffing levels in France. Baudouin Prot, chairman, told shareholders at the group's annual meeting on Wednesday that: "Our employee levels will go down in France this year."

France has the second highest penetration rate of online bank usage in Europe - 60 per cent of internet users visit an online bank - after the Netherlands, according to ComScore, the data analytics group.

ING Group's banking website has the highest number of online visitors, according to ComScore, followed by Crdit Agricole and Lloyds Banking Group. BNP is ranked eighth, according to ComScore.

MARKETS & INVESTING
Investment banks' drive into forex proves less lucrative

By Alice Ross 
407 words
15 May 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
USA Ed1
18
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Investment banks saw dwindling returns in their foreign exchange divisions in the first quarter amid fierce competition on prices, even as the sharp fall in the Japanese yen led investors to pile into the currency market .

Revenues at the 10 largest banks by volume in the forex market fell 3 per cent in the first three months from the period in 2012, according to figures from Coalition, a financial services analytics company.

It does not measure profits, and few investment banks break out figures on forex from their wider fixed income divisions.

The drive by investment banks to expand forex divisions is proving less lucrative than in previous years .

Total revenues at the 10 banks - which include Deutsche Bank, Citigroup, Barclays, UBS, HSBC and JPMorgan - were $2bn.

That marked a fall from $2.2bn in the first three months of 2012 and was well below the bumper third quarter of 2008, when turmoil around the collapse of Lehman Brothers helped forex bankers at the top 10 to revenues of $4.8bn.

Investment banks trading the large currencies have found it harder to make money in recent years. Faster electronic trading has hurt margins and banks have become more competitive on pricing.

In response to dwindling interest in trading the euro in 2011 and 2012, some banks temporarily waived transaction costs to retain market share. "Spreads continue to tighten to levels we never thought possible - it's a hyper-competitive market," said Jeff Feig, head of G10 forex at Citigroup in New York.

Yet banks remain focused on their forex divisions, which require less capital to manage in an era when regulators are calling for banks to put more money aside as a buffer against future economic slumps.

"It's a no-brainer product for the banks," said George Kuznetsov, head of research and analytics at Coalition. "We've seen significant attention from investment banks in foreign exchange in the last two to three years due to the low capital requirements."

Coalition's figures show a significant decline in revenues from forward products - derivatives that allow investors and companies to protect themselves against moves in interest rates - offsetting rises in spot and options products driven by hedge funds selling the yen.

Steadier interest rates as a result of central bank easing this year have led to fewer forward contracts being taken out, banks reported.
