Investing Basics
Personal Finance
Lifelines for Investors on Their Own

By Carolyn T. Geer 
733 words
12 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A reader in Minneapolis needs "direction" with her three pots of money a regular individual retirement account, a Roth IRA and a taxable account, totaling $725,000 but speaks for many investors when she writes:

"I have a hard time believing any adviser they all want to sweep up my assets and collect 1% of the total annually."

Meanwhile, she joins the growing ranks of so-called mass affluent investors (variously defined as investors with $100,000 to $1 million or $2 million in investible assets) who end up "self-directing" their portfolios fending for themselves, many by default.

Assets at the likes of Fidelity Investments, Charles Schwab, Vanguard Group and other so-called direct providers of investments grew 9% to $3.7 trillion in 2011, from $3.4 trillion in 2010, outstripping the 3% growth in retail investment assets overall, according to new research from analytics firm Cerulli Associates.

Sensing an opportunity, direct providers have been shrewdly expanding their own investment advisory services. At the same time, a new breed of online financial advisory firm is out to do what human advisers do, only with software and without the mazes of fees and conflicts of interest that can plague traditional brokerage firms.

Here's the lowdown:

Discount brokers and no-load fund companies generally offer different levels of service.

The most basic comes in the form of all-in-one funds. These are off-the-shelf funds with a diversified mix of investments. Some, such as Fidelity's Asset Manager funds and Vanguard's LifeStrategy Funds, maintain a fixed asset allocation over time. Others, such as target-date retirement funds and 529 college-savings-plan funds, are managed with a specific date in mind and grow more conservative as that date approaches. In either case, all you pay are the management expenses of the fund.

Schwab and Fidelity offer a range of so-called managed accounts, portfolios of funds and individual securities assembled by professional money managers. At Schwab, the minimum account size is $25,000 for a diversified portfolio of mutual funds or exchange-traded funds. Annual account fees range from 0.2% to 0.9% of assets, on top of the underlying fund fees.

You also can buy a financial plan that comes with a list of recommended investments. At Vanguard, the cost is $1,000 for those with accounts under $50,000, and $250 for those with between $50,000 and $500,000 in assets at the firm. This includes the plan and a one-time phone consultation with an adviser.

For your own personal financial planner who'll provide continuing investment recommendations, Vanguard charges an annual "service" fee of 0.7% on the first $1 million in assets, 0.35% on the next $1 million, and 0.2% on subsequent amounts on top of its fund expenses, which average 0.19%. There's a $500,000 account minimum, and a minimum annual service fee of $4,500.

Software-based advisers invest your money in portfolios of low-cost index funds, using algorithms to determine the mix of investments that's right for you. They also continuously rebalance your account when stock-bond allocations stray from targets.

"https://www.betterment.com/" charges an annual fee equal to 0.35% of assets on accounts up to $10,000, 0.25% on amounts above that, up to $100,000, and 0.15% on amounts above $100,000. It allocates your money between two diversified baskets of ETFs one containing stocks, the other Treasury bonds.

"https://www.wealthfront.com/" charges nothing on investment amounts up to $10,000, and 0.25% on higher amounts. It uses a wider array of asset classes, including municipal and corporate bonds and real estate. And it continually trolls for chances to realize losses to offset gains in an effort to improve after-tax returns.

At both services, fees embedded in the cost of the underlying ETFs average an additional 0.14% or so per year.

Adam Nash, Wealthfront's chief operating officer, says rebalancing alone can add 0.4% a year to returns and better tax planning, another 1%.

Wealthfront's clients value transparency more than a human touch, he adds. For them, trust is earned not with a handshake but with clear, timely disclosure of costs and portfolio holdings.

Concern at Bloomberg access to users' data; MEDIA

JPMorgan points to reporters' practices

Bloomberg has come under fire from two of its largest clients after JPMorgan and Goldman Sachs expressed concern that Bloomberg News journalists had used private details of how bankers use its powerful financial data terminals to steer their reporting.

People close to JPMorgan said they had raised informal concerns with Bloom-berg News that its reporters had used data about when its employees logged in and out of the terminal to inform their coverage of last year's "London whale" trading loss.

A spokesman for Bloomberg said he was unaware of any record of any such complaint.

News of JPMorgan's concern came after the New York Post reported that Goldman had formally complained after Bloomberg reporters approached executives to ask about partners who had not logged on for some time. A Goldman spokesman confirmed that it had raised the matter with Bloomberg.

Bloomberg said it had disabled journalists' access to customer relationship information "in light of [Goldman's] concern as well as a general heightened sensitivity to data access".

"Limited customer relationship data has long been available to our journalists, and has never included clients' security-level data, position data, trading data or messages," a spokesman said.

Bloomberg News has long seen a competitive advantage in encouraging its reporters to use the wealth of data on its terminals to generate news stories others miss, but few clients knew that reporters could also see when they were logging in and out of the system and which functions they were accessing.

Bloomberg reporters have been able to see broad information on which functions users access, such as equities or commodities, and aggregated data on how many people read each news story. They have not been able to see marketsensitive details of individual equities or other assets users track or which news stories individuals have read, however, or see details of messages sent over Bloomberg's heavily-used messaging service.

Regulators are paying growing attention to the way in which search engines, social networks and other businesses use information on their customers, as companies balance the value to be gleaned from analysing "big data" with the need to protect users' privacy.

Thomson Reuters, Bloomberg's closest competitor in financial data and newswires, said its terminal business and newsroom "operate completely independently with reporters having no access to non-public data on its customers, especially any data relating to its customers' use of its products or services".

Thomson Reuters collects and analyses aggregated and anonymous customer data, a spokeswoman said, but "there are strict controls in place that prevent any Reuters or other staff outside of the development groups from accessing this data in any form".

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
Bloomberg acts after outcry over users' data; MEDIA

Goldman issues formal complaint

Bloomberg has come under fire from two of its largest clients, with JPMorgan and Goldman Sachs expressing concern that its journalists had used private details of how bankers use its powerful financial data terminals to steer their reporting.

Dan Doctoroff, chief executive, said yesterday that it had long made limited customer data available to journalists, but "we realise this was a mistake".

In response, Bloomberg has appointed an executive to review data compliance policies and disabled reporters' access to information about when customers log in, which functions they access and what contact they have with its help desk.

It acted after a formal complaint from Goldman, whose executives were unnerved by calls from journalists that mentioned when Goldman employees logged in and which Bloomberg functions they used, said one person familiar with the complaint.

Bloomberg said it had limited journalists' access "in light of [Goldman's] concern as well as a general heightened sensitivity to data access". In a memo to clients and staff, Mr Doctoroff added: "Client trust is our highest priority and the cornerstone of our business."

People at JPMorgan said they believed Bloomberg reporters had used login information to try to determine whether Bruno Iksil, the London-based derivatives trader known as the "London whale", had left the bank. "Multiple reporters did it for other traders as well in our equities and fixed income groups in the investment bank," said one person at JPMorgan.

A Bloomberg spokesman said he was unaware of any record of any such complaint from JPMorgan.

Regulators are paying growing attention to the way in which search engines, social networks and other businesses use customer information, as companies balance the value to be gleaned from analysing "big data" with the need to protect users' privacy.

Bloomberg, whose clients range from hedge funds to regulators and central bankers, has long seen a competitive advantage in using the wealth of data on its terminals to generate stories, but few clients knew that reporters could also see details of how they used the system.

Bloomberg reporters have been able to see broad information on which of the roughly 15,000 functions users access, such as charts or asset classes, and aggregated data on how many people read each story. Reporters and salesmen also pooled internal notes that included personal information about customers such as their favourite foods or number of -children.

Reporters have not been able to see market-sensitive details of "securities-level data, position data [or] trading data", Bloomberg said, or communications over its heavily-used messaging service.

About 315,000 subscribers pay it about $20,000 a year for its service.

Wiki's crystal ball; Hedge funds must raise their game to beat web analytics

First it was the factory worker who was made obsolete by technology. Now it is the hedge fund manager who looks increasingly redundant - and not just any hedgie but Wall Street's finest.

The Financial Times this week revealed that tips given by the best and brightest at last year's Ira Sohn conference - where the industry's stars offer their investment advice to raise money for charity - failed to outperform a passive US tracker fund . But even trackers struggle to compete with Wikipedia or Google. Academics have found a direct correlation between market movements and the number of visitors to articles on companies or financial topics.

Wikipedia, the online encyclopedia, is best for predicting when the market is about to tumble, according to a paper published this week by academics from Warwick Business School, University College London and Boston University. The more page views on financial subjects, the greater the chance the Dow Jones index will fall. Last month the same team found that a trading strategy based on Google searches for the words debt, profit and loss could deliver well above average returns.

Hedge funds should beware. In 2011, according to Hedge Fund Research, the average return was negative 5.2 per cent. That compares with a gain of 15 per cent using the Wikipedia formula.

Once lauded as Masters of the Universe for their ability to move markets and demand multimillion dollar fees in the process, many of these high-volume traders are in danger of losing their unique selling point. The industry's domination of daily volumes on the US and UK markets has made it more difficult to devise strategies that differentiate one fund manager from another. The results are showing in ever more pedestrian returns. And, if that were not incentive enough to go elsewhere, Wikipedia offers its services for free. Higher productivity and lower costs - now that must really worry the hedgies.

COMPANIES
Banks tipped off to Harp loans; general financial

By Tracy Alloway in New York 
436 words
9 May 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
USA Ed1
16
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac have inadvertently tipped off US banks and other big mortgage investors to more loan data than the two housing groups intended, resulting in some of the banks using the information released to identify and analyse Harp loans.

The two US mortgage finance groups have been told by their regulator, the Federal Housing Finance Agency (FHFA), to release data on the home loans they guarantee in an effort to encourage private investors to assume more of the risk in the mortgage market.

The loan data cover credit performance for more than 30m single-family mortgages, but exclude information on non-standard loans such as those financed under the government's home affordable refinance programme (Harp).

The swift interpretation of the data by some of the banks highlights the financial industry's increasingly sophisticated ability to digest large volumes of so-called " big data ".

"You could figure out which loans were Harp," Kevin Atteson, of Morgan Stanley's mortgage trading team, said at the " big data finance " conference in New York.

Pinpointing Harp loans is valuable for mortgage investors and traders because it helps identify loans that may face more obstacles to refinancing. US homeowners who qualify for the programme are only allowed to refinance using Harp once.

Morgan Stanley is believed to have used a sophisticated "matching engine" to identify Harp refinanced loans through the released credit performance data.

"The agencies have tried to eliminate the easy matching attributes," said Steven Abrahams, mortgage analyst at Deutsche Bank, "but you could work around that with fair accuracy. If the released loans exclude Harp, then whatever isn't matched, with some qualifications, should be Harp."

As in other industries, "big data" has become a buzzword among financial groups who are seeking to mine reams of new information to gain an edge in the market.

Freddie has historically shared delinquency data for pools of mortgages but not individual loans, while Fannie did not release any type of credit data.

"We are aware of recent research pieces where analysis has been performed on Harp loans as a result of the single-family loan-level data set release," Freddie said. "Freddie Mac can provide no assurances regarding the accuracy of such mapping."

The new data released by the mortgage groups come in files so big that Fannie and Freddie recommend would-be downloaders use specialised "database management systems" to access it. The agencies say that releasing the data will lead to better modelling of the risks involved in investing in such loans.

Special
The Experts: Should You Keep Cash in Your Portfolio?

2,236 words
9 May 2013
17:20
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Besides keeping some in an emergency fund, should the average investor have an allocation to cash and cash equivalents in an investment portfolio? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887324030704578427123993873696.html" that discussed the merits of keeping cash in a stock fund's portfolio and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Wednesday, May 8.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Wealth Management Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts online at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/16jTSr8" three wealth-management thought leaders Morningstar's Director of Personal Finance Christine Benz ("https://twitter.com/christine_benz"), American Association of Individual Investors Vice President Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") and Portfolio Solutions Founder Rick Ferri ("https://twitter.com/Rick_Ferri") speak about succeeding as a do-it-yourself investor in a video chat that aired on Monday, May 6.

Michelle Perry Higgins: In a Word, Absolutely.

In my opinion, absolutely. My rule of thumb for investors is that if you think you may need the funds within the next three years, then those dollars should probably be left in cash or cash equivalents. For example, if you plan to tap into your portfolio within the next few years for a home purchase, college or income needs, my advice would be to keep the funds in cash and call it a day. You can't afford to risk losing money in the stock or bond market with dollars you might actually need to have in hand within a few years. The stock market is notoriously good at tempting you to move out of cash and up the risk curve.

Michelle Perry Higgins ("https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Matt Hougan: In a Word, No.

No. Put your money to work.

Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

Tom Brakke: Cash Lets You Respond to Opportunities

For investors who take a fully passive approach, it's probably not necessary. I think everyone else should have higher levels of cash today than they generally do.

That may seem counterintuitive given the low rates on cash, but cash provides flexibility during times when markets come under pressure. Being fully invested sounds great, but it prevents you from responding to opportunities.

Central banks around the world have driven the returns on cash to zero, and have caused distortions in the prices of more volatile assets. No one knows whether those policies will lead to nirvana or catastrophe, although we will undoubtedly end up somewhere in between those extremes. Sacrificing a little upside by holding extra cash right now is a good trade for having a cushion if this grand experiment goes awry.

(If you want to read a longer "http://blogs.cfainstitute.org/insideinvesting/2012/11/05/cash-as-trash-cash-as-king-and-cash-as-a-weapon/" on this topic, check out "Cash as Trash, Cash as King, and Cash as a Weapon," from the CFA Institute.)

Tom Brakke ("https://twitter.com/researchpuzzler") is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

Rick Ferri: Cash Doesn't Earn a Thing

Cash isn't a good investment because after taxes and inflation, it doesn't earn anything. Today, it's actually a bad investment because inflation is higher than the return on cash.

Cash outside an emergency fund is useful if you have a large liability coming up. For example, your high-school senior is heading off to college and you're footing the bill. Personally, I'm stashing cash this year to pay higher taxes next April.

The only other reason to hold cash is if you're trying to time the markets. Good luck with that. I don't do it, which is one reason I have enough money to pay my taxes.

Rick Ferri is founder of "http://www.portfoliosolutions.com/" LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Rafael Pardo: Liquidity Will Keep You From Robbing Peter to Pay Paul

Yes. As its name suggests, your emergency fund is intended (and should only be used) for emergencies, such as covering living expenses after the loss of a job. Once you deviate from this principle and begin raiding your emergency fund for nonemergency purposes (robbing Peter to pay Paul, as it were), you could find yourself in a heap of trouble. For example, an unforeseen investment opportunity is not an emergency, and yet that opportunity may be too good to pass up. To avoid the dilemma of foregoing the opportunity (and thus missing out on a potential gain) or seizing it (and thus potentially and irreversibly depleting your emergency fund), an allocation to cash in your investment portfolio will give you the liquidity to avail yourself of such an opportunity. And in doing so, you won't compromise the cushion you have built with your emergency fund to safeguard against unforeseen contingencies.

Rafael Pardo is the Robert T. Thompson professor of law at Emory University, where he specializes in bankruptcy and commercial law.

George Papadopoulos: You Need Some Cash for When Opportunity Knocks

I always like to have some cash lying around to take advantage of opportunities that may arise suddenly. Market corrections will always occur; you just never know when. With dividends and capital-gain distributions taken in cash and new cash added to portfolios, we find there is usually at least 5% sitting in cash. This cash came in handy in late 2008, of course. It is always easier to rebalance portfolios when you have available cash as it reduces or eliminates your need to sell a position, which increases transactions costs. The last thing we will do is make market timing calls and "go to all cash."

George Papadopoulos ("https://twitter.com/feeonlyplanner") is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families.

Sheryl Garrett: Don't Think of Cash as an Investment

Put cash in your saving account and investments in your portfolio. The only cash that you may need in your investment portfolio is money to fund distributions, to enable you to take advantage of opportunities, or maybe you've simply met your objective or it's matured and you're awaiting reinvestment. Otherwise, cash is not an investment, and should not be included in your investment portfolio.

Sheryl Garrett ("https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") is founder of the Garrett Planning Network Inc.

Greg McBride: Cash Becomes More Important With Age

An allocation to cash makes sense for many investors, regardless of age, as a way to diversify your portfolio, but is increasingly important as investors get closer to retirement. Particularly now, investors may opt for a little more cash at the expense of fixed income, given the nosebleed valuations in government bonds. Even for ultra-aggressive investors, a modest cash allocation gives you some investing ammunition when short-term market volatility reveals attractive bargains.

Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Manisha Thakor: Keep Cash If You'll Use It Within the Next Seven Years

Whether or not you should be holding cash depends upon how you define your "investment portfolio." My personal rule of thumb is that money you know with certainty that you'll need to spend in the next seven years should not be invested in stocks or bonds. Rather it should be parked for safe keeping in cash equivalents (money-market funds, CDs, T-bills, etc.). The reason is that this frees you up to take full advantage of the risk/reward characteristics inherent in equity investing. In plain English, you can swing a little harder because you know you have a cash safety net. So if your definition of your "investment portfolio" is the sum total of funds that you have at your disposal to save and invest, then yes, there absolutely is a place. I also think cash is a fine parking place while you are trying to decide what you want to do, as rushed decisions can easily backfire.

However, if by contrast you are referring to your IRA or your 401(k) as your investment portfolio and you are not within seven years of planning to take distributions, then I prefer to see portfolios fully invested. The way I would temper the inevitable volatility that comes with investing is to take my "risk" on the equity side and using high quality municipals or treasurys (not corporates) on the fixed income side as a buffer. The idea of keeping some "cash on the sidelines" to put to work when opportunities arise to me sounds an awful lot like market timing something that I view as strikingly close to gambling given it's long statistical history of failure.

Manisha Thakor ("https://twitter.com/ManishaThakor") is founder and chief executive of Santa Fe, N.M.-based MoneyZen Wealth Management LLC.

Larry Zimpleman: Leave Your Allocation at 5% or Less

Unless you are a very conservative investor, I would keep the allocation to cash and cash equivalents to a modest amount say roughly 5% or less. The primary reason for keeping a cash allocation would be to try to move into and out of the market (i.e. market timing). Studies show consistently that market timing is rarely, if ever, successful. The best approach is to set a long-term asset allocation strategy and stay true to that (unless either your time frame for drawing on the assets changes or your tolerance for risk changes).

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

Eleanor Blayney: Why and Where You Should Keep Cash

Yes and no. As we CFP professionals love to say, "It depends."

It depends on why and where you are holding this nonemergency cash. It also depends on what else you may be holding namely, debt.

Take the "why" first. Many of us have short-term financial goals, like saving for a significant vacation in three years, or putting a deposit on a home in the next two years. These kinds of goals assuming a time frame of five years or less are suitably held in cash or equivalents in an account separate from emergency money. Keep the two separate so that you can be religious about never tapping the emergency fund except for bona fide, "oh-crap" emergencies. If you mingle the short-term money with the emergency money, before you know it you'll be raiding the emergency fund for things like new furniture or that pesky annual insurance premium you forgot to budget into this month's cash flow.

Now for the "where." It makes little sense to hold cash or cash equivalents in your qualified retirement portfolio, assuming you are not close to retirement or in the withdrawal stage. The primary reason to hold cash is for its liquidity. When you know you're going to need X-dollars in the near future, you want to be invested in something certain to be nominally worth X dollars at that time. Cash in a qualified retirement account loses all the benefits of its liquidity when you cannot withdraw it without paying a penalty.

Finally, consider your liabilities. It rarely makes sense to hold cash at today's negligible interest rates, when you have debt at rates that are more than 2% or 3% of what the cash is earning. If the cash is not needed in the short term (see "why" above), then the smarter investment move is to pay down or off the higher rate debt.

Eleanor Blayney ("https://twitter.com/EleanorBlayney") is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards.

Charles Rotblut: Think About Your Time Frame

The answer depends on when you intend to spend the cash. If you have a big purchase (e.g. a house, a car, college tuition, etc.) within the next few years, then holding cash or a cash equivalent makes sense. It can also make sense for retirees to hold a few years of anticipated withdrawals in cash to fund withdrawals.

Money not needed for the long term should be allocated to a mixture of stocks and bonds. If a security or fund is sold and you are hoping to reinvest the proceeds at lower prices, set a deadline for doing so. For example, if you think stock prices will dip over the summer, set a deadline to invest the cash no later than Aug. 31. This gives you some flexibility, but will prevent you from staying in cash too long.

Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

Terrance Odean: How's Your Tolerance for Risk?

Besides the emergency fund, most investors should not hold a large portion of their investment portfolio in cash for liquidity purposes. Investors with higher risk aversion or shorter investment horizons may want to hold a portion of their portfolio in low-risk or risk-free assets (e.g., T-bills).

Terrance Odean is the Rudd Family Foundation professor and chair of the finance group at the Haas School of Business at the University of California, Berkeley.

In Depth: U.S. Households, Businesses Picking Up Borrowing Again --- Many Still Struggle To Get Loans as Banks Tightened Standards For Young People, Those With Bad Credit

By Neil Shah 
1,564 words
9 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
14
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Boise, Idaho -- When the economy nose-dived several years ago, Lou Aaron found it impossible to get a bank loan, forcing him to use six different credit cards to keep his 1950s-style drive-in restaurant running.

Now, the 50-year-old chef said banks are coming to him offering loans. His sales are climbing, and he expects to borrow $350,000 to open a third location next year where he will sell prime rib, hamburgers and his "Idaho Ice Cream Potato" dessert.

America's credit crunch is easing. For the past six years, consumers and businesses have struggled to borrow money, but slowly, things are getting easier.

Large U.S. companies are taking advantage of low interest rates to borrow record amounts of capital in bond markets. Banks are opening the spigots for commercial and industrial firms, and loans grew at an 11% annualized rate in the first quarter of this year, the sixth double-digit percentage increase in seven quarters, Federal Reserve data show. According to the Fed's survey of senior bank-lending officers released Monday, 28% of banks lowered the cost of credit lines early this year to smaller firms like Mr. Aaron's that have annual sales of less than $50 million. Residential lending began edging up last year, and even people with bad credit can get a loan to buy a car these days.

In all, some $713 billion in credit flowed to U.S. households and nonfinancial businesses last year, double 2011's $336 billion, according to the Fed. That is still a fraction of the $2.2 trillion in credit that lifted American consumers and businesses in 2007.

Indeed, many Americans still struggle to get loans. Banks have tightened standards, especially for young people and those with past financial problems.

The improved access to credit in recent months suggests America's economic outlook is gradually brightening, despite recent hurdles like federal budget cutbacks, increased payroll taxes and troubled overseas markets. Falling unemployment -- the U.S. jobless rate slid to 7.5% last month -- and rising stock and home prices should fuel spending, the biggest driver of the economy. More credit should speed this process.

"The underlying conditions that support growth have been gradually improving," William Dudley, Federal Reserve Bank of New York president, said in a speech last month. He said improved "household access to credit" was a factor.

The change is evident in Boise, a community that suffered a real-estate crash and recession similar to the nation's.

The metro area's 640,000 residents are recovering from their debt hangover.

Per capita debt among Boise consumers has dropped nearly 19% from its peak in 2008 to about $35,000 as of the first quarter of 2013, mostly due to foreclosures and bankruptcies, according to data from Moody'sAnalytics and Equifax Inc., a credit-monitoring service. Debt levels rose in the first quarter for the first time in five years, suggesting that Boise residents are starting to borrow again.

Over the past year, Boise's economy has picked up. In this year's first quarter, for example, the number of city permits issued for new residential and commercial construction projects -- efforts that usually require borrowed money -- jumped nearly 20% to a five-year high. Meanwhile, Boise's unemployment rate fell to 6.1% in March from 7.6% a year earlier.

"There's an abundant amount of credit available," says John V. Evans III, executive vice president of D.L. Evans Bank, a local lender. Mr. Evans says his bank is "aggressively trying to make loans." Its commercial loans are up 15% so far this year and 20% from 2008 levels, while mortgage loans are up 52% and 170%, respectively.

Al Youngwerth, president of Rekluse, a 10-year-old manufacturer on the outskirts of Boise, recently secured $700,000 in credit to buy equipment and invest in his factory.

His firm, which makes about 750 different parts for off-road motorcycles and employs 40 people, posted a 25% jump in sales last year and its credit history is clean, Mr. Youngwerth says. When the 48-year-old approached his bank, KeyBank, for loans to finance the purchase of a new robot and other equipment, the bank initially fretted about the company's high level of debt relative to its assets, he says.

Eventually, Mr. Youngwerth won over his bankers, and the robot -- which helps with loading -- arrived about a month ago. "The credit came quickly and easily," he said.

John Williamson, senior vice president at KeyBank, said "banks, contrary to popular belief, do want to lend money." However, he said, KeyBank and other lenders need "to be protective of our capital." Lending by KeyCorp, the parent of KeyBank, grew 6.5%, on average, in the first quarter of this year from a year earlier, the bank said.

Credit usually gets tighter during downturns, but the latest recession was significantly worse, thanks to billions of dollars of souring home loans that shook the nation's banks and gummed up its mortgage-finance system.

Many banks remain reluctant to make home loans to borrowers with tarnished credit histories. Lenders worry loans they unload to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, the government-owned mortgage-finance giants, could be returned to them. And Wall Street's mortgage-securitization machine is struggling to rev up amid stricter rules and unfinished regulations.

"The most frozen assets take the longest to unthaw," said Deutsche Bank economist Torsten Slok. "And at the end of the line of dominoes is mortgages."

Credit conditions have eased more quickly for bigger businesses. Micron Technology Inc., one of Boise's top employers, is borrowing more cheaply, despite its low credit rating and frequent dry spells in one of its businesses, making memory chips for personal computers.

Chip prices, along with Micron's profits, tend to swing depending on supply and demand. The firm has a double-B-minus credit rating from Standard & Poor's Ratings Services. A Micron spokesman didn't have a comment on the rating.

Investors are scrambling to buy the company's bonds, says Kipp Bedard, Micron's vice president of investor relations. In February, Micron borrowed $600 million from investors by issuing convertible bonds -- investments that turn into stock under certain circumstances. Micron is paying investors annual interest rates of 1.625% and 2.125% for the two varieties of the convertibles. A year earlier, the company borrowed using similar instruments and paid 2.375% and 3.125%.

Firms like Micron have the Fed to thank for unusually low borrowing rates. By holding its key short-term interest rate near zero and driving down long-term rates through its bond-buying programs, the Fed is pushing investors hungry for returns out of low-yielding bets like government bonds into riskier assets like corporate bonds and stocks.

Consumer credit, which has been slower to respond to the Fed's efforts, is showing signs of life too.

Even borrowers with patchy credit can now qualify for subprime car loans, which were 43% of auto loans made in the fourth quarter of 2012, according to Experian, a credit-reporting firm.

Home-equity loans, which allow homeowners to tap their homes for cash, are creeping back. It is the same story with jumbo mortgages, usually taken out by affluent people. The bottom line is that banks are dipping their toes back into riskier territory.

There also are signs traditional mortgage lending is picking up: Residential home loans from banks expanded at an annualized rate of 1.4% in the first quarter, after contracting in the final months of last year, Fed data show. The Fed's latest survey of bank lending officers showed nearly 10% of banks polled said they were easing standards on prime, or low-risk, mortgages, compared with less than 5% in a previous survey.

The recovery of the housing market, which appears to have turned a corner this year, could provide an additional boost. With rising home values making Americans wealthier and improving banks' own balance sheets, more lenders may become comfortable lending. "Rising house prices are likely to encourage a further loosening of credit," the New York Fed's Mr. Dudley recently said.

Meanwhile, many Americans are taking advantage of low mortgage rates to refinance their home loans.

Carrie Applegate finished refinancing the mortgage on her two-bedroom Boise home in January.

It wasn't easy. Ms. Applegate has lived in her home eight years, held the same administrative job at Boise State University for 10 years and has a solid credit score around 750. But when she had her house appraised last fall, its market value was only $130,000 -- which meant she had 19% equity in her home, not the 20% required by her bank to refinance. She scrapped that effort and retried under the government's Home Affordable Refinance Program, which makes it easier for borrowers to refinance.

Eventually the refinance came through -- allowing Ms. Applegate to shave about $100 off her monthly mortgage payment of $730. "I'm happy I did it, I'm better off financially," she said. The refinancing process "is starting to get better."

The economic recovery hasn't been a boon to stock pickers like Brett Horn.

Just several years ago, "hundreds" of undervalued stocks littered the market, recalls Mr. Horn, an analyst at Chicago-based investment-research firm Morningstar Inc. Now, many of them have bounced back.

"It's slim pickings," he says. "You're choosing your spots a little more carefully."

Still, that didn't keep Mr. Horn, 40 years old, from spotting potential in CoreLogic Inc. last year when others predicted doom. The residential real-estate data and services provider had just come off a dismal year, thanks to a lackluster housing market and a 2010 spinoff that burdened the company with several noncore businesses.

When it bottomed at $8 in August 2011, the stock was trading at less than half of what Mr. Horn believed it was worth. He issued a buy rating shortly after, betting that the company would rebound in the long term.

"It was very clear that the market was way too negative on them," he says. "They saw that the numbers were bad, but they didn't quite understand why."

It was a particularly brazen call for Mr. Horn, who joined Morningstar six years ago after nearly a decade in commercial banking, where he specialized in corporate lending. Transitioning to stock picking was challenging, he recalls, especially after years of focusing "strictly on the downside."

His optimistic hunch proved right when CoreLogic management launched a cost-cutting initiative and exited from certain noncore businesses. In a development that surprised even Mr. Horn, the housing market also experienced a refinancing boom. "I knew housing would recover, but I had no idea how quickly," he says. The stock more than doubled last year.

That wasn't his only win in 2012. He also issued buy and hold ratings on Lender Processing Services Inc., a provider of mortgage-processing services and analytics. The company presented a "risk factor," he acknowledges, mainly because of allegations that it "robosigned" documents to speed up foreclosures on homeowners. (It settled charges with the attorneys general of 46 states this past January.)

He suspected the stock was still undervalued, partly because he observed other investors were placing far too much emphasis on transaction volumes in the real-estate market when valuing companies like CoreLogic and Lender Processing Services. "They don't understand that in the long run, everything normalizes," he says.

Lender Processing Services returned 66% last year, according to FactSet.

His biggest regret, he says, is missing out on an opportunity to be bullish on Jack Henry & Associates Inc., a data- and payment-processing-services provider for financial-services firms. He maintained a hold rating throughout 2012, during which the stock returned 18%, according to FactSet. "The company just bounced back a lot faster than I expected, and a lot faster than its peers," he says.

Mr. Horn sees promise in Fiserv Inc., another banking-technology provider. While the stock is already fairly valued, he says, the business appears to be stable and well managed. "Safety is more of a priority, so a safe name would be a good pick," he says.

Leslie Kwoh

The Experts: What Is the Best Way to Invest on Your Own?

3,069 words
8 May 2013
03:20
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

What advice do prospective do-it-yourself investors need to hear before they start managing their own money? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887323741004578418611242496832.html" about do-it-yourself investors who are using new online tools and a-la-carte advice services to manage their money and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Monday, May 6.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Wealth Management Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts online at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/16jTSr8" three wealth-management thought leaders Morningstar's Director of Personal Finance Christine Benz ("https://twitter.com/christine_benz"), American Association of Individual Investors Vice President Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") and Portfolio Solutions Founder Rick Ferri ("https://twitter.com/Rick_Ferri") speak about succeeding as a do-it-yourself investor in a video chat that aired on Monday, May 6.

Tom Brakke: If You Really Want to Try, First Study Financial History

Think twice or maybe three times.

It is quite unlikely that you'll do very well fighting it out in the financial markets against those with more experience and information than you have. Plus, should you happen to somehow beat the averages, in retrospect it probably won't have been worth all of the time, effort and aggravation along the way.

If you still want to try your luck, start reading, but skip the how-to-beat-the-market books and spend your time learning financial history. Perspective and a long time horizon are what you need most, although you'll probably end up making the same mistakes that the rest of us make anyway.

Tom Brakke ("https://twitter.com/researchpuzzler") is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

Greg McBride: Invest for the Long Haul

Educate yourself as much as possible about investments, economics and financial markets. As far as investing goes, invest for the long haul and build the core of your portfolio with mutual funds and exchange-traded funds rather than individual securities. Keep investment expenses low by favoring index funds, no-load mutual funds and keep trading to a minimum.

Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Terrance Odean: Learn to Golf Instead

My advice to a prospective active do-it-yourself investor is to learn to golf. You'll get a little exercise, some fresh air and time with your friends. Sure, green fees can be steep, but not as steep as the hit your portfolio will take if you become an active do-it-yourself investor. That said, if your plan is to become a passive do-it-yourself investor, to buy and hold well diversified low-cost mutual funds (e.g., index funds), go for it. You'll still have time for golf and, barring a bear market, enough money for green fees.

Terrance Odean is the Rudd Family Foundation professor and chair of the finance group at the Haas School of Business at the University of California, Berkeley.

Rafael Pardo: First Set a Strategy, Then Focus on Tactics

Read, read, read. If you plan to go it alone as an investor, without the guidance of a professional, it is crucial to educate yourself. Failure to do so can result in a series of missteps and blunders that risk squandering away the savings and wealth you have accumulated. Of course, there exists an inordinate amount of literature regarding investment, and so the question becomes, "How do I figure out what I should read?"

At the risk of oversimplification, the key is to differentiate between literature that focuses on investment strategy and literature that focuses on investment tactics. Here, I'm drawing a lesson from the classic game of chess. Chess strategy broadly focuses on the long-term: What are the sound fundamentals that will guide my specific plans and moves to the ultimate goal of checkmate? Chess tactics, on the other hand, narrowly focus on the short-term: What specific moves will enable me to control a particular part of the board, capture a specific piece, etc.?

Your investment tactics should be a function of your investment strategy. First, have a clear picture of your investment goals for example, accumulating enough wealth to retire by the age of 65 or saving enough money to pay for your kids' college tuition. With that goal in mind, you can begin to winnow down the strategy literature to those writings that squarely align with your goals. After doing so, and after becoming familiar with the relevant strategy literature, you should have a better sense of the types of investment tactics that will enable you to bring your strategy to fruition. And after becoming familiar with the tactics literature, you will be ready to wade into what should no longer seem like uncharted waters.

Rafael Pardo is the Robert T. Thompson professor of Law at Emory University, where he specializes in bankruptcy and commercial law.

Sheryl Garrett: Read These Books. All of Them.

Invest in your knowledge before you invest in your brokerage account. If you haven't already, invest time reading really smart books on investing, such as; "The Four Pillars of Investing" by William Bernstein, "The Power of Passive Investing" by Richard A. Ferri, "Asset Allocation" by Roger C. Gibson, "Worry-Free Investing" by Zvi Bodie and Michael J. Clowes, "What Investors Really Want" by Meir Statman and "The Little Book of Common Sense Investing" by John C. Bogle. Read them all! It would only take a few days and it could save you or help you make a fortune.

Reading a few great investing books is my recommendation as a first step for all perspective do-it-yourself investors. Books are generally written with the focus on achieving long-term objectives, not making headlines or gathering a "following". After reading all these books, you'll have a pretty good idea on what your second step should be. If you don't, I suggest you rethink your idea of do-it-yourself, or consult with an investment professional to help you with the next step.

Sheryl Garrett ("https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") is founder of the Garrett Planning Network Inc.

Rick Ferri: Discipline Is the Most Difficult Step

The same advice I would give to anyone successful investing is a three-step process. Decide on an investment philosophy that you can believe in. Create a portfolio strategy based on this philosophy. Implement the strategy and continue to remain disciplined.

Figure out which investment philosophy has the highest probability for success. Do you believe you're smart enough to time markets or pick investments that are going to beat the markets, or wise enough not to know better? Go with the odds.

Strategy is a portfolio building process that starts by matching financial goals with a sound asset allocation. Product selection follows. It is well documented that low-cost index funds outperform a majority of actively managed funds in every investment category. You don't need anything else.

Discipline is the most difficult step. Staying committed to a plan during all market conditions can be a nerve-racking experience. Success goes to those who are willing to do what most people won't.

Rick Ferri is founder of "http://www.portfoliosolutions.com/" LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Michelle Perry Higgins: Be Guided by Facts, Not Feelings

There are essentially three key pieces of advice I would give to the DIY investor. First, don't ever make emotional trades within your portfolio. Create a sound financial strategy you can maintain and use that plan as your guide. You should always base your financial decision on facts, not feelings. If you don't believe you can do that, then perhaps it is time to hire an adviser. Second, keep your costs as low as possible within your portfolio. You might also want to consider the addition of index funds as they are highly cost-effective. Finally, if you are truly going to be a do-it-yourselfer, you should manage your portfolio as if it is a part-time job. Have a routine that you follow regularly and don't try to wing it. To be successful, you have to give your portfolio the necessary time, attention and research that is needed, otherwise known as T.L.C.

Michelle Perry Higgins ("https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Larry Zimpleman: Don't Try to Time the Market

First, I'd want to know two things is this "serious money" as opposed to play money, and does the investor really have a background that allows him or her to make good investment decisions? Assuming we're talking about serious money and that the person has the basic background to make investment decisions, I would suggest rule No. 1 is resist the temptation to market-time (at least with any meaningful amount of money). Study after study shows the danger of trying to market-time. My second piece of advice would be to think about an overall asset allocation approach (based on your tolerance for risk) and then to stay consistent to that asset allocation don't be changing the allocation based on market movements over relatively short periods like six or even 12 months. Since it is serious money, you should have time going for you, so avoid the temptation to make short-term adjustments.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

Charles Rotblut: Four Steps to Better Returns

There are four major points I would make.

The first is to be realistic about your interest level in finance and the amount of time you can devote to managing a portfolio. If looking at a 10-K (an annual report required by the SEC) or analyzing a cash-flow statement puts you to sleep, then you should consider using index funds instead. Similarly, if you don't have the time to read through earnings conference-call transcripts, consider using an index fund.

The second is to consider both your financial and your emotional tolerance for risk. Financial risk is a factor of age, health and wealth. The less money you need relative to the size of your portfolio, and the further out in the future you need the money, the bigger your portfolio allocation to stocks can be. As far as your emotional tolerance, think about what you did with your investments (e.g. your 401(k) plan) in 2008 and even 2001. If you panicked and sold as many people did, you need to consider a less volatile allocation.

The third is to use the power of the written word to your advantage. Write down the reasons that cause you to favorably view an investment and the reasons you would sell it BEFORE you make the actual purchase. Doing this will help you more professionally manage your portfolio and give you a strategy for times when the market's volatility makes you nervous.

The fourth is to pick good securities and diversify. Look for securities of financially strong companies trading at discounted valuations. (Value beats growth over the long term, but some securities are cheap for a reason.) Also, hold a variety of stocks and bonds, including international securities. The unspoken advantage of diversification is that it increases the odds of being in the right asset class at the right time.

Do these four simple things and your future investment returns will be better.

Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

Eleanor Blayney: Know When to Fold

"Know when to hold 'em, know when to fold 'em" &with particular emphasis on the folding.

In my experience, many individuals who manage their own portfolios are also buy-and-holders. Even when they can afford professional management, they reason that they can do better with selecting and holding securities than they will do with an adviser, particularly when they factor in the assets-under-management fee. They choose what they believe are good securities for the long haul, and then walk away from them to do something more productive like playing golf or Sudoku.

They don't sell their holdings, either, because it is hard to admit that their great stock pick isn't so great anymore, or because selling may result in capital gains and they hate paying taxes.

To be a successful manager of your own investments, you need to be disciplined, humble and dispassionate. You need to systematically rebalance the portfolio and be willing to admit your mistakes or at the very least that great stocks, like great athletes, cannot stay on top forever. You need to have a sell strategy in place whenever you buy a new holding. You especially need to be careful never to fall in love with your choices, and at the same time, never allow yourself to hate taxes to the point that the tax tail has wagged the dog to death.

Finally, you may want to consider the Dalbar research that shows that investors, left purely to their own devices, underperform the market and market indexes by a significant margin.

Eleanor Blayney ("https://twitter.com/EleanorBlayney") is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards.

Matt Hougan: You're Never Going to Beat the Market

Don't do it.

First, it is hard. If you think you're going to beat the market, you're wrong. There are very highly paid people who have enormous incentives to beat the market and work at it full-time with nearly infinite resources; almost all of them fail. You're not going to be any different.

But let's say you're smarter than that. You're just going to buy a few index funds, sit back and relax. You're still going to do a terrible job. You'll forget to rebalance, you won't reinvest your dividends, you'll panic and sell at the bottom during a crisis. You'll make a muck of it.

There are good systems out there now Wealthfront, to take one example that will keep you invested in a solid portfolio of low cost ETFs for almost nothing. Do that instead. You'll end up with more money at the end.

Beyond performance, the biggest reason not to go it alone is that you've got better things to do. Your job. Your family. The laundry. Unless you're enormously wealthy, the biggest source of wealth you have is your job. Work at that, and leave the money bit to someone else.

Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

Manisha Thakor: Don't Skip Ahead to the 'Fun Part'

I'd give the exact same advice I'd give to any investor, which is to make sure you can answer these three questions at all time:

(1)What is my asset allocation?

(2)What has my performance been?

(3)What fees am I paying?

To me, these are the core essentials that every investor (do-it-yourself or work-with-an-adviser) should know. If you don't know the answers, you aren't alone &most folks I meet can't answer these questions about their investments. Alas, this is a bit like trying to build a house but not knowing what your foundation is made out of i.e. you can never be truly sure you've got a sturdy structure. So while the tendency may be to jump right into the "fun part" of selecting specific investments to make, taking the time to understand these three elements will enable you to make stronger and more informed decisions for your long run financial health.

I'd also suggest you think long and hard about pursuing an "active" over a "passive" or index-based approach. Time and again academic research shows the primary driver of long-run returns is asset allocation (the mix you select between broad asset classes such as stocks, bonds and cash) as opposed to security selection (trying to find the next hot investment). Whether it is through the use of active mutual funds or selecting individual securities, this approach statistically speaking isn't the winner's game. For more on this, I'd recommend potential do-it-yourself investors read Larry Swedroe's new book "Think, Act, and Invest Like Warren Buffett."

Manisha Thakor ("https://twitter.com/ManishaThakor") is founder and chief executive of Santa Fe, N.M.-based MoneyZen Wealth Management LLC.

George Papadopoulos: Don't Hit for the Fence

 " Understand that how much you invest will have a greater impact on your retirement security than how well your investments perform.

 " You will likely be better off giving up hitting home runs with your stock picking and instead investing in low-cost index mutual funds and ETFs.

 " Avoid investments you don't understand.

 " Have a plan in place and set goals. If your goal is to invest for maximum performance, be aware that you must be prepared for maximum performance on the downside, too.

 " Diversify, keep costs low, be aware of Uncle Sam and rebalance at set intervals. An investment portfolio is like a garden. If you don't tend to it, weeds will damage it. If you don't have time to tend to the garden yourself, look for a full-time gardener, while you focus on what you do best.

 " Keep it simple. You don't need to invest in a highly complex hedge fund. There is no secret sauce or market guru out there.

 " Have an emergency cash fund in place and make an effort to be debt-free by the time you retire. No one has ever told me that they regretted paying off the mortgage early.

 " Don't be too greedy or too fearful. Easier said than done.

George Papadopoulos ("https://twitter.com/feeonlyplanner") is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- The Experts: Insights From Our Online Panel

1,291 words
6 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Two questions are never far from investors' minds: On what occasions might active fund management be better than an index fund, and what would Warren Buffett do? Meanwhile, recent gyrations in the stock market continue to cause investors on Wall Street and Main Street to watch policy developments in Washington with a mixture of dread and anticipation. For insights, The Wall Street Journal turned to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Reports.

Edited excerpts of those discussions follow:

---

Under what circumstances should somebody use active money managers as opposed to index funds?

Two Scenarios Favor Active

In general, traditional index funds need certain ingredients in underlying markets in order to best function. These include minimum levels of liquidity, market capitalization and cost efficiency. When those elements are not ideal, active management is a better choice because it is more flexible when dealing with inefficiencies.

The second area where active managers can be preferable is when implementing investment strategies that are complex or fast moving. These strategies are difficult to index due to the many contextual variables that can arise.

-- Christian Magoon, CEO of Magoon Capital

In the Long Term, Never

If we are talking about investing over the long term, never! If we are talking about short-term trading, perhaps you can try to identify an active money manager and hope to get lucky.

-- George Papadopoulos, a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich.

For My Enemies . . .

I can think of many cases in which I would recommend active money managers over index funds. I might be giving the advice to someone I hate or -- and this happens a lot -- someone I expect to hate later. I would also recommend active money managers if I were accepting bribes to do so, if I were an active money manager myself, or if it were April Fools' Day.

-- Scott Adams, creator of "Dilbert" comic strip

Specialized Equity Markets

I believe a portion of an investor's diversified portfolio should hold actively managed funds, especially for specialized equity markets that require specialized knowledge and expertise, as the pricing tends to be inefficient. These areas include emerging markets, small-cap stocks and companies involved in resources and metals mining. The key is in selecting an active manager who has extensive experience investing in these markets, as they understand the seasonal and historical patterns that help them navigate these complex areas.

-- Frank Holmes, chief executive and chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors Inc .

Small-Cap Stocks and Emerging Markets

While I am a big proponent of index investing, investors can increase their odds of adding incremental return to their portfolios by seeking active managers that have a strong track record and consistent investment philosophy, particularly in small-cap stocks and emerging markets.

-- Greg McBride , a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com

---

What's the most valuable thing Washington could do for investors in mutual funds and ETFs?

Roll Out the Rest of Dodd-Frank

Washington needs to implement the investor-protection agenda in the Dodd-Frank Act. All advisers, brokers and insurance agents who provide personalized investment advice must be held to a duty of loyalty, prudence and care for their clients. This is not the case today, and most investors don't realize it. Most people own mutual funds or ETFs in accounts advised or managed by a financial intermediary. The choice of which fund to use should be based solely on your needs, not on those of anyone else.

-- John Rogers , president and chief executive of CFA Institute

Level the Playing Field

Regulators need to ensure the playing field between institutional and individual investors is level. Products and providers need to be overseen and mischief needs to be punished. Expenses should be kept in line as well.

-- Charles Rotblut , vice president with American Association of Individual Investors , vice president with

Put Clients First

Changing the culture and regulatory structure to mandate that investor interests must always come first, and that the adviser is the party responsible for proving they did right by the client, would drastically improve investors' comfort in the markets, with investing and with advisers.

-- Sheryl Garrett , founder of Garrett Planning Network Inc.

Enforce a Fiduciary Duty

First, force brokers to have a fiduciary duty to their clients. The market is going that way anyway, and (by definition) it would be what's best for investors. Finances are too important to do any other way. Second, require the clear disclosure of fees. We're making progress in the 401(k) space, but sterling clarity should be the goal across all financial products and services.

-- Matt Hougan, president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC

Ensure Investors Are Protected

Bipartisan action and commitment to getting our fiscal house in order in a fair, balanced way would send an important message to businesses and markets that would be positive for investors. Corporations, in particular, are holding huge amounts of cash as a result of the continuing (and tiresome) uncertainty created by congressional gridlock. Getting this cash working in capital projects and more hires would increase economic growth and rates of investment returns.

-- Eleanor Blayney , consumer advocate of Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards

---

What lessons can individual investors learn from Berkshire Hathaway 's Warren Buffett ?

Invest in Things. Don't Rent.

Don't sell. Invest in things; don't rent them for speculative returns. There are caveats to that, of course: if you're tax-loss harvesting, or if the investment landscape shifts, or if your initial thesis (that you wrote down when you first invested) breaks down.

-- Matt Hougan

Keep It Real

Focus on the business aspects of any investment. Research thoroughly. Trust few. Avoid leverage, and be patient and willing to invest forever.

And, if you don't have the time or interest to do all of this with a great deal of confidence, Warren Buffett himself would recommend that we simply invest in index funds.

-- Sheryl Garrett

Don't Follow the Crowd

The most important lesson that Mr. Buffett can offer anyone: Don't get swept up by the crowd.

He has been willing to sit on a mountain of cash, even though interest rates are extremely low. And he is willing to be out of step for long periods in order to do well in the end. Most of us have a very hard time doing that.

-- Tom Brakke, consultant, writer and investment adviser

Be a 'Mensch'

Mr. Buffett teaches us that the most important part of being a self-made man is being a "mensch," a decent man. He recognizes the role of community efforts and luck in his success. He also recognizes the role of government in helping people who have not been successful, and the fairness of sharing the fruits of success.

-- Meir Statman, the Glenn Klimek professor of finance at Santa Clara University , and visiting professor at Tilburg University in the Netherlands

Stick to Your Strategy

A large part of Mr. Buffett's success has come from his ability to stay calm and be willing to increase his stockholdings when everyone else is selling. Everyone has heard they should "buy low and sell high," but few people have shown the ability to truly buy low as Mr. Buffett has.

The second is the importance of having a rational, well-thought-out investment strategy and sticking to it. Mr. Buffett has built a fortune by investing in bargain stocks -- good quality companies that trade at attractive prices.

-- Charles Rotblut

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
'When the moon is full, the crazies come out'; Interview

By Steve Johnson 
1,176 words
6 May 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
04
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Acadian's Churchill Franklin discusses the success of the quant house's unorthodox data gathering with Steve Johnson

Churchill Franklin, the grandly named chief executive of Acadian Asset Management, takes pride in the "objective and unemotional" way the quantitative investment house runs money.

But some of the data Boston-based Acadian crunches in order to make its asset allocation decisions are capable of raising the odd eyebrow.

"If you have ever worked in a bank, you know when the moon is full, the crazies come out," says Mr Franklin, underlining Acadian's observation that the phases of the moon appear to be correlated with moves in equity markets.

Acadian's lunar link is indicative of the lengths it goes to in the hunt for data sources that may give it an edge over competitors.

In order to validate (notoriously smooth) Chinese economic growth data, Acadian's sleuths study satellite pictures to determine if the factory lights are on and attempt to count the number of shipping containers leaving Chinese ports. There are rumours of some companies dispatching empty containers in an attempt to disguise a slowdown in orders.

Acadian also engages in automated reading of periodicals in the search for keywords that might signal future price moves, as well as analysing GPS data produced by tractors as they plough fields.

"The key for us is having the best people that can keep you relatively close to the leading edge of data acquisition," says Mr Franklin, who co-founded Acadian in 1986, 14 years before it was bought by London and Johannesburg-listed Old Mutual, the insurer.

The quant house has since gathered $57bn of assets from 500 institutions in 25 countries for its equity and emerging market debt propositions. Mr Franklin largely attributes this to the success of Acadian's process of "cognitive diversity", a belief that multiple perspectives lead to better ideas and help identify more sources of "alpha", or outperformance.

"The accounting guy, the corporate finance guy, the behavioural finance individual, the machine-based learning, data recognition, robotics [people] - we put all of these capabilities together and we get the classic diversity of thought.

"That is something we believe in. We get more perspectives and more frenetic discussion and we get better outcomes," says Mr Franklin.

"We totally believe in the integrity of the data. The debate will not be about whether we think China is going to collapse, it's how deeply we believe in the robustness of the data or how likely is it to change, to be revised.

"One of the edges we have is we have been doing this all our career. We know when to be suspicious and when to be confident."

That suspicion has led Acadian to reject some potential data feeds, such as the much-hyped ability of Twitter , the microblogging service, to predict markets.

David Purdy, director of marketing analytics at Acadian, argues there is "no evidence" that the balance of positive and negative tweets has any predictive power. "They are synchronised with the market. There is no information that is usable at this stage," he says.

As an example, Mr Purdy points to the level of fear in tweets, which in the US rises every year around the anniversary of the 1999 Columbine high school massacre.

"If you don't understand the data, both the source of it and the meaning of it, it can be very painful," he says.

Similarly, Acadian is wary of correlations it can identify, but not explain. For instance, it says that if the Super Bowl is won by a team from the AFL (rather than the rival NFL), the US stock market tends to rise the following day.

Somewhat surprisingly for a house that sets such store by data, Acadian has been quick to embrace frontier markets , where accurate numbers can be hard to come by. "The data can be lousy, but as long as you have a little bit more than the next guy, that's OK," says Mr Franklin.

He also argues that quant houses have an advantage over bottom-up fundamental players in the fragmented frontier landscape.

"If we had to send an analyst to all of these countries we would go bust in a heartbeat," he argues.

Despite this, Mr Franklin admits Acadian's frontier markets equity offering, which has a capacity of just $400m-$500m, is not "hugely profitable" at present, but he is confident this will change.

"Frontier markets feel to us as emerging markets did in 1993. They have no debt, they have great natural resources, they have a developing consumer class. If you look at a 30-year time horizon, these countries are going to develop."

That said, Acadian does not believe emerging markets themselves are always perfect. It operates a corruption-adjusted price-to-book ratio as a valuation tool, factoring in corruption data from Transparency International, a non-governmental organisation.

"The least corrupt [countries] are not necessarily the best performers but we do find that corruption in general is a tax on the investor, so the more corrupt will have to have a lower valuation to overcome that," says Mr Franklin, who believes examples such as this "humanise" what Acadian is doing.

"The world views quants as black boxy, mathematical and not fundamentally driven. I think of Acadian as being very fundamental, but systematic, repeatable and consistent."

One of Acadian's specialisms is managed volatility strategies, which aim to provide equity-like returns with around 65-70 per cent of the volatility usually associated with stock market investing.

It was a relatively early adopter of the concept, alongside Unigestion, Axa Rosenberg and Robeco, which appears capable of delivering anomalously high absolute, as well as risk-adjusted, returns .

Mr Franklin says the concept "is really taking off", especially in the UK and the Japanese pension sector, although the manner in which such strategies are used may tell us something about regional differences. In Australia and the US investors tend to leverage up their exposure, hoping for equity market volatility but equity-beating returns. More risk-averse Europeans are happy to hope for lower volatility and market returns.

Mr Franklin suggests managed volatility approaches are also seeing interest from institutions that feel impelled to trim their low-yielding fixed income holdings and take on board a little more risk.

"If you think fixed income is going to solve any problems from here on in, that's a hard pill to swallow," he warns.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,778 words
3 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
