English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Economy Unmoved By ECB

Mario Draghi still has the power to steer markets: Bonds rallied and the euro fell as the European Central Bank cut interest rates to a euro-era low of 0.5% and hinted that more might yet be on the table. But the ECB president had to admit that he felt frustrated in his attempts to steer the euro-zone economy out of its funk and that there had been little progress on finding ways of funneling lending to cash-strapped companies. That isn't surprising the real work needs to be done by governments.

For markets, the most significant moment came as Mr. Draghi opened the door a little wider to cutting the rate it pays on money deposited with the ECB, currently at zero, into negative territory. Mr. Draghi warned again that such a cut could have unintended consequences but added that the ECB would "address and cope" with these consequences making negative rates a possibility. That sent German two-year yields into negative territory and pushed the euro sharply lower against the dollar.

Mr. Draghi raised hopes initially by saying the ECB's Governing Council had made a decision on possible ways to enhance credit provision. But that turned out to be only the start of consultations with bodies such as the European Investment Bank to promote a market for asset-backed securities backed by corporate loans. Mr. Draghi admitted that this market is currently "dead" and that the ECB had no precise view on how it might work, meaning there is little near-term relief in the cards for Southern European companies finding it tough to get funding. And even if a funding mechanism were to be developed, there remain questions about bank capital and risk appetite on the part of both lenders and borrowers that the ECB can't address. This is no silver bullet for the crisis.

A negative deposit rate could lead to a much weaker euro, potentially a help for Southern European nations as they rebalance their economies. But the ECB sees the risks to inflation still as broadly balanced, potentially meaning the bar is high for a further cut at least in the near term.

Instead, Mr. Draghi is still calling on governments to take action, in particular on structural reform and creation of a banking union. He did, however, slightly soften the central bank's line on austerity, saying that at the least governments should avoid undoing the progress that has been made on fiscal consolidation.

Unless the economy takes a further sharp turn for the worse, the ECB looks likely to be on hold for some time. Euro-zone politicians have been calling for the ECB to act. Now that it has, the ball is back in their court.

-- Richard Barley

Sanofi's Slump May Be Short-Lived

Welcome to the new growth era in pharmaceuticals. Sanofi's first-quarter profit dropped by a third, after the French drug maker last year lost patent protection in the U.S. on blood-thinner Plavix and hypertension drug Avapro. The pharma group's earnings-per-share forecast this year remains at best flat. But Sanofi's business looks like a decent proxy for the future of the pharmaceutical sector. After years of slumping sales from pharmaceutical products, the worst at Sanofi is over.

Sanofi went into the era of the so-called patent cliff among the worst-placed of its peers. Blockbuster drugs close to losing patent protection accounted for close to 30% of revenue in 2008. Their contribution is now about 3%, set to be negligible by year-end. Sanofi shed its rather parochial attitude to research and development and embraced third-party innovation with a number of licensing agreements. It has bulked up in rare diseases, animal health and consumer medicines. And faster-growing emerging markets already account for about a third of sales, ahead of most European and U.S. peers.

Sanofi expects sales and profit growth in the final quarters of this year and into next year. The delay to the U.S. launch of Novo Nordisk's Tresiba insulin until at least 2017 has created an opportunity for Sanofi's diabetes business. With that tailwind, Sanofi could post double-digit earnings growth next year, argue analysts at Citigroup. The bank forecasts compound annual growth of 9% from this year to 2018, compared with the sector at 7%.

Some skepticism is warranted. An uninspiring quarter in some supposed growth areas, such as animal health, underlined Sanofi's reliance on its diabetes unit, where sales jumped 20%. Moreover, Sanofi's pipeline lacks the near-term excitement of Roche's; the French company won't have final data from trials of its first cholesterol-lowering biologic with partner Regeneron until the back end of next year. But Sanofi, at about 12.5 times 2014 earnings, still trades at a slight discount to diversified peers like GlaxoSmithKline and Novartis, despite its superior growth outlook.

Investors should be wary of betting against a Sanofi bounce-back.

-- Helen Thomas

Infineon Makes

Rapid Rebound

Infineon Technologies is kicking itself back into gear. But investors may be getting ahead of themselves.

Europe's second-largest chip maker by sales says its order books are filling up again after a trying year. That pushed the shares up 9.9% Thursday, reversing recent falls amid fears of a prolonged downturn in the automotive market, from which Infineon gets 46% of its sales. Those fears now look overdone.

True, Infineon still expects revenue to fall by a mid-single-digit percentage this year, while operating profit margins will be below 10%. But sales in its automotive division, which makes sensors and power semiconductors, have stabilized.

Demand for Infineon's chips should continue to grow faster than the moribund car market. While global car production is likely to rise just 2.2% this year, the value of chips used in each car could increase 4.4%, estimates Strategy Analytics. Infineon says premium cars, to which it is a major supplier, contain three times the chip value as midsize or compact models.

Infineon's profitability may take longer to recover. It invested heavily in new machinery in its auto and industrial power businesses last year, which has led to much higher depreciation charges. Infineon believes at least 12% operating profit margins should be achievable longer term, compared with 9% in the second quarter.

But the loss-making industrial power control unit, which supplies engineering companies like ABB and Siemens, faces a tougher slog: Although customer destocking appears to have come to an end, Infineon doubts the unit's margins can return to peak levels of over 20% in 2011, when it benefited from constrained capacity in the industry. Infineon is currently running at 80% capacity across all of its units.

Infineon is already trading at 1.3 times forecast 2014 sales, in line with its three-year average, based on Morgan Stanley estimates. But whereas Infineon generated returns on capital employed comfortably above its midteens cost of capital over that period, returns were just 7% in the second quarter. It would take a much more robust recovery to justify chasing the shares much higher.

-- Renee Schultes

Glencore

Xstrata Is

No Miracle

Birth

It's been a long and painful birth: But GlencoreXstrata is finally here. The mining company will begin trading on Friday, with a market capitalization of about $66 billion. The merger process has delivered Chief Executive and 8%-shareholder Ivan Glasenberg and his longtime Glencore management colleagues their dream: full control of the new company. Xstrata will bring a stronger balance sheet and more commodities for Glencore's traders to use generating arbitrage opportunities in physical commodity markets.

But will investors enjoy watching the infant grow?

GlencoreXstrata will certainly be a noisier kid than staid peers like BHP Billiton and Rio Tinto. The company's pro forma Ebitda was $12.9 billion last year, with 80% generated from mining. But Mr. Glasenberg is a trader to his fingertips. His pitch is that GlencoreXstrata can generate a superior return on equity through opportunistic asset buying, selling assets and returning more cash to shareholders if potential returns decline. Avoiding growth for growth's sake, an approach that has recently led to large asset write-downs, is Mr. Glasenberg's mantra.

Still, Glencore's track record doesn't yet prove that its model delivers better returns. Its return on equity averages 15% over the four years since the financial crisis, slightly better than the 10% delivered by Rio and Anglo American. But in that period, BHP has generated a 26% average annual ROE, albeit its year-end is in June. Moreover, Glencore has substantial assets in politically risky countries like the Democratic Republic of the Congo and Kazakhstan. If problems arise in such places, a nimble exit could prove tricky.

For investors to give GlencoreXstrata a premium rating, they will have to trust that Mr. Glasenberg can continue handling those political risks while practicing his capital-discipline gospel. Finding a strong new chairman might help allay concerns Glencore is run by a clever but self-interested group. Since its May 2011 flotation, Glencore has traded at eight times its expected earnings, just below the average of 8.2 times for major mining companies, according to Sanford C. Bernstein; its shares are down nearly 40%.

GlencoreXstrata is bringing welcome diversity to the mining sector. But toasting the new arrival would be premature.

-- Andrew Peaple

Overheard

DreamWorks Animation is making a bet on television only this channel happens to reside on YouTube. The studio said it is buying AwesomenessTV, a teen-focused online network, for $33 million, plus additional payments that could bring the price up to $117 million if the ad-financed service hits certain earnings targets.

DreamWorks has long held ambitions to own, or partner in, a TV network. Buying a YouTube channel is cheaper than starting a new one and avoids the inevitable struggle with pay-TV providers over carriage fees.

DreamWorks hasn't said which, if any, of its content it plans to put on Awesomeness. But the site could help it make money off the portfolio of older titles like "Casper the Friendly Ghost" and "Lassie" that it got when it bought Classic Media. It could also provide an outlet for shows spun off from its animated films. The purchase has another benefit: Its CEO, Brian Robbins, is a Nickelodeon veteran and has considerable experience in children's programming. For DreamWorks, the new channel may live up to its name.

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
Twitter move fuels listing talk

By Arash Massoudi and Tracy Alloway in New York and April Dembosky in San Francisco

New employee Gaylor is expert on IPOs

Morgan Stanley banker worked on Facebook

Twitter has fuelled speculation that it is preparing for an initial public offering by naming Cynthia Gaylor, a Morgan Stanley investment banker who has worked on deals for Facebook, LinkedIn and Zynga, as its head of corporate development.

The social media company announced the appointment in a tweet and Ms Gaylor responded in kind , saying that she looked forward to focusing on mergers, acquisitions and strategy at the company.

Many analysts regard an IPO as the natural next step for the company, which launched in 2006 and has risen from a quirky start-up to a leading technology and media company with a value, after an investment this year by BlackRock, the asset manager of more than $9bn.

"This kind of hire is a clear indicator that Twitter is preparing now for an IPO," said Sam Hamadeh, founder of market research firm PrivCo.

Facebook, LinkedIn and Zynga, companies Ms Gaylor has worked with for Morgan Stanley, have all been taken public in the past two years.

The move caused a stir on Wall Street, not only as a sign of a likely IPO but also for its significance for Morgan Stanley.

Morgan Stanley's team of west coast technology bankers, led by Michael Grimes, has dominated tech sector deals in recent years, culminating in 2012 with the much-coveted lead adviser role on Facebook's $16.1bn IPO .

However, the chaos surrounding the botched public offering threw the status of the tech team into doubt.

Morgan Stanley was fined $5m last year by a state regulator , which said the company's investment bankers had exerted "improper influence" on the research analysts covering Facebook during the IPO.

Ms Gaylor has most recently been leading Morgan Stanley's technology investment banking division focusing on the financing of capital markets placements.

Ms Gaylor's expertise with IPOs will be helpful if Twitter pursues a public debut while her skills and industry connections will strengthen Twitter in the fierce battle for Silicon Valley talent. Twitter, Facebook and Google often vie to buy the same companies, either for the team of engineers or the products they are developing.

In the past months, Twitter has acquired a series of companies, including a music discovery service We Are Hunted, Bluefin Labs , a social TV analytics firm, and Crashlytics, a mobile crash reporting tool.

World News: Europe: Jobless Rate in Euro Zone Hit a Record in March

By Paul Hannon and Brian Blackstone 
1,001 words
1 May 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
6
English
Copyright  2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

LONDON -- The euro zone's unemployment rate rose to a fresh high in March, while the annual rate of inflation fell to its lowest level in April since February 2010, a combination that increases the likelihood that the European Central Bank will cut its benchmark interest rate Thursday.

With a further 62,000 people losing their jobs during the month, bringing the total to 1.723 million over the past 12 months, opposition to the austerity programs pursued by many euro-zone governments in recent years is likely to mount.

The European Union's official statistics agency Tuesday said the rate of unemployment across the 17 countries that share the euro rose to 12.1% from 12.0%, the highest level since records began in 1995. By contrast, the unemployment rate in the U.S. stood at 7.6%, while in Japan it was 4.3%.

Eurostat also said the annual rate of inflation fell to 1.2% in April from 1.7% in March, to hit its lowest level in more than three years. The ECB defines price stability, the maintenance of which is its sole mission, as an inflation rate of just below 2.0%.

The inflation rate was well below expectations, with economists surveyed last week expecting a drop to 1.6%.

The rise in unemployment brought the total number of people without jobs to 19.21 million, but many economists expect that to rise further as the year progresses.

"Policy makers need to address this issue urgently," said Marie Diron, senior economic adviser to business-services firm Ernst & Young. "First, the ECB needs to relax monetary policy at its meeting on Thursday. Second, the nature and pace of fiscal consolidation needs to be reviewed and amended."

The ECB is widely expected to reduce its main lending rate by 0.25 percentage point to a record-low 0.5% at Thursday's meeting in the Slovakian capital of Bratislava, one of the meetings the central bank holds away from its Frankfurt headquarters.

But while signaling that a rate cut is a possibility, ECB officials pointed out that it wouldn't provide much of a lift to the bloc's economy, particularly in Spain and other crisis-hit countries where small business face high borrowing costs.

"The pass-through of rate cuts to the periphery would be limited, and this is where they are most needed," ECB board member Jorg Asmussen said last week.

That has prompted some economists to call for more radical action, such as large-scale purchases of private-sector assets, which they argue would provide more help to ailing economies.

"Given that these economies [in Southern Europe] are already in deep recession and have more austerity coming, [lower interest rates] won't turn things around," said Jennifer McKeown, an economist at Capital Economics, a consultancy.

However, ECB officials have been cool to these proposals, saying it is up to governments to find ways to boost growth prospects.

Within the euro zone, there were wide differences among national unemployment rates, which were highest in those countries that have pursued the most aggressive austerity programs, or suffered the largest economic contractions.

The unemployment rate was highest in Spain at 26.7%, followed by 17.5% in Portugal, while the unemployment rate was lowest in Austria at 4.7%, followed by Germany at 5.4%. Figures for Greece have yet to be released, and it is likely to have recorded a higher rate than Spain, given that it did so in January, the most recent month for which numbers are available.

Figures released separately by German's Labor Ministry showed the number of people without jobs rose by 4,000 in April, having risen by 12,000 in March. However, the unemployment as calculated using Germany's own methodology was unchanged at 6.9%, near its lowest level since reunification in 1990.

"Germany's sluggish economy appears to be starting to have a negative impact on the labor market," said Thomas Harjes, an economist at Barclays. "Firms appear less willing to expand their labor force at times of still-elevated economic uncertainty and rising wage pressures."

The rise in unemployment and easing of inflationary pressures are linked. As the number of people without work rises, consumer demand weakens, and companies respond by cutting their prices, or limiting increases to the minimum needed to preserve profit margins.

"Rising unemployment, higher taxes, weakening wage growth and tightened credit are eating into household purchasing power and constraining household spending," said Anna Zabrodzka, an economist at Moody'sAnalytics.

Data released Tuesday painted a mixed picture of the state of consumer demand in the euro zone's largest members. While a monthly survey recorded a pickup in consumer confidence in Germany, figures for March showed retail sales fell for the second straight month.

By contrast, French consumer spending rose faster than expected in March, although that partly reflected the higher cost of heating homes given unusually cold weather, and purchases of manufactured goods actually fell.

The euro-zone economy contracted in each of the five quarters to the end of 2012. Official figures for the first quarter of this year won't be published until May 15, although business surveys and the available data suggest that they will record a sixth quarter of contraction.

Figures released by Spain's statistics agency Tuesday showed the euro zone's No. 4 economy contracted 0.5% in the first three months of the year, a less steep decline than the 0.8% fall recorded in the fourth quarter of last year.

The euro zone isn't alone in experiencing a decline in the annual rate of inflation. The Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development said that across its 34 members, inflation fell to its lowest level in three years during March.

---

Nina Adam contributed to this article.

Real Estate
Saving Affordable Housing

By A.D. Pruitt and Dawn Wotapka 
728 words
1 May 2013
01:07
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

An apartment complex in suburban Chicago was saved from the clutches of gentrification by a private real-estate investment trust formed to buy and protect affordable housing.

The property, serving a working-class section of Aurora, Ill., was acquired for $5.2 million by a Chicago unit of Denver-based Mercy Housing Inc. in a joint venture with Housing Partnership Equity Trust, a private REIT that was created by Mercy and 11 other housing-related nonprofits.

Although the deal was small, it is significant in that it was the first acquisition made by a real-estate company owned and operated by nonprofits and illustrates how organizations with a social mission can use the for-profit structure to advance their goals.

Housing Partnership has raised over $100 million from investors, including Citigroup Inc., Morgan Stanley and Prudential Financial Inc., as well as the John D. and Catherine T. MacArthur Foundation. The money will be chasing additional acquisitions in the future, says Drew Ades, Housing Partnership's chief executive.

Housing advocates have been alarmed by the rapid decline in the supply of affordable housing, the result of landlords scooping up low-income properties and converting them to luxury dwellings to capitalize on rising values for multifamily properties.

The increased activity of for-profits has made it difficult for nonprofits to remain active buyers; they can't pay the high price tags or move quickly. "We're losing affordable units like crazy," says Cindy Holler, president of Mercy Housing Lakefront, the Chicago office of Mercy Housing, one of the country's largest nonprofits dedicated to affordable housing.

To compete with the profit-making companies for properties, the housing advocates are combining the benefits of their nonprofit structure with the financial flexibility that comes from operating a REIT.

"We were only able to get this property because of this REIT structure," says Ms. Holler, noting that Mercy closed on the property in three months instead of the two years it would typically take to raise financing strictly through the nonprofit channels. Yet, Mercy's nonprofit status came in handy given it was able to use state tax credits to secure the property at a below-market rate.

"There is a stock of affordable housing that's really much more appropriate in mission-driven hands," says Mr. Ades, the former executive director of multifamily risk at Fannie Mae. "You have private investors that are coming in and purchasing these properties, hoping to get 12%, 15% and 20% returns. The only way that happens is if you increase the rents," he says.

The Joint Center for Housing Studies of Harvard said in its 2011 report that the recession increased the pool of low-income renters at the same time competition for cheaper affordable housing units became more fierce. For instance, renters earning $15,000 or less rose by 2.2 million over the past decade, as affordable rentals declined by 470,000.

Some of tenants at the Aurora property expressed relief their new landlord would keep rents affordable.

"We couldn't afford any more than what we're paying," said 64-year-old Sharon Wetherbe, who lives in the complex with her husband. She said they probably would have to move into a nursing home if they had to leave their two-bedroom apartment where they pay $855 a month, accounting for half of their monthly income.

The average income for residents at the complex is about $26,000 a year, where they have access to an outdoor pool with a sun deck, covered parking and a community room with free Wi-Fi. Their rent is substantially lower than the average in Chicago.

Mr. Ades, declined to disclose the projected returns of the REIT, but estimated the capitalization rate for the acquisition was relatively high at about 7%, indicating a strong initial yield for the buyer. The average cap rate for apartments nationally was 6.1% in the first quarter, according to Real Capital Analytics. In major metro areas, where investor demand for apartments remains strong, the rate was 5.5%.

In addition to Mercy, other owners of Housing Partnership include Homes for America Inc. based in Annapolis, Md., LINC Housing Corp. of Long Beach, Calif., and NHP Foundation in New York.

Write to Dawn Wotapka at "mailto:dawn.wotapka@dowjones.com"

Special
THE MONEY GAME; Warning: Property Foreclosures Ahead; Small companies face balloon payments on their mortgages and no way to raise the money

By Emily Maltby 
1,129 words
29 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Many small firms that own commercial property are facing big trouble.

The problem is simple: Banks typically re-evaluate commercial mortgages every five to 10 years. At that point, they can renew the loans, or ask business owners to pay them off.

These days, lenders are a lot less willing to extend loans that don't seem like good bets and it's tough for businesses to look creditworthy after years of slumped sales and exhausted savings. "So many small businesses have lost their reserves during the recession," says Brent Case, president of Coldwell Banker Commercial Atlantic International Inc., in Charleston, S.C. "They don't have that chunk of cash that banks want as a buffer."

That leaves many small-business owners scrambling to find a lender that will cover their loan or facing the loss of their property through foreclosure.

Trouble in Store

For an idea of the scope the problem, consider this: Some $276.2 billion of nonresidential commercial-property loans are expected to come due in 2013. That's higher than any prior year, according to Trepp LLC, a commercial-mortgage research firm in New York.

Many of these loans were made leading up to the financial collapse in 2008, when property values were high and business owners could depend on steady income to repay the loan. In other cases, the loans came due in the depths of the financial crisis, but banks were willing to give businesses extensions essentially pushing the question of refinancing to a later date. Some analysts estimate as many as 60% of commercial real-estate loans that came due were extended during this period.

Now the extension periods have been coming to an end. Borrowers are again confronting big balloon payments they can't make and banks are more willing to foreclose. They're better capitalized than they've been in years and "are in a position to take a hit" on a loan, says Dan Fasulo, managing director of Real Capital Analytics Inc., a commercial real-estate research firm in New York.

Bud Matthews of Chapel Hill, N.C., faced a five-year balloon payment of $240,000 in 2006 and had no trouble refinancing with SunTrust Banks Inc. But when the second balloon payment came due in June 2011, he couldn't find a bank to refinance the roughly $200,000 still outstanding. "We went to other banks, and they'd say, 'You are a good bet, but not good enough,' " says the 61-year-old, who occupies a commercial office for his 15-person construction and appliance-repair firm.

After much negotiating, the bank extended the loan through August 2012, but when it came due, Mr. Matthews couldn't pay the outstanding amount in full. The bank didn't want to refinance and started the foreclosure process.

Days before Mr. Matthews's property was scheduled to be auctioned in February, Harrington Bank, a local lender, agreed to assume the loan.

Hugh Suhr, vice president of corporate communications for SunTrust, says, "While we are not at liberty to discuss specific client relationships, we make every effort to work with clients on a case-by-case basis &in order to reach an outcome that best addresses the needs of all involved parties."

Given the number of loans coming due this year, and banks taking a critical eye to loan applications, it seems the problems will drag out for a number of years. As of mid-2012, about one in three loans coming due through 2016 was under water, meaning the property is worth less than what's owed, according to one estimate from Trepp.

"We can look out three to four years and know with certainty that a super amount of debt will come due that can't be dealt with in an ordinary way," says Phil Jemmett, chief executive at Breakwater Equity Partners, a San Diego consultancy and investment firm that specializes in troubled commercial real estate. "Roughly 20,000 small commercial properties &are facing distress in the next 12 months. We expect similar numbers in the next three years, and then it will taper off after 2016."

The incoming debt could trigger a "wave of foreclosures," says Tom Watson, CEO of JPB Commercial Real Estate Advisors LLC, a brokerage and real-estate consulting firm in Miramar Beach, Fla. The size of that wave, he says, will depend on whether the economy continues to improve. If it does, banks will steadily agree to refinance loans and offset the number of foreclosures that would have otherwise transpired.

The Hardest Hit

To be sure, commercial real-estate analysts largely agree that the commercial real-estate market won't experience a full-on collapse. One January report found that 60% of $399 billion in troubled commercial real-estate assets tracked since 2008 have been bought by a third-party investor or refinanced with a lender. But the report, from Real Capital Analytics, evaluates properties valued at greater than $2.5 million. The smallest businesses may have the longest road to normalcy. Small properties "are much less attractive to the banks, especially larger banks," says Matthew Anderson, managing director at Trepp.

In January 2008, the Spiliadis family, Stelios, Pauline and their son, Dimitris, received a $5.06 million loan from 1st Mariner Bank to build the Inn at the Black Olive. The property narrowly escaped a foreclosure auction late last month when the business filed for bankruptcy a legal maneuver that prevented the auction from going forward.

The Black Olive, which touts environmentally friendly features including solar panels, geothermal wells and recycled wood furniture, was under construction during the depths of the recession. After construction, the elder Mr. Spiliadis, 74, applied for the loan to roll into a 20-year mortgage with balloon payments every three years.

But the inn, which opened in March 2011, wasn't a good bet for the bank. The property value had plummeted, and cash flow was tight. Mr. Spiliadis asked the state of Maryland to provide a $1.5 million loan guarantee, but it never materialized. 1st Mariner, which declined to comment, didn't refinance the debt and Mr. Spiliadis stopped making monthly interest payments.

Now in Chapter 7, the business is bringing in enough sales to sustain operations, says Mr. Spiliadis, but the family is not in the clear they have until June to find an investor. If no investor steps up, the business will likely be on the auction block once again.

Ms. Maltby is a small-business reporter in The Wall Street Journal's New York bureau. She can be reached at "mailto:emily.maltby@wsj.com".

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,856 words
29 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
32
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Maximum 'Office' Effort

One consolation for Microsoft after a record slide for shipments of personal computers? At least it is making a little headway in tablets.

But that progress comes at the cost of lost sales for its biggest and most profitable software product: Office.

In the first quarter, 7% of tablets sold globally ran a version of the Windows operating system, according to Strategy Analytics. That remains a distant third to the iPad (48% first-quarter market share) and Android tablets (43%). But considering that a year ago Microsoft had zero share, it counts as decent progress.

Windows is a big contributor to Microsoft's top and bottom lines, naturally. It is responsible for about a quarter of revenue and a third of operating income. So the 14% year-on-year decline in PC shipments in the first quarter, a record according to IDC, wasn't good news for Windows' future. To protect it from the PC slump, Microsoft needs to get Windows onto fast-growing mobile devices. And its strategy to do that, at least in tablets, is novel. Instead of just aping the iPad, as so many Android-based tablet rivals are doing, it is promoting a new line of "convertible" devices combining the features of tablets and laptops.

The idea is to convince buyers that Windows-based tablets can do more than rival devices and, consequently, that they can save money buying one product instead of a laptop and tablet. That sales pitch revolves around a key feature of Windows tablets: They are the only ones that run a full version of Office.

Particularly popular among business users, the Office suite includes Word, Excel, Power Point and other productivity apps. And it is a far bigger business for Microsoft than even Windows. Microsoft's Business Division, of which Office is the primary component, generates around 30% of sales and nearly half of operating income. Office is so popular, Morgan Stanley has estimated that even 30% to 40% of Mac users pay for it.

So the fact that Office isn't available for iPads and Android tablets, the most popular devices in the fastest-growing segment of the computer market, means Microsoft is leaving money on the table.

Take the iPad alone. Analyst Brian Marshall of ISI Group expects Apple to sell 88 million of them this year. If 35% of those iPad buyers also purchased Office, and Microsoft received about $40 per copy of the software, that would translate to about $1.2 billion in additional sales. Add potential customers from the existing pool of iPad owners, plus all of those with Android tablets, and the figure could well be higher.

Granted, this amount would boost earnings only 3% or so. But an added benefit would be to nip in the bud those mobile rivals training a new generation of device users to rely on Office alternatives such as Google's Google Docs, applications from Evernote, and Apple's iWork apps.

Gaining share for Windows in mobile is the right priority. And early market-share data suggest the present strategy is worth pursuing a while longer. But it is still possible that being able to build Excel spreadsheets won't help Windows-based tablets earn more than nominal market share.

In that case, Microsoft will have to rethink whether it is worth sacrificing its best franchise at the altar of Windows.

-- Rolfe Winkler

Standard Life:

Beware of Vertigo

Don't look down. Standard Life PLC, the U.K. life insurer, saw its shares wobble on Friday on the surprise departure of its finance chief to rival Prudential PLC. It hardly matters -- the stock has risen a whopping 72% in the past year, as investors have embraced Standard Life's strategy of shifting away from the capital-intensive business of underwriting insurance risk and toward the gathering of assets into a range of savings products. But the insurer-turned-asset manager's lofty perch leaves it exposed.

True, Standard Life should benefit from two big trends in its home market. The first is new laws requiring employers to automatically enroll staff in a pension plan. The company welcomed some 21,000 employees in the first quarter from its existing pension customers, with opt-out rates lower and contributions higher than expected. The rest of the year looks busy, says management, and the company has boosted capacity so it can process 60,000 new members a day.

The second change is new rules that prevent financial advisers' commissions being bundled into fees for fund- management products. The company stopped paying commissions from 2004 and -- while U.K. retail net inflows were similar to last year's first quarter -- Standard Life is seeing more interest from advisers who previously sought out commissions elsewhere.

The company's ascent has unshackled its valuation from peers. It trades on about 15 times 2014 forecast earnings, well above Legal & General on closer to 10 times and in line or above asset managers like Schroders or Henderson. The company's first-quarter numbers were impressive: New business sales, measured by the present value of premiums, rose 24% from last year. Net inflows of third-party funds into Standard Life's investment arm almost tripled.

But these inflows were underpinned by investors' appetite for the company's multi-asset absolute-return fund, GARS. That leaves the company exposed to a drop in performance of that product.

Standard Life is riding high, but investors could be forgiven for feeling a little queasy.

-- Helen Thomas

Spain's Move May Pressure Bonds

When Spain unilaterally raised its budget deficit targets in March 2012, it marked the start of a long rise in Spanish bond yields. They didn't stop until they were over 7%, forcing European Central Bank President Mario Draghi to pledge to do "whatever it takes" to hold the euro together. Spain has just raised its deficit targets again. Spanish bonds might end up coming under pressure, but a rerun of last year's bond-market rout doesn't seem imminent.

True, some of the circumstances are similar. Last year, as now, hopes for the euro-zone economy were fading fast. The budget target revisions in both years followed a sharp fall in yields, leading to worries that reduced market pressure was allowing governments to backslide on reform. This year, the Spanish revisions are even sharper: the 2013 target is now 6.3% of GDP, up from 4.5%, and the deficit is only expected to fall below 3% in 2016 rather than 2014.

But other factors mitigate against a selloff. Last year, there was also great uncertainty over how Spanish banks would be recapitalized; while concerns remain that last year's bank bailout didn't go far enough, the problem has at least been addressed. In the first half of 2012, foreign investors were also pulling out of Spanish bonds as fears of a euro-zone breakup ran high; the situation has stabilized. Mr. Draghi's pledge is still providing support to the bond market, even if doubts have grown about its implementation. And the new deficit targets, while perhaps still optimistic, are at least closer to reality.

That said, caution on Spain might be warranted given the deterioration in the deficit and growth outlook, and the prospect of higher bond issuance over time. Spain's credit ratings are on the brink of "junk" and the deficit revisions could put them under pressure.

Spanish 10-year bonds Friday yielded 4.27%, a premium of just 0.2 percentage point over Italy. But the latter, especially now that it has formed a government, looks like a more stable credit on many measures. Spain will probably rally along with Italy in the near term as investors welcome political stability. But Spain now looks more vulnerable to a bond market setback.

-- Richard Barley

ProSieben

Still a Star

As RTL

Debuts

Bertelsmann AG wants a Hollywood ending to its affair with the television business. Should investors play a role?

Rather than buy out minority shareholders, the media conglomerate has decided to sell down its 92% stake in Luxembourg-based RTL Group to as little as 75% and invest the cash in expansion elsewhere. RTL, whose assets include a German TV programmer and FremantleMedia, will start trading in Frankfurt on Tuesday.

While European media remain dicey, RTL has advantages that set it apart. First, nearly 60% of RTL's operating income comes from Mediengruppe RTL Deutschland, whose main assets are German broadcasters RTL Television and Vox. Germany is a rare European market where advertising has held up; the division's revenue rose a respectable 3.7% last year.

Another plus: German pay-TV operators recently began paying broadcasters to carry their channels. Previously, broadcasters paid operators for carriage, but high-definition-TV turned the tables as operators became desperate for HD content.

RTL could also capitalize on a shift away from print ads. Some 49% of ad spending in Germany goes to print, compared with 35% for Western Europe, according to ZenithOptimedia. TV accounts for just 22% of advertising in Germany versus 30% in Western Europe. FremantleMedia, while only contributing 13% of RTL's operating profit, could also draw a crowd. There aren't many ways to invest in pure production.

Even so, investors should consider ProSiebenSat.1 Media, Germany's other big broadcaster. While RTL is coping with declining markets like France and the Netherlands, nearly 90% of ProSieben's operating income comes from German-language TV. And ProSieben is ahead of the pack with digital initiatives like maxdome, a video-streaming service.

Analysts expect ProSieben's earnings per share to rise more than 8% annually in the next three years, compared with roughly 5% for RTL. If RTL prices at the bottom of the 54 euros-62 euros per share range, it would trade at 11.5 times consensus 2014 earnings, compared with ProSieben's multiple of 12, adjusting for a coming special dividend. While both are star material, ProSieben looks camera ready.

-- John Jannarone

Overheard

The weather in Chicago this earnings season has been decidedly drizzly. Groupon shareholders may want to make sure they pack an umbrella. Actually it is one Windy City building in particular over which clouds seem to have gathered -- 600 W. Chicago Ave. -- where a number of companies co-founded by Eric Lefkofsky, and in which he is still the largest individual shareholder, all have head offices.

One is InnerWorkings, which in mid-April reduced financial projections after a major customer took most of its business to a rival. Its shares dropped 25% on that news. Another is Echo Global Logistics. On Thursday, it reported first-quarter results that fell short of expectations. Its shares dropped 12% over two days. Groupon also calls that address home. Besides holding a $700 million stake in the online coupon company, Mr. Lefkofsky also assumed the co-chief executive role in February after former head Andrew Mason was fired.

Groupon reports its first-quarter results May 8. Lucky for shareholders, Accuweather.com is forecasting "abundant sunshine."

FT REPORT - RISK MANAGEMENT: PEOPLE
Scandals put spotlight on the human element

By Brian Groom 
1,212 words
29 April 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys RMT1
01
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Getting the measure of people and their potential shortcomings presents one of the big challenges to companies, writes Brian Groom

If a company's success comes down to its people, as business leaders often say, then they also pose some of its biggest risks. People risk is one of the most important - if not the most important - elements of risk management. The question of how to measure and manage that most unpredictable of resources is preoccupying employers worldwide.

The financial crisis has heightened awareness of this, not least through an ever-growing list of scandals such as investment bank JPMorgan's $6bn trading losses incurred in the "London whale" affair, the alleged rigging of the Libor benchmark interest rate and the US investigation into alleged insider trading by employees of the hedge fund SAC Capital.

People risk goes far beyond the financial services sector. Prime examples include the UK's phone hacking scandal, in which journalists' covert gleaning of information from mobile phones led to the closure of Rupert Murdoch's News of the World news-paper, or the lax culture over decades at the BBC that allowed the late disc jockey Jimmy Savile to get away with sexual assaults on children.

It can range from deliberate acts to simple incompetence or poor human resources management; or from rogue traders and fraudsters to inadequate staff training, lax safety procedures and lack of performance management consistency.

"Every organisation that employs people has got to be concerned about people risk," says Carolyn Williams, head of thought leadership at the Institute of Risk Management (IRM), which has members in more than 100 countries. "As organisations get bigger and more complex, the problem multiplies."

Research by SHL, a talent management company, found that one in eight managers and professionals were a high risk to their companies, mainly through poor decision-making and communication. The risk was most acute at middle manager level.

"They have a critical role to play as they are the interface between strategy and operational execution," says Eugene Burke, chief science and analytics officer. On the one hand they have to manage compliance and commitment from frontline staff. On the other, they need to communicate decisions from leaders in a relevant way so frontliners feel motivated and empowered."

SHL found that one in eight frontline staff posed a high risk by not sticking to rules or attending to detail - which could translate into increased error rates and accidents - or by poor commitment and teamwork.

It found the riskiest sectors for all job levels were telecoms and consumer goods. The public sector and retail were the most risk averse. Banking was in the middle, but it is a sector in which a few cases can have a big impact - and it is of systemic importance, so problems there have repercussions across the whole economy.

"If you look at crises that corporates find themselves faced with, it all to a greater or lesser degree starts from some issue relating to people," says Nick Squire, a partner at law firm Freshfields Bruckhaus Deringer.

Questions about individual behaviour seem inextricably linked to a company's culture. Problems can arise if the leadership turns a blind eye to misdemeanours or tacitly encourages them, or if policies and processes are poorly communicated.

There is a global standard for risk management, ISO 31000, providing principles and generic guidelines. "A lot of work has gone into systems and processes and yet still people are not behaving as they are expected to," says Ms Williams at the IRM.

"People are looking for explanations as to why this is - and culture then comes up. Do you have a comprehensive network of policies that just don't get followed? Or, which can be just as bad, do people follow them blindly, without thinking about what they are really doing?"

The purpose of risk management is to allow risks to be taken, not to cut them out completely. Too little risk-taking can be as damaging as too much, which is arguably what happened to Eastman Kodak, the photographic film business. It saw itself as being in the chemicals rather than the image business, Ms Williams says, and failed to make the leap to digital technology.

Steve Saporito, chief executive for the Americas at the global solutions consulting group of Willis, the insurance broker, says he cannot think of any project with a client in which people risk has not been a top 10 issue in some form.

Whether it is failure to attract and retain critical staff, high turnover or poor succession planning in case important personnel leave, he says it is important to dig below the surface. It usually has a deeper cause such as poor accountability, misalignment of incentives with a company's aims or a culture in which employees are not encouraged to question the status quo.

Willis, with other brokers, accountants and others, offers "enterprise risk management", or ERM, a framework for identifying, analysing, responding to and reducing risks. Business has boomed since the crisis, especially since Standard & Poor's, the ratings agency, included evaluation of ERM in its credit rating protocol three years ago.

Some risks can be quantified, such as fraud losses per branch within a bank, stock losses at a retailer or injuries at a mining company. But people risk often defies precise quantification. "You want to get a good understanding of the risk, both at the surface level and below the surface, get some sort of qualitative five-by-five matrix of likelihood and severity to comparatively measure that risk against other risks," Mr Saporito says.

Companies also measure employee engagement to monitor the degree of commitment to the company, or the extent of compliance with ethics policies. Some use psychometric testing to establish employees' risk-taking propensity, which needs to be balanced against safeguards. As companies cut staff, the number of risk managers has in some cases been reduced, so procedures need to be firm.

Steve Culp, managing director of Accenture's risk management practice, says managing people risk is "first and foremost about communication. The policies, structures, the data being tracked are all going to be secondary". He stresses the importance of internal hotlines.

One question since the financial crisis has been why risk management in banking, which has the most complex and rigorous systems, failed. Some say the models were flawed but others believe executives simply overrode advice because profits were so tempting. It certainly seems that systems and processes did not take sufficient account of the human factor.

"The important thing is to recognise there is an issue, which has to come at board level," says Ms Williams. "Companies focus a lot on the management of their assets. People need to be seen as an asset in the same way. You can have the best rules, policies and processes, but that still might not mean that people follow them."

Companies need to show humility in acknowledging problems can happen, she suggests. "People look at an organisational car crash and think 'that couldn't possibly happen here, we're not in that sector or we are not like that' - and then you discover horsemeat in your burgers."

COMPANIES
Slow start forecast for LSE's tech IPO strategy; exchanges

By Henry Mance and Kate Burgess 
478 words
29 April 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
London Ed1
24
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The London Stock Exchange's efforts to lure technology groups is set for a slow start, with some advisers expressing doubts that it will lead to a significant batch of initial public offerings.

The High-Growth Segment , which allows companies to trade on London's main market by floating as little as one-tenth of their shares, has been open for applications for the past month.

Intended to facilitate the path "from start-up to stardom", it has been enthusiastically received by venture capital investors. However, advisers active in the technology sector told the FT that the option of a smaller listing was unlikely to have a significant impact, given that only four technology groups launched IPOs in London last year.

"I rather get a sense of the exchange thrashing around, looking for ideas," said a partner at a City law firm, saying he did not see a long pipeline of groups seeking to list. About six companies have made preliminary enquiries to the LSE since the draft rules were published in February, but none has made clear their intention to list on the segment.

Sam Smith, chief executive of brokerage FinnCap, said: "It is early days and a step in the right direction. However, we have seen very limited enquiries."

The High-Growth Segment is aimed at companies in Europe that might otherwise decide to list on Nasdaq in the US, or sell themselves to larger US companies. About 20 European groups listed in New York in the past three years, according to the LSE.

Companies listing on the segment must float a stake of at least 30m, meaning candidates are likely to have a market capitalisation above 300m.

Established tech start-ups such as Mind Candy would be eligible, but the segment would be out of reach of others such as Rosslyn Analytics, which has announced its intention to list on AIM this year .

One of the advantages of listing on the segment is reduced disclosure and governance requirements. Yet one banker said: "It's going about things in the wrong way. Lowering the rules does nothing but alert and alarm the investors that something of lower-quality is coming their way."

Nonetheless, others support the LSE initiative. "Being able to drip feed a company on to the market is a good way to balance the seller hoping for a high valuation with the buyer worrying about owning an asset with little public track record," said Andrew Griffin, at Monitise, the mobile payments group.

Tim Kay, who heads KPMG's work with London tech start-ups, said he had modest expectations for the initiative. "Anything we can do to support UK plc is a good idea. Is the High-Growth Segment the only answer? Of course it isn't."

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Henderson revamp starts to pay; Fund focus

By Eleanor Lawrie

The new global outlook is yielding results, finds Eleanor Lawrie

Henderson Global Investors ' revamped Global Equity Income fund is showing the green shoots of a performance U-turn, following a significant overhaul a year ago.

The former New Star fund, managed by Andrew Jones and Ben Lofthouse, was taken over by Henderson when it bought New Star in 2009. Three years later, on May 8 last year, Henderson launched a complete revamp of the fund following sustained underperformance.

The changes centred on switching the fund from predominantly focusing on UK equities to a global outlook, and moving it into a global equity income fund peer group.

The overhaul came after five years of lacklustre returns in which the fund ranked 78 out of 79 funds in its former sector (IMA UK Equity Income), according to FE Analytics data correct to the end of April 2012.

Since the mandate change, Global Equity Income has delivered a top-quartile 29.3 per cent gain, compared with a 25.1 per cent average rise from funds in its new IMA Global Equity Income sector, the data provider adds. The fund is currently yielding roughly 3.5 per cent, according to Henderson.

Mr Jones says the UK was "never a bad market", adding that the decision to move to a global focus was due to the "increasing concentration of income" in the UK stock market.

The fund is currently 22 per cent weighted to UK equities, compared with a 15 per cent weighting on its benchmark MSCI World index - amounting to a 7 percentage point overweight in its home market.

"Royal Bank of Scotland, BP and Lloyds Banking Group were cutting their dividends, so income fell in the UK, meaning income is concentrated within only a few stocks," Mr Jones says.

The managers now have more than a 40 per cent exposure to the US and they have added marginally to Europe through stocks such as Electrolux, the Swedish appliance manufacturer.

The next step may be a bigger foray into emerging-market stocks, the manager says. The duo are keeping a watchful eye on emerging markets, which they caution still vary widely in terms of their attitude to paying dividends to investors, as opposed to purely focusing on growth.

Mr Jones hails companies in Brazil, Taiwan, Korea and China as having a "quite established" trend of paying out reliable dividends to shareholders.

"Emerging markets have underperformed, and we are having a look to see if there is anything more exciting there," says co-manager Mr Lofthouse.

In China, the fund holds Hong Kong-listed infrastructure company NWS Holdings, which the team suggests is a strong investment in spite of broader market weakness in the world's second-largest economy.

Mr Jones and Mr Lofthouse add that they are also populating their developed-market equity portfolios with companies that derive revenues in emerging economies.

For example, UK-listed Unilever, the consumer goods conglomerate, has benefited from growth in Brazil, the managers say. They also hail US-listed Las Vegas Sands, a global casino operator that paid out a major special dividend at the end of last year thanks in part to its growing business operations in China and Singapore.

Eleanor Lawrie is senior reporter at Investment Adviser

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,273 words
29 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
32
English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns

BOJ Nods Toward Difficulties

A year can be an awfully long time in economics. The Bank of Japan now says that it will try to accomplish in three years what it previously set out to do in two.

Japan's central bank made a splash three weeks ago when it announced its intention to stoke 2% inflation within two years. The goal was to reverse more than a decade of economic malaise in which Japan has often seen prices fall rather than rise.

On Friday, the central bank partly backed off its original plan, saying that it will now aim to meet the inflation target by March 2016 instead of March 2015.

The bank's earlier promise to inject 60 trillion yen to 70 trillion yen ($612 billion to $714 billion) per year into the financial system stands. Even so, the revised timeline seems like a tacit acknowledgment that ending Japan's deflation trap remains a formidable task.

Indeed, data out Friday showed that Japanese consumer prices fell 0.9% in annual terms in March. Prime Minister Shinzo Abe and the central bank have made some steps forward recently, but a fall in prices is a step in the wrong direction. Nor will it necessarily engender confidence among consumers that the central bank can achieve its inflationary aim.

To be fair, monetary policy takes time to filter through the economy. What's more, the central bank is trying to undo 15 years of deflation.

The bank's promise of loose monetary policy has certainly weakened the yen, which has flirted of late with a level of nearly 100 to a dollar, and strengthened Japanese stocks.

Japanese companies like Sony and Canon say they expect higher earnings as exports get a boost on the back of the depreciating currency.

The bigger problem, though, is how monetary easing filters through to household income and consumption. Expanding the size of the labor force or boosting the purchasing power of workers is what would really stoke demand.

But there's much to be done in this respect. The average monthly earnings of Japanese workers actually fell 0.7% in February from a year earlier, CLSA says.

The participation of women and immigrants in Japan's labor force remains too low given the country's aging and shrinking population. It is well below that of European nations and the U.S.

In other words, loose monetary policy can only do so much. Tokyo also has to do more to convince companies to raise wages and add workers.

But this will be tough. UBS analyst Duncan Wooldridge says that even if the labor participation rate for women age 15 to 64 rises by a full percentage point in each of the next two years, achieving sustained 2% inflation is doubtful.

Mr. Abe and the Bank of Japan have given themselves an extra year to meet a difficult inflation target, but without important policy measures another year may not be enough.

-- Abheek Bhattacharya

Samsung Shines as Rivals Fade

Samsung's legal battle with Apple over smartphone patents drags on. But there is no disputing which of them is winning the fight for profits.

Days after Apple reported its first drop in quarterly earnings in 10 years, Samsung on Friday announced record first-quarter net profit of 7.15 trillion won (US$6.4 billion), up 42% year-on-year.

The drawn-out patent dispute between the two consumer-electronics giants may be an overhang. Samsung booked provisions in its mobile division in the first quarter of 2013, but didn't disclose exactly what the provisions are related to or how big they are.

Still, at the rate Samsung is racking up profits -- and given its cash position of $28 billion at the end of the first quarter -- even a severe penalty would hardly make a dent. A California judge recently cut the amount of damages Samsung must pay Apple for patent violations from $1 billion to about $600 million, about 1% of quarterly revenue.

There are other concerns. Demand for televisions and personal computers is slowing. Lingering doubts about the global economy could hurt sales more broadly. Still, Samsung is a dominant player in just about all its business lines, which means it is in a relatively strong position compared with many rivals.

Despite the success, Samsung shares trade at about seven times estimates of next year's earnings. That looks cheap. The 10-year average is 10.4 times forecast earnings, Bernstein Research says.

Some investors might worry Samsung has too many eggs in one smartphone basket -- three-quarters of operating profit is tied up in cellphones and telecom equipment. But to punish Samsung for its success would be churlish.

Samsung benefits from having a wider range of phones than most of its rivals, from cheap to ultra-expensive, in a fast-growing market. In the first quarter of 2013, global smartphone shipments increased 36% from the same period last year, Strategy Analytics says. Samsung shipped 69.4 million units in the quarter, almost twice as many as Apple, to capture a record 33% share of the global market.

Its latest phone, the Galaxy S 4, has hit a bump in the U.S., with some carriers complaining of delays getting the new device to market. Samsung says that is due to strong demand, a plausible excuse given recent sales growth. The Korean company only has to maintain momentum for its star to rise even higher.

-- Duncan Mavin

For ADM and GrainCorp,

It's Love at Third Sight

Archer Daniels Midland and GrainCorp should now be able to walk down the aisle with heads held high.

ADM has been courting GrainCorp for a while and was twice spurned last year. But an improved bid of three billion Australian dollars (US$3.1 billion) now seems likely to tip the scales, nudging up the offer price just enough to gain the support of GrainCorp's board. Even after upping the bid, ADM expects the deal to boost earnings in the first year following acquisition.

The base for ADM's offer remains A$12.20 cash a share -- the same price that was rejected by GrainCorp in December -- but ADM is throwing in a A$1 dividend per share. Australians among GrainCorp's shareholders can benefit from tax rules that mean the dividends are worth about 43 cents more a share.

The offer marks a 49% premium to where GrainCorp's shares were trading in October, just before ADM made its first offer. Macquarie says the bid implies a valuation of 9.5 times the average of earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortization over the past four years. That is more expensive than recent deals, such as Glencore's acquisition of Canada's Viterra and GrainCorp's acquisition of Integro Foods and Gardner Smith Group to build its edible-oils business.

The terms of the agreement prevent GrainCorp from shopping for rival bids. If an unsolicited counteroffer shows up, ADM has two days to match it. Other potential bidders would have to factor in that ADM already has a stake of almost 20%.

GrainCorp's board can proudly say it held out for more. ADM, too, can point to a dogged pursuit that lands it Australia's largest independent grain handler and a dominant position in grain ports along the east coast, with routes to fast-growing markets in Asia. That is about as happy a start to a marriage as either could hope for.

-- Robb M. Stewart

BoCom Earnings Surprise on Upside

473 words
26 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia

English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns

SHANGHAI -- Bank of Communications Co. kicked off the first-quarter reporting season for China's major banks, and its stronger-than-expected profit growth brought a sigh of relief in the sector.

Profit growth at Chinese banks is expected to continue to slow as Chinese lenders grapple with a slowing economy and interest-rate deregulation, but analysts said resilient net-interest margins will help buoy lenders' net income in the coming quarters.

China's fifth-largest bank by assets, also known as BoCom, said Thursday its first-quarter net profit rose 12% to 17.71 billion yuan ($2.87 billion) from 15.88 billion yuan a year earlier, thanks to healthy net-interest income and higher commissions.

The result was better than the average 16.33 billion yuan net-profit forecast among five analysts polled by Thomson Analytics. Still, it was down from the 20% increase in earnings in the year-earlier period.

Net interest income in the three months ended March 31 rose 11% to 32.03 billion yuan from 28.81 billion yuan a year earlier. Analysts said China's major banks have been boosting their lending profitability by making loans to retail and small and medium-size enterprises instead of big state-owned companies, which typically pay lower rates.

Net fee and commission income from credit cards and sales of wealth-management products rose 24% from a year earlier to 6.93 billion yuan, from 5.60 billion yuan.

BoCom's net-interest margin fell to 2.57% in the first quarter from 2.63% a year earlier, which analysts attributed to a reduction in residential mortgage rates.

But analysts said a recovery in borrowing demand will help improve loan pricing and support Chinese lenders' net-interest margins.

The lender's nonperforming-loan ratio rose to 0.97% at the end of the first quarter from 0.92% at the end of last year.

"A slight deterioration in nonperforming loans in the first quarter isn't systemic, and we're confident that the bank's bad loan ratio will be capped at 1% this year," BoCom Executive Vice President Qian Wenhui said at a press briefing after the lender's first-quarter results.

Mr. Qian attributed the decline in loan quality to small corporate customers in eastern China's Jiangsu and Zhejiang provinces. The two provinces are known as hubs for small and medium enterprises and exporters, which are likely to fare poorly given a slowing economy.

Contrary to analysts' expectations, Mr. Qian said he doesn't expect HSBC Holdings PLC to sell or reduce its 19% stake in the Chinese bank, because the London-based bank remains interested in having exposure to emerging markets and China's growth.

-- Yue Li

In the Wired World, Feeds Drive Trading

By Geoffrey Rogow and Telis Demos 
1,021 words
25 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Corrections & Amplifications

Ted Bailey says his trading-analytics company, Dataminr, is making headway with trading clients in using technology to scan news streams and make rapid buy or sell decisions. In a Money & Investing article on Thursday about such technology, he was quoted saying that his firm was "making a lot of headwind" with clients.

(WSJ April 26, 2013)

Subscribe to WSJ: "http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires"

(END)

When the words "explosions" and "White House" popped up Tuesday on a verified Associated Press Twitter account, computers in the bare-bones offices of a New York City startup sprang into action.

The computers, housed in the Madison Avenue headquarters of a company called Dataminr, sent alerts to clients like hedge funds and government agencies about the apparent news. Shortly thereafter, the Dow Jones Industrial Average tumbled 145 points in seconds.

But the machines weren't fooled: Before the AP announced that the tweet was a hoax -- its account had been hacked -- Dataminr's computers generated a fresh alert saying the news may not be legitimate.

Moments later, shares rebounded across the board, and the blue-chip index ended the day with gains greater than before the incident.

Tuesday's tumult is shining a light on the investment world's use of sophisticated computers programmed to scan news streams -- including those from Twitter and other social media -- to make buy or sell decisions in fractions of an instant.

The technology has been around for several years, but it is increasingly finding adherents among financial firms, including hedge funds, investment banks and even long-term investors such as mutual-fund companies. Experts say traders use the data for just about every kind of investment around the world, from U.S. stocks to the Korean won.

To some on Wall Street, the Securities and Exchange Commission's recent decision to allow companies to release material information via Twitter had "legitimized" the strategy, said Ted Bailey, chief executive and founder of four-year-old Dataminr. The company is lately "making a lot of headwind" with large hedge funds and government clients, he said.

The roots of these computer programs go back to those built by media companies and hedge funds to trade around publicly disclosed information, such as earnings and economic data, say traders and programmers.

Media firms including Dow Jones, Bloomberg and Reuters began setting up "elementized news feeds" around 2006. With the feeds, this public information was piped nearly instantaneously to data centers and sold to trading firms. Dow Jones publishes The Wall Street Journal.

These trading firms would then devise computer programs to buy and sell based on an earnings report being up or down, or a firm being named in a lawsuit.

Some of the new programs are rather simple, looking for key words in verified accounts of major news sources. Others use complex algorithms to detect patterns. Dataminr's program works by processing hundreds of millions of daily tweets instantly to find credible and actionable information that they can share with clients.

Dataminr looks at dozens of variables about each tweet, such as the influence of the user, the geolocation of where the tweet is sent from, and how tweets are clustered together.

In 2010, Johan Bollen, an associate professor at Indiana University, and two other professors published research showing how using Twitter could with more than 85% accuracy predict the daily up and down readings of the Dow.

Some investors that use news-scraping programs draw the line at Twitter, however. "The accuracy of tweets is a major concern," said Zeid Barakat, co-founder of Flyberry Capital, a startup that uses high-speed strategies to trade commodity futures around news events.

Credit Suisse AG doesn't use these sorts of algorithms because the technology is still developing, said Dan Mathisson, the head of U.S. equity trading.

Tuesday's incident "doesn't happen often enough to make it worthwhile" to invest in such technology, he said.

Others say being first is a critical advantage. Selerity Inc., based in Jersey City, N.J., detected an August 2012 tweet from a person who witnessed a fire at a Chevron Corp. refinery in Richmond, Calif., near San Francisco, news that hadn't reached the broader media, according to the firm's founder, Ryan Terpstra. The tweet read "oh [expletive] the chevron refinery in richmond [is] on fire!!!"

Selerity alerted clients, who were able to buy gasoline futures contracts before the news spread widely and caused prices to soar, says Mr. Terpstra.

In the case of Tuesday's commotion, Dataminr's computers not only flagged the event but the algorithms noticed quickly that there was no corroboration. Unlike the Boston Marathon bombing a week earlier, there were none of the telltale signs of a disaster: no sudden swell of tweets from people in the vicinity of the White House, for instance, Mr. Bailey said.

The company alerted customers to another tweet, from a reporter at the White House, saying the report appeared to be a false alarm. The AP and White House quickly issued official denials of the explosions shortly thereafter.

One of the first firms to embrace the small but growing industry being created around social media and investing was New York-based RavenPack.

The business, begun in 2007, now has dozens of hedge funds, mutual funds and banks as clients, all of which have embraced social media more aggressively in the past year, said Armando Gonzalez, chief executive of the firm. The company today counts several institutional investors with more than $5 billion in assets under management as clients of its social-media products.

When Tuesday's minicrash happened, Mr. Gonzalez said he was inundated with questions from clients about how the incident could happen. Most wanted to be sure that RavenPack was using information only from trusted sources, which he said they had. Mr. Gonzalez said he finished answering questions from clients at 2 a.m.

---

Scott Patterson, Chris Dieterich and Liz Moyer contributed to this article.

Lombard: Pensions stars align to drag auditors into new orbits

By Jonathan Guthrie 
768 words
25 April 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

In cosmic terms, the 25 years that European lawmakers think it should take for a company to rotate its auditor is but a blink of an eye. After all, 165 years pass while Neptune rotates once around the sun. But a quarter-century is a long time in business. A watering-down of the European Commission's plan for companies to enlist fresh abacus rattlers every six to 12 years shows just how good at lobbying accountants are.

Their job has been made easier by a lack of serious opposition. The brain-shrinking boringness of audit regulation has made debates low-key compared with those concerning executive remuneration.

That should change in the UK thanks to the decision of two pensions titans to "draw a line in the sand". The 38bn Universities Superannuation Scheme and RPMI Railpen, which manages 18bn, say they will vote against the reappointment of any audit firm whose tenure at a FTSE 350 company exceeds 15 years. Their preference is for a change every seven to eight years.

The average FTSE 100 company waits for Jupiter to orbit the sun four times (about 48 years) before it replaces its auditor. So USS and RPMI Railpen are set to cast a lot of protest votes.

Good. This will remind accountants that while they are hired by company audit committees, their real employers are the shareholders.

There are sound arguments for regular auditor rotation. Incomers are better at spotting anomalies than incumbents blinded by over-familiarity. Accountants who can count on reappointment will naturally seek to lift revenues by cross-selling other services, such as management consultancy. And that can compromise their objectivity.

The auditors who signed off the accounts of tottering banks as "true and fair" have enjoyed a remarkably easy ride since the financial crisis. USS and RPMI Railpen deserve support from other investors in making it bumpier.

It is 60 years since James Watson and Francis Crick published their model of the structure of DNA in the journal Nature, writesAndrew Hill. No better time, then, to eradicate the use of the expression by smug business leaders, as in: "It's in our DNA" or "It's in the company's DNA".

The likely origin is Gareth Morgan's 1986 book Images of Organization, which laid out eight metaphors through which people think about organisations, including as a machine, as a political system and as an organism. Fair enough, but the use and abuse of the term has bloomed in recent years. Delve into the archive and you will find 550 instances of the two sentences above in the past year alone.

Why is this bad? Apart from the laziness of using just one metaphor - which may lead to groupthink - one suspects all 550 of the past year's examples were positive: sustainability is "in our DNA", customer service is "part of our DNA".

Yet it is the embedded nature of negative corporate traits, from inefficiency to outright corruption, that is the real risk for organisations. To say something is in your DNA suggests it cannot be easily changed, whereas corporate culture has to be constantly under review. Time for some new metaphors and fresh thinking.

Despite its name, WANdisco is not a nightclub for goths, but a UK-based tech success. Shares in the Aim company, whose products help people handle data overload or work on shared files, have quadrupled since last June's float.

The shares levitated another 3 per cent on Thursday, thanks to a doubling in cash bookings to $3m in the first quarter. WANdisco also announced it had made its first sale of products for managing "big data" - the slew of information generated by proliferating IT systems.

The Silicon Valley-spawned venture does not say who the customer is or how much they will pay. Which begs the question: why make an announcement at all?

The danger facing lossmaking start-ups is that they become slaves to the need to produce "newsflow". Lacking the proof of concept that profits or a lucrative buyout may supply later, investors clamour for heartening press releases instead.

WANdisco should beware of fuelling unsustainable optimism. Tech dunces dazzled by the sparkle of the group's story should breathe into a paper bag, meanwhile. The shares, trading on a 49-times multiple of enterprise value to sales, look expensive compared with established businesses, such as Arm Holdings at 21.5 times. To paraphrase ukulele-playing sage Warren Buffett: never invest in a company you flatter yourself you understand but probably don't.

Website revenue optimization co Dynamic Yield raises $2m; Dynamic Yield plans to use the proceeds to expand its global footprint by opening a New York City office and to support the growth of its expanding customer base.

Globes' correspondent

24 April 2013
Israel Business Arena
AIWIBA
English
 2013, Copyright of Globes Publisher Itonut (1983) Ltd. 

Website revenue optimization company Dynamic Yield has raised $2 million in Series A financing led by Bessemer Venture Partners. The New York Times Company and Innovation Endeavors, the investment fund of former Google CEO Eric Schmidt, also participated in the round.

Dynamic Yield was founded in 2012 by CEO Liad Agmon and CTO Omri Mendellevich. Based in Tel Aviv, Dynamic Yield develops real-time audience personalization software that increases revenue yield and key engagement metrics for online publishers and e-commerce websites. The start up has been self-funded to date.

Dynamic Yield plans to use the proceeds to expand its global footprint by opening a New York City office and to support the growth of its expanding customer base.

Dynamic Yield's software can help publishers and e-commerce sites more effectively monetize their visiting user traffic. Dynamic Yield provides website owners with an automated SaaS solution that measures the revenue impact of each page layout and individual in-page components. Real-time personalization algorithms can then be directed to increase overall revenue yield by increasing user engagement, ad clicks, product purchases, social sharing and page-views.

Dynamic Yield also provides a comprehensive audience module that helps website owners to understand who are their most valuable users, why they are valuable and how to increase reach, engagement and revenues from existing and new traffic channels. With an understanding that publishers lack the time and resources to go through complex integration projects, Dynamic Yield has focused on making the product easy to deploy and manage.

Dynamic Yield is already powering nearly a billion monthly page-views.

Agmon said, "We supposedly live in an era of dynamic, personalized web experiences, yet data loads and algorithmic capacity have kept the vast majority of websites largely static. We have shown in the past 12 months that our low-touch SaaS solution has significant and far reaching effect on our clients' bottom-lines."

Bessemer's Adam Fisher, who sits on Dynamic Yield's board, said, "There are a lot of tools and services in the market to optimize discreet components of a website, but nothing that takes a holistic view of how to maximize revenue across revenue sources and yet improve user engagement and satisfaction at the same time. With its big data approach, Dynamic Yield helps website owners achieve this difficult balance, while still allowing for editorial control."
