Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,193 words
22 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
32
English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns

Apple Struggles With Chinese Checkers

Despite being seemingly invincible, even Apple may struggle to climb the Great Firewall of China.

China is Apple's main growth market. In the quarter ended in December, China accounted for 13% of Apple's revenue, and Chief Executive Tim Cook has said he expects it will become Apple's largest market. Shareholders anxiously await a deal for China Mobile, the world's largest wireless carrier by subscribers, to officially carry the iPhone.

Yet a string of criticism and proposed regulatory curbs suggest Apple's trajectory in China may deviate from the course many expect.

The latest came in Wednesday's edition of the People's Daily. An article included Apple in a list of websites and app stores that have been investigated for providing pornographic content in China. This comes a month after Apple was attacked by China Central Television over its iPhone warranties, prompting an apology from Mr. Cook.

Then there is a proposed law that could give the Chinese government more control over apps and put new standards in place that may force companies to help the government track users.

Why does Apple suddenly find itself in the cross hairs? The proposed law suggests the Chinese government is wary of the growing power of a company whose smartphone "ecosystem" Beijing doesn't control.

This would fit a pattern. Facebook is a "walled garden" of content that is blocked to Chinese Web surfers entirely. Twitter is, too. And then there is Google which, in effect, kicked itself out of China's mainland search market after refusing censorship demands.

Ironically, Google's Android mobile operating system dominates the smartphone market in China, despite the company's strained relationship with the government. But those devices don't come with Google services that are standard elsewhere, such as YouTube, search, and Google Maps. Also, without access to the Google Play store, Chinese users have a weaker selection of apps to choose from.

Apple is famous for the control it exerts over so many aspects of its devices, including the apps available to users. This is a big part of the iPhone's appeal: Apps just work and are largely free of bugs or malicious code. Mr. Cook will be in a tough spot if, for instance, Beijing requires Apple to alter its services in a way that lends itself more easily to government control.

Not that China is exclusively focused on Apple. A Chinese government research institute in March said that the country is too reliant on Android and that local manufacturers should have more control over it.

One issue Beijing must deal with, though, is that iPhones and Android devices are popular for a reason. And Chinese consumers will grow more attached to them over time, especially as there are few good alternatives today. Curbing Chinese consumers' access to such technology would likely cause grumbling across the country, something the government has historically been keen to avoid.

Still, any adversity for Apple and Google in China could spell opportunity for local smartphone makers like Lenovo and Huawei Technologies. Together, these two had about 26% of China's smartphone market in the fourth quarter of 2012, estimates Strategy Analytics.

Foreigners Samsung and Apple had 16.3% and 9.7%, respectively. Hewlett-Packard offers a cautionary tale here. Its share of China's personal-computer market collapsed after it came under fire from state media in 2010 over product quality. Lenovo's market share increased sharply.

Apple's reputation, and huge valuation, rests on its ability to create new markets that it then dominates. China won't necessarily fit that model.

-- Rolfe Winkler

Kiwi Is Already Flying High

Middle Earth is getting pricey. New Zealand may be well known as the setting for the Lord of the Rings movies. But its currency has been the recent scene-stealer, strengthening to a near 20-month high against the U.S. dollar.

In the past month, the kiwi is up about 3% against the greenback. Anticipation of an interest-rate increase is part of the reason. Earlier this month, a central bank official said rising house prices might lead to a monetary-policy response. The official cash rate stands at 2.5%, where it has been since March 2011. Central bank officials meet this week.

New Zealand is also about to see a wave of significant share sales -- including the privatization of three state-owned utilities -- that could raise as much as seven billion New Zealand dollars ($5.89 billion). Some foreign investors may have been buying the kiwi ahead of the listings.

But there is another reason for the currency's strength. New Zealand is on the verge of being included in Citigroup's World Government Bond Index, having crossed the threshold for market size in outstanding government-bond issuance earlier this month. A NZ$2 billion bond took the eligible outstanding bond issuance to almost NZ$63 billion -- above a US$50 billion threshold.

Inclusion in the index should boost the investor base for kiwi debt, with portfolio managers adjusting their weightings to send more funds down under. The problem, though, is that some investors may be trying to get in ahead of the word from Citigroup. That could mean there is little upside left on the table once the kiwi is officially part of the index.

Royal Bank of Canada estimates that 50% to 60% of kiwi bond inflows related to potential portfolio adjustments have already occurred. In two other recent cases, the currencies of South Africa and Mexico strengthened ahead of inclusion in the index and for a short while after, but that was followed by significant underperformance, RBC notes.

Ahead last week's Group of 20 gathering in Washington, International Monetary Fund Deputy Managing Director Min Zhu said the kiwi is about 10% overvalued. The currency actually fell almost 1% last week. This kiwi may already have flown too high.

-- Robb M. Stewart

Overheard

There is no way of falling off a cycle gracefully, but that won't stop Wall Street from trying.

The past week or so has brought a clutch of reports from analysts signaling the end is nigh for the so-called commodities supercycle, with at least two on Friday alone. It comes amid a selloff in hard assets of all descriptions, with gold's slide most prominent.

Given efforts the past decade to promote commodities as an investment for the masses, via such novelties as metal-backed exchange traded funds, don't expect a quick funeral.

Citigroup, which has been calling a turn in oil markets, released a report called "From Commodities Supercycle to Unicycles."

Barclays followed with a tome on commodities subtitled "From an age of shortage to an era of enough?" The key theme there is that investment in new capacity is finally leading to more supply, loosening markets and prices. But Deutsche Bank stuck closest to the true spirit of the Street with its report: "Trading the Commodity Underperformance Cycle."

The cycle is dead; long live the cycle!

The Experts: What's the Most Valuable Thing Washington Could Do for Investors in Funds and ETFs?

2,055 words
21 April 2013
21:58
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

What's the most valuable thing Washington could do for investors in funds and ETFs? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887323869604578370350748580878.html" that discussed ways Washington officials could benefit individuals buying mutual funds and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Thursday, April 11.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Investing in Funds & ETFs Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts online at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html?mg=inert-wsj"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/14CDLEj" three of The Experts Christian Magoon ( "https://twitter.com/ChristianMagoon") of Magoon Capital, Rick Ferri ( "https://twitter.com/Rick_Ferri") of Portfolio Solutions and Matt Hougan ( "https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") of IndexUniverse speak about investing in index funds and ETFs in a live video filmed on Monday, April 8.

John Rogers : Roll Out the Rest of Dodd-Frank

Washington needs to implement the important investor-protection agenda contained in the Dodd-Frank Act since, so far, only one-third of the Act has been rolled out. All advisers, brokers and insurance agents who provide personalized investment advice must be held to a duty of loyalty, prudence and care for their clients. This is not the case today, and most investors don't realize it. Most people own mutual funds or ETFs in accounts advised or managed by a financial intermediary. The choice of which fund to use should be based solely on your needs, not on those of anyone else.

John Rogers is the president and chief executive of the CFA Institute.

Tom Brakke: Tax Simplification Would Help

I suppose if I had to pick one thing, it would be tax simplification (and not just for the parts of the code related to investments).

Some other thoughts regarding what "Washington could do":

It depends on which funds or ETFs you own as to which specific policy changes would do you the most good.

The Federal Reserve has been punishing savers in favor of speculative activity. The "most valuable thing" may already have been done on behalf of those in risk assets.

When it comes to legislative and regulatory proposals, investors should not assume that their best interests will be represented by investment firms any more than the buyers of food should assume that their best interests will be represented by food companies.

Tom Brakke ( "https://twitter.com/researchpuzzler") is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

Rick Ferri: Don't Impose a Financial Transaction Tax

It's not what Washington could do; it's what they shouldn't do. Washington shouldn't impose a financial transaction tax (FTT) on the purchase and sale of securities, such as stocks, bonds, mutual funds and exchange-traded funds (ETFs). The proposed tax between 0.03% and 0.5% would be applied to the market value of the securities in a transaction. This tax would harm individual investors who are saving money in mutual funds and ETFs to meet retirement, education and other financial goals.

Rick Ferri is founder of "http://www.portfoliosolutions.com/" LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Charles Rotblut : Put Institutional and Individual Investors on the Same Footing

If I were to ask members of the American Association of Individual Investors what Washington politicians could do for them, the majority would say fix the budget problem, get serious about paying down the debt and do something about the economy. Regulatory issues involving mutual funds and ETFs would be far down their list of priorities.

Regulators do need to ensure the playing field between institutional and individual investors is level, however. Products and providers need to be overseen and mischief needs to be punished. Expenses should be kept in line as well.

Though I do favor more disclosure than less, it is possible to overwhelm individuals with too much detail. Efforts to make prospectuses clearer with important information about fees, rates of returns, investment objectives and strategies are always welcome.

Charles Rotblut ( "https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors .

Scott Adams: Stop Giving Money to Strangers

This might be a trick question but I'm going to go with the obvious answer: I think investors would like Washington to stop taking their money and giving it to strangers.

Scott Adams is the creator of the Dilbert comic strip that appears in thousands of newspapers world-wide and "http://www.dilbert.com/".

Sheryl Garrett : Make Advisers Put Their Clients' Interests First

Fully empower the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) to implement and enforce "http://www.thefiduciarystandard.org/" for all those who render professional investment advice to retail investors! If all financial advisers are required by law to put their clients' interests ahead of their own always that would be the most valuable thing Washington could do for investors.

I recognize that many investors do not work through financial advisers; however, the financial advisory industry has a significant impact on workplace investment options, as well as personal decisions and investments that people make every day. Changing the culture and regulatory structure to mandate that the investor's interests must always come first, and the adviser is the party responsible for proving they did right by the client, would drastically improve investors' comfort in the markets, with investing and with advisers.

Sheryl Garrett ( "https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") is founder of the Garrett Planning Network Inc.

Frank Holmes: Thoughtful and Moderate Referees

I often compare government regulation to referees at sporting events. All sports require officials to make sure the participants play by the rules. Fans love watching March Madness, the World Series and the Super Bowl, as they trust they are following a fair competition. Imagine these sports with more complicated rules, more referees than players and a controlled outcome.

Washington has good intentions, as regulators are watching out for the investor. However, excess and complicated regulation can dilute the efforts of innovators and game-changers, which can lead to lower profitability and fewer jobs created.

Americans need thoughtful and moderate regulation so that initiative and achievement is appreciated, companies are competitive and investing is fair and rewarded.

Frank Holmes is chief executive and chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors Inc .

Greg McBride : Allow Deferral of Taxes on Reinvested Dividends

Given the federal deficits and the hot-button issue of taxes, it'll likely never happen, but providing investors the option of deferring taxes on reinvested dividends and capital gains in a taxable account would encourage more capital investment and avoid taxes on phantom income.

Greg McBride ( "https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Matt Hougan: Enforce a Fiduciary Duty

First, force brokers to have a fiduciary duty to their clients. The market is going that way anyway, and (by definition) it would be what's best for investors. Finances are too important to do any other way.

Second, require the clear disclosure of fees. We're making progress in the 401(k) space, but sterling clarity should be the goal across all financial products and services.

Matt Hougan ( "https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

George Papadopoulos: Adopt the Fiduciary Standard and Get Rid of the Alphabet Soup

I think the SEC should adopt the fiduciary standard of care for all persons dispensing financial advice, who must place their clients' best interests above their own at all times. The current suitability standard is not strong enough because it allows advisers to recommend what is "suitable" for an investor. Just yesterday I met with a prospect whose adviser had sold him several variable annuities, all stuffed in his Individual Retirement Account. It made my blood pressure reach a dangerously high level! If the adviser had been a fiduciary he or she would have thought twice about making such an awful recommendation.

If the SEC does not do the right thing, I could settle with reform to the overly confusing world of retirement plans. The alphabet soup has reached absurd levels. These are just some: 401(k), Individual 401(k), Roth 401(k), 403(b), 457, SARSEP, Regular IRA, Rollover IRA, Roth IRA, SEP IRA, Simple IRA, Keogh, Money Purchase, Profit Sharing, Defined Benefit Plan and Coverdell Education Savings Accounts (Education IRAs). There is a lot of room available to consolidate these accounts. At the very least, we should stop coming up with new ones to add to the mix!

George Papadopoulos ( "https://twitter.com/feeonlyplanner") is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families.

Eleanor Blayney : Ensure Investors Are Protected

First of all, bipartisan action and commitment to getting our fiscal house in order in a fair, balanced way would send an important message to businesses and markets that would be positive for investors. Corporations, in particular, are holding huge amounts of cash as a result of the continuing (and tiresome) uncertainty created by congressional gridlock. Getting this cash working in capital projects and more hires would increase overall economic growth, and thus rates of investment returns.

Second, we support extending the fiduciary standard (which SEC-registered investment advisers have to follow) to broker-dealers who provide retail investment advice. This standard would improve consumer trust and confidence in financial professionals and markets. More confidence and better advice equals better investment results.

In fact, research done by CFP Board (as part of the Financial Planning Coalition) found that 93% of respondents said that they agree with the statement that financial advisers providing advice "should put your interests ahead of theirs and should have to tell you upfront about any conflicts of interest that potentially could influence that advice."

Eighty percent of American investors do not believe the federal government is doing enough to protect "consumers from being taken advantage of" by financial advisers. Also, the survey found that 84% of American investors agree that "financial advisers should be regulated by the federal government to protect investors and build confidence in financial services." In other words, American investors want to ensure they are protected.

Eleanor Blayney ( "https://twitter.com/EleanorBlayney") is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards .

Michelle Perry Higgins: Stay Out of the Way

While I'm sure that many of the changes they make are well intentioned, some of them are actually harmful to the market and to investors. In my opinion, the best thing they could do is to stay out of the way. Every new law should be examined in the clear light of day to assess its short- and long-term consequences. Similar to the tax code, each adjustment they make produces a corresponding change in behavior. They should also provide some incentives for job creators instead of punishing their success. For example, every time Washington bureaucrats decide to increase the capital-gains and dividend tax rate, they take money out of investors' pockets, and that is definitely not a good thing. I think lawmakers should cut the long-term capital-gains tax rate and dividends back to 15% for top-bracket taxpayers.

Michelle Perry Higgins ( "https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Larry Zimpleman : Give Investors Greater Certainty

The word that comes to mind is "certainty." Provide some certainty around tax policy and a longer-term path toward a much more balanced budget that also gets the size of the outstanding federal debt to a reasonable level in relation to gross domestic product (GDP). We must get away from a legislative environment that causes us to lurch from crisis to crisis, keeping markets and investors on the edge of their seats. Businesses, individuals and investment markets can only make good decisions when there is certainty over the longer term about how the economy will perform and what the level of interest rates will be. We all hope we might get back to that environment soon.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group .

The Experts: When Does Active Management Make Sense?

2,971 words
21 April 2013
21:58
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Under what circumstances should somebody use active money managers as opposed to index funds? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887323869604578368643942370104.html" on a new generation of investments that aren't nearly as passive or as cheap as readers might think and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Monday, April 8.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Investing in Funds & ETFs Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts online at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html?mg=inert-wsj"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/14CDLEj" three of The Experts Christian Magoon ("https://twitter.com/ChristianMagoon") of Magoon Capital, Rick Ferri ("https://twitter.com/Rick_Ferri") of Portfolio Solutions and Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") of IndexUniverse speak about investing in index funds and ETFs in a video chat.

Christian Magoon: Here Are Two Scenarios Favoring Active Managers

Active managers often have an edge in two scenarios. The first occurs in markets that aren't efficient. In general, traditional index funds need certain ingredients in underlying markets in order to best function. These ingredients include minimum levels of liquidity, market capitalization and cost efficiency. When those elements are not ideal, active management is a better choice because it is more flexible when dealing with inefficiencies. Why? Active managers aren't bound by the predetermined set of rules that traditional indexes follow. Thus market segments, including less-liquid fixed-income sectors and certain emerging equity markets, are often attractive candidates for active management.

The second area where active managers can be preferable is when implementing investment strategies that are complex or fast moving. These strategies are difficult to index due to the many contextual variables that can arise. In comparison, active managers are ideally able to make investment decisions quickly and based off countless variables. In comparison, contextualization and speed are not easy elements to "program" into index development.

Christian Magoon ("https://twitter.com/ChristianMagoon"), chief executive of Magoon Capital, provides strategic advice to potential and existing ETF sponsors in areas of product development, marketing and distribution.

George Papadopoulos: In the Long Term, Never

If we are talking about investing over the long term, never! If we are talking about short-term trading, perhaps you can try to identify an active money manager and hope to get lucky. If you go that route, you should try to pick an active fund with lower-than-average investment costs. You can't control investment performance, but you can control investment costs.

George Papadopoulos ("https://twitter.com/feeonlyplanner") is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families.

Larry Zimpleman: Remember It Takes Expert Knowledge to Pick the Right Index Fund, Too

In general, 75% to 80% of the population does not consider themselves investment "experts." While it might sound appropriate to ask if they should use index funds, the reality for this group is that using index funds assumes a person has enough investment expertise to be able to determine which index funds are appropriate for them, and what their overall asset allocation should look like.

The majority of the investing public really isn't in a position to answer that question. The best option for this group is: a) find an investment adviser that you're comfortable with or b) consider the use of "life cycle" funds (either risk-based or time-based) as a way to adopt a multi-asset-class portfolio that provides good diversification and adjusts the portfolio over time as horizons change.

If you consider yourself an investment expert, then the question of index funds vs. managed funds is more appropriate. But, again, this assumes you know what overall asset-allocation approach you want to use. In considering the use of active funds, be sure to look at the investment track record over an entire market cycle not just for one or three years. Also, make sure the size of the fund is stable or growing and that the portfolio managers have been with the fund over that entire time.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

Charles Rotblut: An Argument for Both Active and Index

Though some of my fellow expert panelists may disagree with me, I think there is an argument to be made for combining both active and index funds.

Index funds work best when seeking broad exposure to stocks, particularly large-cap stocks. Given the low cost and the high propensity for active managers to underperform, start with index funds.

A good active manager, however, can take advantage of market anomalies such as value, size or liquidity. Sequoia (SEQUX), which I personally own, is a good example. A manager who adheres to a rational asset-allocation strategy over the long term can also be beneficial. The Permanent Portfolio (PRPFX) and Vanguard Wellington (VWELX) are examples. In AAII's Model Fund portfolio, we combine actively managed mutual funds with index-tracking ETFs to profit from the advantages of both.

The big questions to ask are, what is the active manager bringing to the table, and is his strategy replicable year after year? A strategy that merely involves the manager selecting stocks he thinks are attractive isn't going to help investors. The approach has to exploit market anomalies or otherwise be truly unique, and there has to be long-term performance good enough to justify the higher expenses and risks associated with active management.

If you don't see consistently good long-term performance, stick with index. Considering that the average fund investor doesn't even match the returns of the funds they invest in, simply focusing on mimicking the performance of the broad indexes can offer a huge improvement in portfolio returns.

Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

Scott Adams: It's a Perfect Strategy for My Enemies

I can think of many cases in which I would recommend active money managers over index funds. For example, I might be giving the advice to someone I hate or and this happens a lot someone I expect to hate later. I would also recommend active money managers if I were accepting bribes to do so, if I were an active money manager myself, or if it were April Fools' Day. And let's also consider the possibility that I might be drunk, stupid or forced to say things at gunpoint. I've also heard good things about a German emotion called schadenfreude, so that could be a factor too.

Scott Adams is the creator of the "Dilbert" comic strip that appears in thousands of newspapers world-wide and "http://www.dilbert.com/".

Frank Holmes: Use an Active Manager for Specialized Equity Markets

I believe a portion of an investor's diversified portfolio should hold actively managed funds, especially for specialized equity markets that require specialized knowledge and expertise, as the pricing tends to be inefficient. These areas include emerging markets, small-cap stocks and companies involved in resources and metals mining. The key is in selecting an active manager who has extensive experience investing in these markets, as they understand the seasonal and historical patterns that help them navigate these complex areas.

Frank Holmes is chief executive and chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors Inc.

Sheryl Garrett: Hope Says Active, Reason Says Passive

Although I follow the passive school of investment philosophy for most circumstances, there are times when active management has its place. Unfortunately, I don't know exactly when or if that place will occur. If buying the market is good, wouldn't it be even better to have a group of trained professional sort through and screen out the weakest companies/stocks or issuers/bonds and so on? Optimistic reasoning would tell us yes, but the academic evidence says otherwise.

Some of us don't want to settle for just what the market will bear, but rather hope that human beings (active money managers) have an ability to help mitigate risk and receive a higher return per unit of their risk taken in the less efficient segments of the investment marketplace, such as high-yield municipal bonds, international bonds, small-cap foreign equities and so on. I acknowledge that is wishful thinking and that there have only been a few dozen people in the history of the modern capital markets that really do add Alpha consistently and over time. If we can only find the next Alpha managers! Meanwhile, take a load off and go passive.

Sheryl Garrett ("https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") is founder of the Garrett Planning Network Inc.

Eleanor Blayney: Some Reasons for Active Managers (but Not Many)

If the person I was counseling was herself an active manager, I would definitely advise her to invest in her own fund: It sends a good message to other investors of her conviction and confidence in what she is doing!

For the most part, however, index funds trump actively managed funds when it comes to low information costs (you know what you are getting), low expenses and competitive returns over the long run. A case for active funds might be made for investments in areas that require highly specialized knowledge or expertise, such as a merger-and-acquisition fund or a fund focused on a very narrow segment of the market. Another possible argument for active funds: In a bear market, active managers can react defensively or opportunistically, whereas indexes must stay fully invested in their benchmarks.

Eleanor Blayney ("https://twitter.com/EleanorBlayney") is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards.

Rick Ferri: High-Yield Corporate Bonds Are Better Suited for Active Management

Certain asset classes are better suited for active management than indexing. High-yield corporate bonds are a good example. An unrestrained high-yield bond index includes all bonds outstanding and weighs each security based on the amount issued by a company. This type of index would create problems for investors.

Before General Motors went bankrupt, their bonds were the largest segment of the high-yield bond market. GM paper dwarfed all other issuers. An investor wouldn't have wanted to own an index fund of high-yield bonds when the company went under because they would have lost a bundle.

To reduce single-issuer risk, high-yield-bond-index providers created constrained indexes. The methodology held each company to a few percentage points of an index. It was a managed solution.

The second problem with high yield is liquidity. Many high-yield bonds trade infrequently, and that makes it impossible for an index-fund manager to track the index. To solve this issue, index providers added security screens to eliminate low-volume bonds. It was another managed solution.

There is nothing wrong with using an ETF or index fund that tracks a managed index, except that these products tend to hold fewer bonds and have higher expenses than some actively managed funds. The Vanguard High-Yield Corporate Fund Admiral Shares (VWEAX) holds more bonds and has lower expenses than any high-yield index fund or ETF. In my view, this makes VWEAX a better index fund than existing index funds and ETFs.

Rick Ferri is founder of "http://www.portfoliosolutions.com/" LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Greg McBride: Small-Cap Stocks and Emerging Markets Present Opportunities for Good Active Managers

While I am a big proponent of index investing, investors can increase their odds of adding incremental return to their portfolios by seeking active managers that have a strong track record and consistent investment philosophy, particularly small-cap stocks and emerging markets.

Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Matt Hougan: Active Management Might Work In Bonds

All of the data suggest that this is a terrible idea in nearly every corner of the market. We like to think that managers can have an edge in undiscovered corners of the market small-cap emerging markets, illiquid credit but the data suggest otherwise. Most active managers lose over the long haul, and it's impossible to find the ones that will outperform in advance. Anyone who debates this with you in the equity space is &confused.

In bonds, it's different. Broad-based bond indexes are so overloaded in U.S. government debt right now, and that debt is yielding so little and subject to so much interest-rate risk, that I worry about investors who "own the market" in the bond space. Even John Bogle, the father of index investing, has said that just buying the Barclays U.S. Aggregate Index is not such a good idea these days.

I think considering the services of successful fixed-income managers like Bill Gross or Jeff Gundlach is a legit idea. They've shown the ability to navigate tricky waters and have established remarkable long-term track records. Thanks to their size, they also have a real advantage when it comes to accessing liquidity in the bond market.

Will it work out? I have no idea. But if you can get it for low costs BOND costs 0.55% a year, for instance it's a reasonable idea. (Run away screaming from anyone who wants to charge you 1% for investing.)

[Disclosure: Though I work for IndexUniverse, I own the actively managed BOND in my own portfolio.]

Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

Tom Brakke: Three Points to Consider When Pursuing an Active Strategy

Passive strategies like index funds should be the default approach for most people. They are cheaper, easier and have had better results over time than active managers.

Selecting active managers is difficult, and many of those who would give you advice on which manager to select base that advice primarily on past performance, which isn't a good predictor of future performance. (Those in the business of making the recommendations might say they do much more than that, but ask yourself this: When was the last time they proposed buying a fund with subpar recent performance or selling one that's been very good?)

Which is not to say that active strategies should never have a place in your portfolio. If you use them, do so carefully:

1) Choose managers that are truly active, not the "closet indexers" that are prevalent in the business. (Hint: You should want more "tracking error," not less.)

2) Focus on the least efficient parts of the market, where active managers have the best opportunity to outperform.

3) Discount the narrative you hear about the manager from those recommending the investment and seek out information from other sources.

Active managers offer the possibility of outperformance, while often delivering the reality of underperformance. If you really want to "outperform" your neighbor, save more than they do. It's as close as you'll ever come to a sure thing.

Tom Brakke ("https://twitter.com/researchpuzzler") is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

Michelle Perry Higgins: Active Mangers Can Help You Navigate Lesser-Followed Areas

Although certainly not in the majority, there are several actively managed funds available to investors that have done an outstanding job of outperforming their benchmarks over time. The idea of an investor using funds like this in combination with some core index funds makes great sense. However, with so many funds in the universe today (per Morningstar, 29,432 as of Feb. 28, 2013), it takes a lot of time and effort to screen and identify funds that have past records of exceeding target levels. Even with appropriate due diligence, investors still have to keep in mind that past performance is no guarantee of future results. If I had one suggestion to offer the individual who was going to wade into this pool, it would probably be that they may have more success with active managed funds by going to lesser-followed areas such as small-cap stocks, emerging markets or even the high-yield bonds. In these sectors, there are more opportunities for a skilled manager to find undervalued opportunities as compared to a typical large-cap-fund manager.

Michelle Perry Higgins ("https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Rick Ferri to Frank Holmes and Greg McBride: The Myth of Inefficient Markets

Rick Ferri is responding to "http://on.wsj.com/12AS3kQ" by Frank Holmes and "http://on.wsj.com/12BjF9u" by Greg McBride.

Frank Holmes and Greg McBride advocated investing in actively managed funds that specialize in part of the equity markets that they believe require specialized knowledge and expertise. Their theory is that small-cap and emerging markets are inefficient and that active managers outperform indexing.

This theory doesn't pan out in the numbers. According to a newly compiled study by researchers at Vanguard, a significant majority of actively managed funds in so-called inefficient sectors such small-cap stocks and emerging markets have not delivered on the promise of outperformance, particularly after accounting for the large number of poor-performing funds that closed or merged over the years.

It's a myth that actively managed equity funds have a leg up in markets perceived as inefficient. Low-cost market-matching index funds outperform a majority of active managers in every style and sector and across every continent in the long run.

Rick Ferri is founder of "http://www.portfoliosolutions.com/" LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - HOUSE & HOME
Property of the state

By Jim Pickard 
1,576 words
20 April 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys RES1
01
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

On both sides of the Atlantic, governments that shy away from 'socialist' policies are propping up national housing markets. Jim Pickard asks what impact this has on prices and what happens when the support stops

Socialism is a dirty word in many parts of the US. After all, America is a global symbol of free markets, muscular capitalism and the small state. Yet somehow the government has turned its mortgage market into a giant nationalised enterprise on a par with China's Red Army or Britain's National Health Service.

US mortgage finance vehicle Fannie Mae, created by Franklin D Roosevelt to drag the US out of the Great Depression, underwrote around one in five mortgages during the 1940s. It was seen as the archetype of Keynesian intervention. Yet Roosevelt's efforts have been eclipsed by those made by 21st-century governments around the world to pull their economies out of the post-credit crunch tailspin.

Today, in the US, almost nine out of 10 mortgages issued in the US are subsidised by the state through a bewildering array of state-sponsored groups. They include Freddie Mac, the Department of Veterans' Affairs, the 12 Federal Home Loan Banks and Fannie itself. Housing, in other words, has become an arm of the state.

One of these groups, the Federal Housing Administration, is so integral to the market that without it prices could have fallen a further 25 per cent, according to Moody'sAnalytics.

And at the same time the Federal Reserve is soaking up some $40bn of mortgage debt a month - through "quantitative easing" - with more than one eye on the housing market.

It is hard to dispute that if you own a residential property in any of the 50 states its value is being held up by the whim of politicians and central bankers.

"The US doesn't use the term socialist very much to describe policies like this, they use words like "progressive" or "redistribution", says Edward Pinto, fellow of the American Enterprise Institute, a free market think-tank. "I recently spoke at a conference in New York where the Europeans were staggered by the extent of government involvement in our mortgage market."

This new status quo is not entirely the result of conscious decisions by the political classes. Washington was forced to prop up real estate when it realised it was so closely entwined with financial markets and their "too big to fail" banks that letting either collapse would be disastrous.

Bear in mind that it was George W Bush, a Republican president, who nationalised Fannie and Freddie in the heat of the crisis in September 2008 - not his more leftwing successor.

But attempts to send the two mortgage underwriters back into the private sector have withered on the vine.

"There was momentum before the election but that has completely evaporated," says Gary Painter, director of research and of graduate programmes in public policy at the Lusk Centre for Real Estate at the University of Southern California. "Now the new political focus is almost entirely on austerity instead."

Meanwhile the Obama administration exhorts private lenders to relax their lending criteria for borrowers.

Across the Atlantic, the tentacles of the state have also become entwined with the housing market, albeit in different ways. The British government would not set Soviet-style targets for tractor production or widget manufacturing. Housing is a different matter.

The central interest rate has been slashed to close to zero. Tens of thousands are buying homes arm-in-arm with the state under "shared equity schemes". Most strikingly, one in three mortgages taken out in the UK are through two government-controlled banks.

Lloyds Banking Group and Royal Bank of Scotland remain in majority state ownership with little sign of progress on a mooted sell-off. Lloyds had 26.7 per cent of the mortgage market last year; RBS had 7.5 per cent.

This is reminiscent of supposedly communist China, where most banks are majority-owned by the state with small public floats.

Andrew Jakabovics, senior director of research at Enterprise Community Partners, a US group, says Westminster is as involved in housing as Washington.

"It's a matter of what you count as involvement in the property market," he argues. "The British intervention was directly into the banks themselves . . . we rescued the secondary mortgage market. That's qualitatively different but not quantitatively different."

Under last year's new initiative, the Bank of England has pumped more than 14bn into a scheme called "Funding for Lending" aimed at forcing down the price of business loans and mortgages.

March 2013 saw the gradual nationalisation move up a gear as chancellor George Osborne earmarked billions more for shared equity programmes and - most controversially - paved the way for Britain's own Freddie/Fannie system of mortgage guarantees.

In theory his move is meant to be temporary. But then so was the "short-term" rescue of Fannie and Freddie five years ago.

The house price crash was certainly spectacular. American homes have lost 35 per cent of their value on average. Irish homes fell by a third. How much worse would it have been without government intervention? "That is the million-dollar question," says Martin Ellis, head of market research at Lloyds, which owns Halifax.

Yet the British market has recovered fast - much faster than the US. The average home last year sold at 246,000, up 10 per cent on five years earlier, according to the government's Office of National Statistics.

That average disguises the mismatch between regions. House prices have powered ahead in London - but in Northern Ireland they are still down by a half from their crazy peak.

Ed Stansfield, an economist at Capital Economics in London - one of the few to predict the housing crash - says there is still a major mismatch between people's income and house prices: "We still have a market where pricing is not at a rational level."

Perhaps the interventions by the government have been, well, too successful?

Voters who own their homes are as asset-rich as they were before and many are making lower mortgage payments. But with lenders loathe to lend as generously as before the crash, young adults who lack sufficient savings remain locked out of the market. The average age of a first-time buyer has risen by eight years since the 1960s to 35, according to Post Office Mortgages. The ratio between house prices and earnings in Britain has barely changed since 2007.

One of my colleagues, Carly Ince, a 30-year-old editorial assistant, is an exemplar. She wants to buy her own flat in Tower Hamlets, a far-from-glamorous borough of east London where she grew up. Even there a basic former council flat in Bow is on the market for 220,000 - seven times the average London wage.

"When my parents first came to the area, both aged 22, they got a flat in east London and the rent was only 9 a month," she says. "People could afford their own home back then. Now you have no chance unless your parents help you or you inherit."

Critics say the UK government's new 130bn mortgage guarantee scheme is more likely to drive up prices than to increase housing supply, re-inflating a damaging bubble.

"Is it just going to drive up house prices? By and large, in the short-run, the answer is, yes," said Stephen Nickell, an official from the Office for Budgetary Responsibility. Alistair Darling, former Labour chancellor, says the move could create a new "housing bubble", replicating the sub-prime crisis in the US.

The question goes to the heart of the dilemma faced by politicians on both sides of the Atlantic. How can they avoid another crash if and when they withdraw support from the market? Some experts speculate on a route out for governments, albeit over a long period.

"Nobody is talking about the government going cold turkey," says Pinto. "You would have to phase it out over, say, five to seven years to give the market a chance to normalise."

Roger Harding, head of policy at Shelter, a British charity for the homeless, says the ideal scenario would be for nominal prices to stay stable, allowing real-terms house prices to gradually become more affordable. At the same time ministers should address the issue of low supply, with housebuilding at a historic low; if they can break entrenched local resistance to development.

Harding says governments should use their armlock over mortgage lending to get homes built: "Otherwise we are going to repeat the mistakes of the past."

The model, he argues, could be the 1950s, when housing minister Harold Macmillan oversaw the building of more than 300,000 new homes a year, mostly through local authorities.

"But politicians will struggle to square the circle. It seems likely they will remain chained to policies that prop up the housing market, even as they keep paying lip service to first-time buyers who they cannot help en masse at the same time.

"It's broadly accepted nowadays that China still lives under the banner of 'communism' despite capitalist markets playing an increasing role in society. In Britain and America - at least where the housing market is concerned - the reverse process seems to be taking place."

Jim Pickard is UK chief political correspondent for the FT

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,664 words
19 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
32
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Apple Struggles in China

Despite its seeming invincibility, even Apple may struggle to climb the Great Firewall of China. China is Apple's main growth market. In the quarter ended in December, China accounted for 13% of Apple's revenue, and CEO Tim Cook has said he expects it will become Apple's largest market. Shareholders anxiously await a deal with China Mobile, the world's largest wireless carrier by subscribers, to officially carry the iPhone.

Yet a string of criticisms and proposed regulatory curbs suggest Apple's trajectory in China may deviate from the cosmic course many expect. The latest came in Wednesday's edition of the People's Daily. An article included Apple in a list of websites and app stores that have been investigated for providing pornographic content in China. This comes a month after Apple was attacked by China Central Television over its iPhone warranties, prompting an apology from Mr. Cook.

Then there is a proposed law that could give the Chinese government more control over apps and put new standards in place that may force companies to help the government track users. Why does Apple suddenly find itself in the cross hairs? The proposed law suggests the Chinese government is wary of the growing power of a company whose smartphone "ecosystem" Beijing doesn't control.

This would fit a pattern. Facebook is a "walled garden" of content that is blocked to Chinese Web surfers entirely. Twitter is, too. And then there is Google which, in effect, kicked itself out of China's mainland search market after refusing censorship demands.

Ironically, Google's Android mobile operating system dominates the smartphone market in China, despite the company's strained relationship with the government. But those devices don't come with Google services that are standard elsewhere, such as YouTube, search, and Google Maps. Also, without access to the Google Play store, Chinese users have a weaker selection of apps to choose from.

Apple is famous for the control it exerts over so many aspects of its devices, including the apps available to users. This is a big part of the iPhone's appeal: Apps just work and are largely free of bugs or malicious code. Mr. Cook will be in a tough spot if, for instance, Beijing requires Apple to alter its services in a way that lends itself more easily to government control.

Not that China is exclusively focused on Apple. A Chinese government research institute said in March that the country is too reliant on Android and that local manufacturers should have more control over it. One issue Beijing must deal with, though, is that iPhones and Android devices are popular for a reason. And Chinese consumers will grow more attached to them over time, especially as there are few good alternatives today. Curbing Chinese consumers' access to such technology would likely cause grumbling, something the government has historically been keen to avoid.

Still, any adversity for Apple and Google in China could spell opportunity for local smartphone makers such as Lenovo and Huawei Technologies. Together, these two had about 26% of China's smartphone market in the fourth quarter of 2012, estimates Strategy Analytics.

Foreigners Samsung and Apple had 16% and 10%, respectively. Hewlett-Packard offers a cautionary tale here. Its share of China's personal-computer market collapsed after it came under fire from state media in 2010 over product quality. Lenovo's market share increased sharply.

Apple's reputation, and huge valuation, rests on its ability to create new markets that it then dominates. China won't necessarily fit that model.

-- Rolfe Winkler

Diageo Drinks

To U.S. Health

Diageo PLC should be grateful Americans haven't lost their thirst for hard liquor. The U.K. spirits giant said European and some emerging-market sales were weak in the first three months of the year. But North America, which generates nearly 40% of Diageo's operating profit, delivered a roughly 7% rise in organic sales last quarter.

Still, how much more can Diageo squeeze out of the U.S. drinker? Much of the recent revenue growth is the result of a series of price increases implemented since last May, which have continued to boost sales. The anniversary of those increases in just a few weeks will raise the question of whether more will be needed to boost revenue growth.

True, wealthy drinkers won't blink at a couple of extra bucks for a bottle of Diageo's top-tier brands like Don Julio tequila and premium varieties of Johnnie Walker whiskey. But Diageo sells far greater volumes of standard brands that could be tougher to mark up: Smirnoff vodka and Captain Morgan rum were its top two sellers by volume in North America by a long shot last year, estimates International Wine & Spirit Research.

Besides, many Americans have thinner wallets this year, thanks to a payroll-tax increase imposed in January and delays in income-tax refunds. Brown-Forman, which makes Jack Daniel's whiskey, recently questioned whether consumers will accept price increases as willingly as they did prerecession; consumers may be flexible with aged spirits, but price increases on un-aged liquors like vodka are "more difficult," it said.

Another risk: While Diageo hasn't commented on future price increases, it is rational for retailers to expect another round. That could have led some retailers to buy extra stocks in advance, temporarily boosting Diageo's revenue.

Diageo shares have risen 26% in the past year and trade at 19.3 times consensus earnings for the year through June, well above its five-year average of 15.3 times in the past five years. The group still has plenty of growth potential, but investors who buy now may regret it in the morning.

-- John Jannarone

Raising Dutch Austerity Stakes

When even the deficit hawks take a breather from austerity, you know things are tough. And in the Netherlands, things look very tough indeed.

Dutch unemployment climbed in March to 8.1%, from 7.7% in February and 7.2% just three months ago. True, the latest jump was more due to a rise in the labor force than outright job losses, which were low. But the jobless number is just one in a string that show an economy in trouble, hamstrung by its high levels of personal debt and exposure to euro-zone trade. Dutch gross domestic product contracted 1% last year. Corporate bankruptcies are at a record. Real disposable income dropped 3.2% in 2012, the fifth consecutive annual fall and the biggest on record. House prices have fallen 18% from their August 2008 high, and as of 2011, 25% of mortgages were underwater. In February, the Netherlands had to nationalize SNS Reaal, the country's fourth-largest banking group.

As a result, the Dutch government is struggling to reduce its budget deficit to 3% of GDP. In 2012, it stood at 4.1%; in March, the government conceded it wouldn't get to 3% this year. It has identified 4.3 billion euros ($5.6 billion) of new measures to hit the target in 2014, but last week Prime Minister Mark Rutte said he would wait until September to decide whether austerity is needed. That smacks partly of desperation: The economy seems unlikely to turn around enough that the cuts will be reduced by any significant amount. But it also recognizes that Dutch consumers and businesses have been battered.

The dismal economy and the softening of austerity may present some political complications for Dutch Finance Minister Jeroen Dijsselbloem in his role as head of the Eurogroup, hammering out bailout terms for other countries. But they seem unlikely to cause a deepening of the crisis. Ten-year Dutch bond yields, at 1.44%, are only 0.2 percentage point higher than Germany's. The Netherlands may even benefit, like France, from quantitative-easing flows from Japan. In January, Japanese investors bought 6.2 billion euros of Dutch bonds, a record, according to UBS.

The debate about austerity will rage on. But the battle lines have been drawn in the euro-zone bond market, and the Netherlands is on the fortunate side of them.

-- Richard Barley

Jupiter

Grapples

With New

Universe

Charging customers for something they assumed came free is a recipe for upheaval. Jupiter Fund Management blames new U.K. rules that force intermediaries to charge upfront fees rather than bundle commissions into product charges for sluggish inflows into its funds this year. Net flows into Jupiter's mutual funds of GBP 247 million ($376 million) in the first quarter were down 50% from last year's final quarter. The company believes the hiatus will be short term. But that may be too optimistic.

The ranks of U.K. advisers are likely to thin by up to a fifth, estimates Deloitte. Some cost-conscious customers are taking investment into their own hands: Hargreaves Lansdown, whose platform offers savings products directly to consumers, had record inflows in the first quarter. But the money should still find its way to fund managers; Jupiter is the best represented company in Hargreaves Lansdown's ranking of its top 150 funds. Fund managers could also develop their own consumer platforms, cutting out the middlemen.

The snag is that greater transparency is ultimately likely to lead to more pressure on fund-management fees. Much of the industry has got away with charging top-drawer fees for substandard returns, so a shake-up is overdue. Separating advisers' remuneration from product choice may mean greater allocation to passive funds, which charge about 0.2%, rather than active managers, charging closer to 0.75%.

Already, Investec Asset Management has waived initial fees for its U.K. fund range. Lower-cost active funds of the type offered by Schroders or J.P. Morgan are trying to woo money away from index-followers but risk cannibalizing more upmarket business.

The Property Report
Tax Plan May Provide Boost --- In Obama's Budget, a Spur for Foreign Pension Funds to Invest in U.S. Property

By Sarah Krouse 
784 words
17 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Despite the austerity mood in Washington, President Obama's proposed budget would provide tax relief to some foreign investors, a move the U.S. real-estate industry has been seeking for years.

As part of a larger push to spur private investment in U.S. infrastructure, Mr. Obama has proposed changes to a 1980s tax policy called the Foreign Investment in Real Property Tax Act, or Firpta, that would exempt foreign pension funds from paying taxes on gains from real-estate sales.

The proposal is still subject to congressional approval, which will likely prove tricky in the current budget-cutting climate. It also doesn't go as far as the real-estate industry would like because the proposed exemption would only apply to overseas pension funds, not all foreign investors.

But this is the first time Mr. Obama has included a Firpta change in a budget. If the change is approved, it likely would lead to an increase in U.S. investments by foreign pension funds.

"For a pension investor who is looking around the world thinking, 'I want a global portfolio,' quite often the Firpta has stopped them filling in that North American piece," said Paul Richards, head of European property at consulting firm Mercer.

The proposed exemption would cost $2.2 billion over the next 10 years, according to the 2014 budget, and is part of a larger package of incentives for infrastructure investment.

Firpta had led a number of investors to change their strategies to investing in real-estate debt in the U.S. rather than equity, said Ben Sanderson of Hermes Real Estate Investment Management Ltd., which manages money for pension funds. "It's not a barrier that makes it entirely impossible to invest, but [investing in the U.S. would be] more attractive if it was reformed," he said.

Firpta taxes gains from foreign sales of assets including buildings, land, mines and timber, as well as stock in real-estate companies. While foreign investors consider it onerous, many still invest in the U.S. because of its clear legal system and a transparent property market, where price and performance data is easy to obtain.

However, foreign pension-fund investments accounted for just 1% of the total $267.12 billion in U.S. commercial real-estate deals completed last year, according to data from Cushman & Wakefield and Real Capital Analytics. Overseas pension funds represented the same percentage of the total investment volume in 2011.

Deals by foreign investors as a whole represented 7% of total deal volume in 2012.

High-profile transactions by foreign pension funds in 2012 included the Canada Pension Plan Investment Board's $1.8 billion investment in a joint venture with Westfield Group that included interests in 10 regional U.S. malls and two redevelopment sites.

The Second Swedish National Pension Fund, a $37.64 billion fund, made its first investment in U.S. commercial real estate last year as part of a push to diversify its portfolio. The fund paid $325 million for a 41% stake in US Office Holdings, a company owned by South Korea's National Pension Service and Tishman Speyer Properties LP. The Swedish fund had invested 7.5% of its portfolio in real estate throughout the world in 2012, according to its latest annual report.

Patrick Kanters, managing director of global real estate at APG Asset Management, the fund manager that invests 329 billion euros ($428.9 billion) for pension funds including Dutch civil-service pension fund ABP, said the group had been investing in U.S. real estate since the late 1990s, but that the proposed change was a "great idea" that would set a more-level playing field for investors.

He said that today, domestic buyers in the U.S. are able to place higher bids in competitive tenders for properties because their future internal rates of return, or IRR, aren't affected by the added tax on future capital gains. "They can pay slightly more to get the same IRR," Mr. Kanters said.

Jim Fetgatter, chief executive of the Association of Foreign Investors in Real Estate, said the move to revise the law showed investors for the first time that the White House understands the impact of Firpta. "The fact that the president acknowledged that Firpta regulations are an impediment to foreign investment, that's a significant step for it to be publicly acknowledged, and from such a high level," Mr. Fetgatter said.

Roper to Buy Managed Health Care for $1 Billion

By Debbie Cai

17 April 2013
22:28
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Roper Industries Inc. has agreed to buy Managed Health Care Associates Inc. for about $1 billion in cash as the high-technology company looks to expand in medical-related services.

Privately owned Managed Health Care supplies software and analytics services to specialty pharmacies, assisted-living facilities and other health-care providers outside of an acute-care hospital setting.

In 2007, private-equity firm Diamond Castle Holdings LLC bought the Florham Park, N.J., company in partnership with its management.

Roper, whose products range from radio-frequency readers to automatic water-meter readers, had seen double-digit-sales increases for nearly two years, thanks in part to acquisitions. But its sales growth began to ease in late 2011.

The Sarasota, Fla., company expects the deal to generate $95 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization over the first 12 months of ownership, excluding the impact of acquisition-related fair value accounting.

Roper Chief Executive Brian Jellison pointed to Managed Health Care's technology tools and customer-service model, adding the company generates substantial recurring revenue through long-term customer relationships and high retention rates.

The deal is expected to be completed within 30 days.

Roper's recent fourth-quarter earnings rose 18% amid strong revenue growth in it medical and scientific imaging segment, while margins also widened.

Forex chiefs hope for end of risk-on, risk-off trend

By Alice Ross, Currencies Correspondent 
1,060 words
16 April 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

After long months in the dark, currencies are coming back into the light. Investors have flocked back to the global foreign exchange market this year as trends have developed in key currencies, while talk of currency wars has put the Japanese yen, the euro, the pound and the US dollar firmly back on policy makers' agendas.

Heads of forex at investment banks are hoping 2013 will finally shed the risk-on, risk-off trend that has dominated currency markets since the financial crisis of 2008, and that traditional currency trading on economic fundamentals will return.

The Japanese yen has been the big draw for investors this year. Shorting the currency has been the most popular trade in the foreign exchange market since late last year, when a new government that promised to drag Japan out of its decades-long period of deflation was elected.

Hedge funds began to front run changes expected at the Bank of Japan. This sent shockwaves through the currency market in April when it unveiled a far more aggressive monetary easing package than had been expected.

The move has created a clear trend in the Japanese yen that is music to the ears of investors and traders, who have wearied of watching currencies react to news headlines and developments in the eurozone crisis over the past two years.

Since the start of 2013, the dollar has risen 15 per cent against the yen to approach Y100, its strongest level in nearly four years, while the euro is at its strongest level in more than three years at Y130, a 14 per cent gain this year.

The Japanese yen is not alone in showing a strong trend this year. The UK pound fell steadily in the first 10 weeks of the year as waning haven demand and concerns over the health of the UK economy led investors to dump sterling.

Meanwhile, the dollar has been rising in line with US equities. Traders and analysts believe this could mark a period of dollar strength at times of global risk appetite, as investors use the weaker yen to fund trades instead of the more liquid dollar.

"We've definitely seen a resurgence in forex this year as an asset class," says Peter Taylor, managing director in forex trading at Barclays.

"If we look at the previous two to three years it was an asset class that many investors stayed away from for various reasons: we were living in a more complex environment that was driven by political happenings in Europe and we had currency moves that were a lot more headline driven.

"The themes we've seen play out in the dollar and yen have made for an easier trading environment and definitely been a driver of bigger volumes from investors."

Kevin Rodgers, head of forex at Deutsche Bank, says: "There's been a slightly more normal feel as we used to have a lot of interest in the yen, sterling and Swiss franc. That turned heavily into emerging markets and the euro in 2011 and 2012, so I think the mix of currencies seems more normal this year."

Jeff Feig, head of G10 foreign exchange at Citigroup in New York, says: "There's definitely less risk-on, risk-off trading, and a greater focus on the economic fundamentals underlying currency pairs, and I think that's good for business."

"Risk-on, risk-off is not in vogue, thank God," says Tim Carrington, global head of currencies and emerging markets at Royal Bank of Scotland. "That's another way of saying correlations between asset classes are going back to normal, which is a good thing."

Bankers are still expecting more trade from the Japanese yen, despite the recent sharp falls, with many pointing out that much of the movement so far has been driven by hedge funds. RBS says that US and European asset managers have not yet become heavily involved in shorting the yen, while a wall of cash from domestic Japanese investors and life insurance companies is expected to hit overseas markets in the coming weeks.

That has already led to a fall in bond yields across Europe and emerging markets as hedge funds front-run the expected buying spree.

"The interest in the yen trade is very high - I don't think it's necessarily peaked," says Mr Feig at Citi. "There's a sense that a lot of this is foreign driven, and the next leg could really be driven by domestic flows."

Bankers are still cautious on calling a definite end to risk-on, risk-off trading. The crisis in Cyprus in March caused some to wobble in their predictions that the currency world had returned to fundamental trading, with the yen re-emerging temporarily as a haven during the eurozone crisis.

"We like a bit of volatility: it prompts businesses to hedge, it brings risk money into the markets; but what we don't really want is a catastrophe," says Mr Rodgers.

Bankers are hoping the upswing in interest in currency trading will lead to higher revenues this year in their foreign exchange divisions.

Figures from Coalition, a financial services analytics company, showed a drop of around a fifth in revenues at many top banks last year as investors shied away from trying to make money by trading currencies. Companies frequently did not bother to hedge currency exposure because volatility was so low between currency pairs.

Mr Feig says: "The first quarter has been very good for us and for our clients and we hope to see more of that - fingers crossed."

Tim Carrington at RBS says: "In the first quarter, we've seen better returns in forex than in the past several years. The investor community is far more active than they have been for years."

But he sounds a note of warning: foreign exchange is becoming less profitable for many banks as more competition and increased electronic trading make clients more cost-conscious, leading to lower margins at the banks. The difference in price between buying and selling currencies, known as the bid-offer spread, for example, has narrowed.

"The spread compression will hit the banking community quite hard," Mr Carrington says. "The sheer cost pressures in business have made the investor side more aggressive."

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Russia poses problems for BlackRock; Fund focus

By Eleanor Lawrie 
739 words
15 April 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Energy holdings blamed for fall in trust's shares, writes Eleanor Lawrie

BlackRock's Sam Vecht has admitted that "inappropriate" holdings in Russian energy stocks contributed to losses on his eastern European investment trust in the past year. This comes even as the trust's board plans an overhaul of the product.

Shares of the 130.7m BlackRock eastern European Trust shed 2.9 per cent in the past 12 months - a performance the fund manager describes as "disappointing".

The trust held roughly 10 per cent in Russian gas giant Gazprom as the company's share price peaked in September last year, along with large holdings in Lukoil, Surgutneftegaz and Novatek, according to historic portfolio numbers from data provider FE Analytics.

"It's a big sector and I got the stocks wrong," says Mr Vecht.

The trust's shares fell 2.9 per cent in the year to April 3 compared with a rise on its benchmark MSCI EM Europe 10/40 index of 1.7 per cent.

The trust's share price discount to the value of its assets widened from 9.1 per cent to 9.8 per cent during the period, FE says, contributing to the loss.

The trust's board has just announced changes to be put to shareholders in its annual general meeting in June. This will involve changing the trust's name to the BlackRock Emerging Europe trust, and slashing its holdings from 50 to between 20 and 30 companies.

The manager said this was to reflect investor demand for higher conviction holdings to emphasise its active management, as exchange traded fund inflows have dominated inflows into the region in recent months.

The 12-month loss has not derailed the manager's impressive longer-term record. Since Mr Vecht took over in May 2009, the trust's shares have gained 75.9 per cent, compared with a 49.5 per cent benchmark performance.

The trust's five-year loss of 21.6 per cent continues to show the scars of Russia's sharp falls in the early phase of the subprime financial crisis.

The manager says he is still not "massively" bullish on Russia, which made up 62.9 per cent of the portfolio as of February 28 out of a maximum allocation of 70 per cent, although there are positive signs.

Russia has "never been cheaper", Mr Vecht says, citing other developments including rising dividends, liberalisation and economic reforms .

Elsewhere, Mr Vecht says he is also targeting several markets with proximity to Russia.

He has built up a roughly 2.5 per cent weighting in Kazakhstan and 1.5 per cent in Turkmenistan, saying the economies are "coming out of a challenging period" and offer an opportunity that is "well below most people's radars".

Mr Vecht says his best-performing stock in the past year was Turkish gold mining company Koza Altin, which has come good after several years in the portfolio.

He says eastern European economies face an appearance of "guilt by association", under which their proximity to the big developed economies of Europe and the US creates the impression they face similar fiscal and monetary troubles.

Ben Seager-Scott, senior research analyst at Bestinvest, says the trust is on the group's "radar" but he prefers to access emerging markets through broader mandates, leaving regional allocation to managers' discretion.

"When you get into regional funds - and the same holds true for funds that invest specifically in some of the larger countries - in many ways the fund may be forced into stocks simply to 'make up the numbers' for a broad and diversified portfolio," says Mr Seager-Scott.

"That said, the eastern Europe investment trust is a pretty reasonable investment, and Sam Vecht has good form in this area, adding value for investors since he took over."

Mr Seager-Scott says more than half the investable eastern European region was accounted for by Russia, a country which was more of a "fallen superpower than a rising star" when compared with other emerging market countries.

"It is also volatile and highly sensitive to the oil price," he adds. "This volatility means even good managers are prone to extended periods of underperformance as their good judgment is obscured by noise and global macroeconomics or geopolitics that are difficult to predict."

Eleanor Lawrie is a reporter at Investment Adviser

US
Economist Eyed for Fannie Watchdog

By Nick Timiraos 
636 words
13 April 2013
01:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Mark Zandi, a prominent economist, has emerged as a leading candidate to head the regulator of mortgage-finance companies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac amid signs that he would likely attract support from Senate Republicans, according to people familiar with the matter.

The White House hasn't yet decided who to nominate as the next director of the Federal Housing Finance Agency, a position it has struggled to fill. Along with Mr. Zandi, the Obama administration has also been considering Rep. Mel Watt, a North Carolina Democrat.

The FHFA director has become an increasingly important economic-policy position in Washington, because the agency serves as the warden of Fannie and Freddie, which own or guarantee half of all the nation's mortgages.

Mr. Zandi, co-founder of an economic-forecasting firm that was purchased by Moody's Corp. in 2005, serves as chief economist of Moody'sAnalytics. A registered Democrat, he was an economic adviser to the 2008 presidential campaign of Sen. John McCain (R., Ariz.). He speaks frequently on the economy, fiscal issues and housing testifying before Congress at least nine times in the last two years and played a key role advising congressional Democrats on the 2009 economic-stimulus bill.

In an interview Thursday, Mr. Zandi dismissed a question about whether he was being considered for the FHFA job. "Who knows what they're going to do with that," he said, declining to elaborate.

The FHFA's current director, Edward DeMarco, took the job four years ago in an acting capacity after his predecessor left for the private sector. Mr. DeMarco has at times clashed with the Obama administration over homeowner aid, and left-leaning groups have campaigned to replace him. The agency, created five years ago, has never had its own director confirmed by the Senate because of Republican opposition to an earlier nomination.

Mr. Zandi has attracted attention, in part, because he may have a good chance at winning Senate confirmation. Senate Republicans have largely supported Mr. DeMarco and are expected to scrutinize his successor closely. But Mr. Zandi may placate these critics.

Sen. Bob Corker (R., Tenn.), who serves on the Senate Banking Committee, said in a written statement Friday: "If Mark Zandi and the administration have an acceptable plan to transition us away from our dependence on [Fannie and Freddie], I'm optimistic that he could do a good job helping our country effectively execute that plan while also protecting the taxpayer."

On housing, Mr. Zandi has published a paper outlining the need for a continued government role in backstopping the home-loan market.

While he has sometimes advocated more government intervention to address the foreclosure crisis, he has also called for scaling back the mortgage-interest deduction and the government's role in the mortgage market once the current crisis has abated.

Replacing Mr. DeMarco could give the administration greater latitude to expand initiatives to help homeowners facing foreclosure and to mediate disputes over when banks should be forced to "buy back" defaulted mortgages, which some policy makers fret has kept credit standards too tight. But people familiar with the process have said that the Obama administration's push to fill the FHFA position has less to do with specific policy flare-ups and more to do with its desire to have a permanent director oversee the process of overhauling Fannie and Freddie.

Without clear direction from the White House or Congress, Mr. DeMarco has begun to move more aggressively to shrink the firms' heavy presence in the mortgage market. Last month, he announced plans to merge some of the firms' back-office operations into a separate company that, for now, would be jointly owned by Fannie and Freddie.
