Health
When Your M.D. Is a Computer Algorithm --- To Curb Prescription Painkiller Abuse, Firms Take Data-Driven Approach; Some Physicians Leery of Analytic Programs

By Timothy W. Martin 
919 words
12 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

ORLANDO, Fla. -- Despite all that drug-enforcement agents and regulators are doing to fight prescription painkiller abuse, the most effective combatant might turn out to be a computer algorithm.

That became apparent at last week's National Rx Drug Abuse Summit in Orlando. Joining an audience of patient advocates, policy makers and law-enforcement officers were members of a burgeoning cottage industry: a swarm of firms that are taking a data-driven approach to the drug-abuse epidemic.

With the costs of abuse soaring, these firms are combining medical research and guidelines with computer analysis to guide some doctors about what prescription painkillers should be administered to which patients, and in what doses. Or, if at all.

The companies, largely insurers and medical-bill review consultants for firms paying workers' compensation claims, say they're filling an unmet need: providing research-driven clinical advice and guidelines to physicians who haven't been thoroughly educated on how to treat pain. They say they will help lower health-care costs, dictate safe use and identify potential painkiller abusers before they get hooked.

But some physicians are leery of analytic programs -- with names like "Opioid Defense Manager" and "VantageComp" -- playing any role in medical treatment, especially when the software is deployed by companies looking to lower drug costs and medical spending.

Rising Medical Solutions Inc. was one of around 40 analytic companies attending the Orlando conference. Using algorithms developed in-house, Rising Medical assesses whether an individual is low, medium or high-risk.

The valuation is based on a 30-question survey that asks about an injured worker's optimism about recovery, their previous injury history and whether they smoke, among other signs that measure vulnerability to becoming addicted to painkillers.

It also considers factors like the "lag time" -- when an injury occurred versus when it was reported -- since a delayed disclosure could signal higher potential for abuse. In those situations, Rising Medical assigns case managers to high-risk cases from day one.

For instance, if a restaurant worker with a broken ankle stays on opioids longer than two weeks, Rising Medical may issue an alert. The Chicago-based company has determined that being on prescription painkillers any longer could be an early indicator of addiction, said Jason F. Beans, CEO at Rising Medical.

"We enact interventions to prevent the small lower-back claim from becoming a lifetime addiction," Mr. Beans said. "We want to deal with cases before they become problems."

PMSI Inc.'s statisticians and actuaries built a proprietary Risk Intelligence System that employs 18 criteria it says could signal fraud or abuse. What pops out is a Population Risk Scorecard, on a scale of 0 to 150, that compares an individual company's results versus other firms in the area or in a similar industry.

If individual cases show potential abuse, PMSI-employed physicians will dial up doctors treating an injured worker and walk them through a "real-time clinical road map," based on the medication history and evidence-based guidelines. "We know that all physicians aren't perfect," said Jay W. Krueger, an executive vice president at PMSI.

Some doctors question whether analytics could discriminate against people who have a legitimate need. "One person doesn't heal the same way another person does. Treatment has to be directed by the physician -- not the insurer," said Andrea Trescot, a practicing pain doctor and past president of the American Society of Interventional Pain Physicians, a nonprofit industry group.

The analytics-driven industry has grown to more than three dozen companies from just a handful a decade ago, said Ishita Sengupta, director of workers' compensation at the National Academy of Social Insurance, a nonprofit research group that tracks workers' compensation benefits and costs.

Yet it is growing like an industry with a future. Insurers like American International Group Inc., Liberty Mutual Insurance Co. and Travelers Cos. have added analytical or predictive-modeling abilities to their workers' compensation divisions in recent years.

Some pharmacy-benefit managers like Modern Medical Inc., of Lewis Center, Ohio, specialize in paring down workers' compensation costs. "Your doctor's approach to pain care may lead you to become addicted -- but that's not the case with high cholesterol or high blood pressure," said Michael Gavin, chief strategy officer at Prium, a Duluth, Ga., firm that consults for workers' compensation payers.

Prium can require direct nurse oversight of individual cases or physician reviews of an individual drug regimen.

The nascent industry's growth comes as workers' compensation spending is soaring. The average medical cost for workers paid to stay at home was $28,000 in 2011, nearly doubling in a decade from $15,900 in 2001, according to the National Council on Compensation Insurance, which tracks workers' comp spending. Painkiller spending also nearly doubled last decade, to $35 a claim in 2009, says NCCI.

"Hydrocodone is cheap, but the medical treatment spending is expensive," said Michael A. Testa, a fraud analytics senior manager at Express Scripts Holding Co.

Companies using analytics say it is just one tool -- not the only one -- and that they employ their own physicians for guidance.

Their internal guidelines, even if proprietary, are often tied to either state-mandated workers' compensation regulations or evidence-based research, they say.

Special
Sector Investing; Funds to Fine-Tune Technology Exposure; These four funds focus on areas of tech and communications where analysts see rapid growth

By Steven Rosenbush 
1,069 words
11 April 2013
21:41
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Fund investors can get considerable exposure to technology stocks through broad index funds such as those tracking the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, which was 16% in tech as of March 31. Some people also own broad tech or communications sector funds.

But for investors who want to capture tech's growth opportunities in the purest way and are comfortable with more concentrated risk, narrower, thematic mutual funds and exchange-traded funds can make sense.

Here are four highly targeted funds that offer exposure to what many analysts consider to be promising markets in technology and communications. While the performance of some of these funds hasn't been strong over the past year, the funds do give investors exposure to several tech niches that analysts predict will have strong revenue growth in coming years.

First Trust ISE Cloud Computing Index. Cloud computing allows big businesses to lease the computing and software resources of a third party instead of buying their own hardware and software. This can save the companies money, as well as give them earlier access to the latest technology, since company-owned data centers are expensive to upgrade and designed to last for years. Researcher Gartner Inc. has forecast that spending on cloud services will grow at a compound annual rate of 17.7% from 2011 to 2016.

The majority of the stocks in this $95 million ETF are tech vendors, but about 10% of the index developed by International Securities Exchange LLC is dedicated to end users of the technology, such as Netflix Inc., the online movie rental service. The fund's top holdings include Aruba Networks Inc., which sells networking equipment; software giant SAP AG; cloud-based corporate software company NetSuite Inc.; and Netflix. The expense ratio is 0.6%.

The ETF, launched in 2011, returned a negative 0.7% over the 12 months through March, compared with the 0.6% average return of funds in Morningstar Inc.'s tech category.

First Trust Nasdaq CEA Smartphone Index. It might seem that everyone in the world has a smartphone, but that is hardly the case. Smartphones still constitute a minority of the mobile market world-wide, according to Todd Rosenbluth, head of ETF research at S&P Capital IQ. "We think smartphones are going to continue to be a growth market, especially as prices for the technology continue to fall," he says, and that is good news for "the food chain of companies in the smartphone manufacturing process." He also thinks that many companies in this $12 million ETF such as top holdings Samsung Electronics Co. and electronics manufacturers Sanmina-SCI Corp., Benchmark Electronics Inc. and Compal Communications Inc. are good values.

One issue for investors, according to Mr. Rosenbluth, is that this two-year-old fund is thinly traded and can have a wide bid-ask spread (the difference between the prices potential buyers "bid" to acquire shares and the prices potential sellers "ask" to part with theirs). The expense ratio is 0.7%.

The smartphone ETF returned a negative 3% over the year through March, trailing the Morningstar tech category's average return.

But growing demand for high-speed mobile devices and equipment bodes well for companies such as Samsung, a maker of 4G LTE smartphones, currently the fastest technology. Gartner sees the market for 4G LTE infrastructure growing at a compound annual rate of 42.6% from 2013 to 2017.

PowerShares Dynamic Networking. This $37 million ETF gives investors exposure to companies that make the equipment that runs telecom networks, a sector that Gartner predicts will expand about 5% this year, or more than twice the pace of the economy.

Some segments within that broader market, however, are expected to grow much faster. Gartner sees the wireless corporate network market growing at a compound annual rate of 18.4% from 2011 to 2016, and the market for high-speed fiber-optic Internet-access equipment growing at a compound annual rate of 12.1% from 2010 to 2014.

The ETF tracks a NYSE Arca index that selects stocks based on price and earnings momentum, earnings quality, management and value, says Ed McRedmond, senior vice president of institutional and portfolio strategies at Invesco PowerShares, the fund's sponsor.

Top holdings include router makers Juniper Networks Inc. and Cisco Systems Inc., enterprise information-technology company Citrix Systems Inc., communications-infrastructure company Motorola Solutions Inc. and connector company Amphenol Corp.

The fund has an expense ratio of 0.63%.

The ETF returned a negative 6.7% over the past year. But for the past five years, it is in the top 10% of Morningstar's tech-ETF category, with an average return of 12.9% a year.

Fidelity Select Communications Equipment. Investors seeking an actively managed fund focused on the communications and data-networking market might consider this offering from Fidelity Investments as an alternative to the PowerShares ETF.

Brian Lempel, a research analyst and IT sector leader at Fidelity Investments, says Fidelity looks for equipment companies that benefit from a number of long-term technology trends, including cloud computing, data analytics and consumerization, which refers to the growing demand for consumer tech products within large corporations.

"Secular trends in technology take place over many years," he says. He says he and his colleagues aim to "identify the winners" and hold on to them through market ups and downs as their businesses grow.

This $300 million mutual fund, managed by Ali Khan, holds companies such as Cisco, Juniper and Motorola Solutions, and wireless technology companies Qualcomm Inc. and Ericsson. The expense ratio is 0.89%, according to Morningstar.

The fund is down 3.2% over the past year, vs. the average 13.4% gain for funds in the Morningstar communications category.

But for the past five years, the fund has returned an average 5.2% a year, beating the average 2.9% return of funds in its Morningstar category.

Mr. Rosenbush is deputy editor of CIO Journal, at WSJ.com. Email him at "mailto:steven.rosenbush@wsj.com".

Corrections & Amplifications Brian Lempel is a research analyst and IT sector leader at Fidelity Investments. An earlier version of this article incorrectly identified him as the manager of Fidelity Select Communications Equipment, which is managed by Ali Khan.

Markets
Yen Extends Losses

By Ira Iosebashvili And Nicole Hong 
454 words
10 April 2013
21:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The yen fell to its lowest price against the dollar in four years, but stopped just short of the 100 level.

Japan's currency has fallen around 7% since last week, when the Bank of Japan surprised markets with the magnitude of its new easing program. The central bank plans to pump trillions of yen into the Japanese economy as a way to lower long-term interest rates and stoke inflation, a process that is also expected to result in a weaker yen.

"The Bank of Japan understands the urgency of the moment, so they will be quite aggressive from here," said David Gilmore, a partner at Foreign Exchange Analytics. "We're not looking at a one-time move by any means."

In late-afternoon trade, the dollar bought 99.76, its highest level since April 2009, compared with 99.03 late Tuesday.

It was helped along by an earlier-than-expected release of the minutes from the Federal Reserve's March 19-20 meeting, which showed Fed members actively debating whether to pull back on its bond-buying program before the end of the year. Bond buying by the Fed typically weakens the value of the dollar.

"A lot of people in the market are targeting a dollar-yen move to 100, so the minutes were an excuse for people to sell yen again," said Greg Anderson, a currency strategist at Citigroup in New York.

The yen saw a brief bout of strength early Wednesday, after new BOJ Gov. Haruhiko Kuroda said in an interview with Dow Jones Newswires and other media that he doesn't expect an "avalanche" of capital leaving Japan due to the central bank's newly enhanced easing program. Mr. Kuroda said the BOJ plans were not aimed at weakening the yen.

However, the yen erased all gains within an hour.

Michael Woolfolk, a currency strategist at Bank of New York Mellon, said, "Japanese officials are concerned about the pace of the yen's decline ... but unless they say anything significantly new, the market is going to quickly return to yen selling."

The euro, meanwhile, slipped to $1.3070 from the previous day's close of $1.3082, after industrial production numbers across the region came in mixed, highlighting the long road the euro zone faces as it attempts to kick-start economic growth.

The Wall Street Journal Dollar Index, which tracks the U.S. dollar against a basket of currencies, was recently up 0.03% to 73.72.

The British pound bought $1.5330 from $$1.5322 late Tuesday. The Australian dollar fetched $1.0539 versus $1.0489.

Easy Money: Fed Policies Spur Corporate Spending

By Victoria McGrane 
1,033 words
9 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Historically low interest rates engineered by the Federal Reserve have paid off for Fricks Co., a maker of industrial concrete floors based in Fort Worth, Texas.

On one side of the ledger, the low rates have encouraged several big customers to dust off construction plans they had shelved during the recession, bringing in a flood of work, says Ronald Arrington, the privately held company's chief financial officer. "Our estimator is just swamped," he adds, referring to the company employee who provides price estimates to prospective customers.

Coupled with tax breaks, the low rates have also led Fricks to increase its own borrowing, Mr. Arrington says. The company has used the money to replace about half its fleet of specialized construction equipment, as measured by dollar value, ahead of schedule.

Mr. Arrington is one of 450 CFOs who responded to questions about the effect of low interest rates on company borrowing and debt management over the past 12 to 18 months for a survey conducted by Duke University and CFO Magazine. The latest round of the quarterly online survey, which polls public and private companies of various sizes across a range of sectors, concluded March 8. The questions related to interest rates were posed exclusively for The Wall Street Journal.

The results of the survey, as well as interviews with some participants, provide a window into how the Fed's unconventional policies are influencing corporate behavior.

CFOs at 202 firms, about 45% of the survey respondents, said low interest rates had led them to increase borrowing. The poll also drilled down on what firms are doing with the borrowed funds, finding that half of the financial officers who are borrowing more said they're spending the proceeds on new capital investments.

About one in four of those borrowing more said they had used the funds to increase mergers and acquisitions, and about a fourth said they had expanded their domestic operations. About one in 10 said they had increased hiring. Each CFO could check more than one use of the money.

The Fed has kept short-term interest rates pinned at nearly zero since late 2008, and more recently it embarked on several rounds of aggressive bond-buying to lower long-term rates, all in an effort to boost a sluggish economy.

The easy-money policies are designed to induce spending and investing, which the Fed hopes will lead to more hiring. But Fed critics say the policies aren't having their intended effect, pointing to disappointingly slow growth and still high unemployment of 7.7%.

The survey, though, shows the Fed's easy-money policies are motivating at least some businesses to invest in new equipment, buildings and technology upgrades. Moreover, some business executives say lenders are starting to make credit more available, too.

John Graham, a finance professor at Duke and a director of the survey, said the results show Fed policies are providing a "solid step in the right direction," especially when combined with the strengthening housing market.

The impact of the Fed's policies on companies' hiring is tougher to gauge. Few of the CFOs responding to the survey said they were borrowing money specifically to hire more workers. But interviews with several of them revealed that their borrowing for other activities sometimes led to increased staffing.

Low rates prompted family-owned liquor and wine distributor Young's Market Co. to double its credit line, which is secured by the company's inventory and receivables, over the past two years, said Kevin Manion, CFO of the Tustin, Calif.-based business.

The borrowed funds have been spent on investments aimed at boosting growth, and they have included higher-speed conveyor and sorting systems to move product through its warehouses faster, as well as technology upgrades to help track that inventory better and fill customer orders more quickly and accurately.

While the borrowing didn't directly underwrite new hiring, the company increased its workforce about 5% in each of the past two years, Mr. Manion says.

Similarly, Hartford, Conn.-based Kelser Corp. hasn't used new borrowing specifically to increase staffing, but the technology company has hired workers to keep up with annual revenue growth of 25% to 30% over the past two years, says CFO Donald Noble.

Despite low rates, Mr. Noble says it wasn't until last June that he started to see lenders actually willing to extend credit. The company has since accepted an offer from International Business Machines Corp. to increase its "flooring" line of credit, which finances inventory, by 50%. In addition, Kelser took out a loan to buy other companies.

While lenders are interested in making loans to his company, credit is still tight for weaker companies, Mr. Noble says.

In fact, many of the firms Kelser is looking at as possible acquisition targets can't get the financing or credit facilities they want, he says. "They're not taking as many chances on companies anymore," he says.

While the Fed's policies appear to be spurring more borrowing, one risk is that companies could overdo it.

U.S. banks' commercial and industrial lending in the week including March 20 was up 11% from a year earlier, to near precrisis levels, according to Fed data. More than $1 trillion of nonfinancial U.S. corporate bonds, a record level, were issued last year, according to Dealogic, and the pace is about the same so far this year, with about $259 billion of bonds issued as of April 1.

"Short-term positive, long-term problematic" is how Moody'sAnalytics economist John Lonski described the interest-rate environment for U.S. corporations. The low rates have helped companies strengthen their financial positions by lowering the cost of their debt and encouraging them to use more long-term debt, but they could also take on more debt than they can handle.

Corporate debt is growing at nearly twice the rate that profits grew last year, Mr. Lonski says. And if the trend continues, it could "destabilize the economy in the future."

MarketWatch / The Personal Finance Report: Bank To-Do List: Make a Deposit, Grab a Brew, Maybe Strike a Pose

By Charles Passy 
501 words
9 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Forget free toasters -- think beer and yoga.

Those extras are among the draws being used by banks to lure customers to branches in an age when checks can be deposited with a smartphone picture and mortgage applications filled out online.

An Umpqua Bank branch in Portland, Oregon, drew a crowd of 150 last fall with an Oktoberfest-style celebration, replete with pretzels, beer and a strolling accordionist. Such events are part of a larger slate of community events and programs -- from yoga classes to dog fairs -- that Umpqua offers in its 200-plus branches. The idea is simple: The more reasons a bank gives customers -- or just as important, potential customers -- to visit a branch, the more likely the bank will grab a piece of their business. "You take the chore" of going to the bank "and turn it into a pleasant experience," says Eve Callahan, an Umpqua spokeswoman.

The idea is spreading, as banks aim to reshape themselves from transactional hubs into more like community centers. The driving factor: Many customers never set foot in branches any more -- even to visit an ATM. A study by Financial Management Solutions, Inc., a Georgia-based research and consulting firm, found that monthly teller transactions at community banks and credit unions plummeted 40% from 1992 to 2012.

But banks remain dependent on branches to pitch services: The customer who walks in to cash a check -- or get a free scoop of ice cream -- may walk out with a car loan. And those marketing opportunities don't translate as well in the online and mobile worlds, where customers are apt to remain focused on the transaction at hand, says Will Weidman, vice president of Applied Predictive Technologies, a software and analytics company that works with financial institutions. "Banks have not cracked the other channels as a means to cross-sell," he says.

The new approach is similar to the Starbucks model. In some instances, it can be as basic as providing free coffee and Wi-Fi -- or, in the case of TD Bank, free coin-counting at "Penny Arcade" machines. Other banks are opening up their spaces for community meetings, or hosting their own classes and seminars.

Connecticut-based Connex Credit Union has held events for first-time home buyers and for seniors grappling with understanding Medicare. And Philadelphia-based Beneficial Bank set up financial libraries within its branches, so customers can read up on any number of money-related topics. In the process, Beneficial CEO Gerry Cuddy says, the bank hopes to create a "stickier" relationship with its clients.

Mr. Cuddy says the goal is to create a welcoming environment -- not make a "hard sell." But some industry observers warn that banks risk alienating customers if the cross-selling becomes too obvious or intense.

Health Care (A Special Report) --- Your Company Wants to Make You Healthy: A look at the pros and cons of the most popular wellness programs used by companies

By Jen Wieczner 
1,292 words
9 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Employer wellness programs used to mean just having a gym in the office or posters on the wall encouraging people to take the stairs instead of the elevator. Now more companies are using real money, and sometimes penalties, as incentives for workers to get in better shape.

Nearly 90% of employers offer wellness incentives, or financial rewards or prizes to employees who work toward getting healthier, according to a recent survey from Fidelity Investments and the National Business Group on Health. That's up from 57% of companies in 2009. The perks are also worth more now: $521 per employee on average, compared with $260 four years ago.

"They feel that if they pay more they'll get a better result," says Adam Stavisky, a Fidelity benefits consultant.

But is that true? Which incentives work, and which ones don't? Employers are experimenting with different models in hopes of improving employees' health, lowering claims costs and getting better returns on their wellness investments.

"Incentives themselves are not the silver bullet," says Stephanie Pronk, health and wellness consultant with Aon Hewitt, the human-resources business unit of Aon PLC. "It's really important to change up the incentive design and keep people on their toes," she says.

What follows is a look at the pros and cons of popular kinds of corporate wellness incentive programs being used today.

Educational/Awareness Incentives

THE PLAN: Companies offer rewards for completing activities that include an assessment of their personal health and risk factors. These can range from filling out a questionnaire about family medical history, diet and fitness routine, to taking a biometric screening for cholesterol, blood pressure and other factors. Incentives worth at least $100 are needed to get about 75% of employees to participate, says Lockton Benefit Group. Smaller rewards than that tend to motivate in the range of only 30% to 50%, experts say.

TYPICAL ANNUAL VALUE: $120 for a health-risk assessment; $130 for biometric screening

PROS: Experts say the simple activities are a good way to introduce employees to healthy behavior and their own risk factors. Companies can also use the data to figure out what their workers' health needs are.

CONS: Telling people about their risks and giving them advice won't necessarily lead them to take action.

EXAMPLE: Caterpillar Inc. reduces premiums by $75 a month for employees who complete a health-risk assessment; up to 90% of eligible employees participate, according to the National Business Coalition on Health.

Action-Based Incentives

THE PLAN: To earn rewards, and sometimes avoid penalties, employees are required to take action to improve their health after going through a risk assessment, such as joining a weight-management program or getting a preventive screening.

TYPICAL ANNUAL VALUE: Rewards of $200 to $350 for assessment followed by action

PROS: The model motivates employees to take steps to change their unhealthy behaviors, Ms. Pronk says.

CONS: The incentives don't encourage healthy behavior beyond the completion of the required programs.

EXAMPLE: Houston city employees must complete three tasks in order to avoid a $25 monthly payroll surcharge: fill out a health-risk assessment, take a biometric screening and either talk to a health coach, sign up for a program like Weight Watchers or get a screening such as a mammogram. As a result, 90% of employees have completed three of the tasks or more.

Everything-But-The-Kitchen-Sink Incentives

THE PLAN: Companies offer incentives for an array of tasks; the more complex and beneficial the task, the bigger the reward.

TYPICAL ANNUAL VALUE: Generally $25 to $125 per task

PROS: Employees like the freedom to choose their health activities, from bike rides to self-defense classes, rather than having to talk to a nurse or participate in an official program to get the reward, say experts. A wellness program that lays out a tempting trail to follow will have more success keeping people involved, Ms. Pronk says.

CONS: Too many options can overwhelm employees, and companies can waste money on programs that won't effectively address workers' biggest health problems.

EXAMPLE: JetBlue Airways Corp. offers as much as $400 for a range of activities from teeth cleaning ($25) to completing an Ironman triathlon ($400).

Progress-Based Incentives

THE PLAN: Employers offer rewards for taking steps to hit optimal benchmarks for cholesterol, blood pressure and weight. Steps can include enrolling in a weight-management program and reducing body mass index.

TYPICAL ANNUAL VALUE: $100 for reaching a healthy weight

PROS: Employees are financially motivated to improve their health, instead of getting penalized for not being perfect, says Paul Terry, CEO of StayWell Health Management, which runs corporate wellness programs.

CONS: Rewarding employees for simply showing up to a wellness program doesn't mean they will actually get healthier, and there's a long way to go from a "morbidly obese" body mass index of 40 to a healthy under-25.

EXAMPLE: At insurer Florida Blue, workers can earn rewards of up to $500. The firm's total staff with five or more health-risk factors has decreased by half, to only 11%, since 2009; those with two risks or fewer now make up 59%, up from 41%.

Outcome-Based Incentives

THE PLAN: Companies tie incentives and penalties to health metrics, with workers paying more until they hit ideal cholesterol, blood pressure and body mass index levels; 41% of employers use this model, a survey by Fidelity and the National Business Group on Health found.

TYPICAL ANNUAL VALUE: $100 for achieving a healthy weight; $125 for hitting other biometric targets; $200 for quitting tobacco. (According to Fidelity's survey, employers don't penalize employees for not achieving results, but reward those who do with lower premiums, cash or deposits into their health savings or flexible savings account.)

PROS: Experts say this model is effective at making people objectively improve their health: Participants in a recent Mayo Clinic study lost nine pounds on average when they received $20 per month for meeting weight-loss goals (or paid $20 when they didn't); participants who didn't receive incentives lost only two pounds on average.

CONS: Critics believe the model could be discriminatory and decrease access to health care. Legally, employers must offer workers who don't hit targets an alternative way to earn the incentive, such as a doctor's note or program participation, so experts say employees might never reach the goal.

EXAMPLE: Furniture company KI sorts workers into four tiers of premiums based on biometrics, with the healthiest people paying $1,000 less (or $1,500 for a family) than the riskiest.

Targeted Incentives

THE PLAN: Companies crunch employees' health-risk, claims and other data -- or have the insurer or an analytics company do it -- to offer personalized wellness programs and incentives.

TYPICAL ANNUAL VALUE: $100 to $125 for targeted programs

PROS: Experts say that personalizing wellness programs boosts participation among those who will benefit the most.

CONS: Some workers feel personalized interventions are intrusive and compromise privacy.

EXAMPLE: Johnson & Johnson employees get $500 off annual premiums for submitting a health profile; the company then uses the data to offer an additional $100 to $250 for participating in activities tailored to the employee, such as a diabetes management program. The company says its return on investment is up to $4 for every dollar it spends on employee wellness.

---

Ms. Wieczner is a reporter for MarketWatch in New York. She can be reached at jwieczner@marketwatch.com.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Russian reforms prove a lure; Fund focus

By Nick Reeve 
566 words
8 April 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A Jupiter manager expects a wave of investment in domestic markets, writes Nick Reeve

The Russian equity market is undergoing landmark reforms that could make it a far more attractive bet for investors, according to the manager of the 287.8m Jupiter Emerging European Opportunities fund.

Elena Shaftan says a wave of new liquidity could flood into Russian stock markets in the next few months as authorities put in place equity trading reforms that will bring its stock exchange up to international standards in terms of speed of trading and regulation.

The country's two main exchanges, RTS and Micex , have merged, and President Vladimir Putin has voiced a desire to float more state-owned companies on public markets.

Ms Shaftan, who has 18 years' experience of the region's markets, says she has built up her investment in Russian companies to roughly two-thirds of the Jupiter Emerging European Opportunities portfolio.

In a sign of confidence, she also says she is focusing on less liquid small and mid-cap stocks rather than more "defensive" large-cap names.

She says that Russia is experiencing a "change of culture" in terms of attitude to financial markets as it seeks to move away from its reliance on oil exports, adding that people are more appreciative of capital markets as a "road to personal wealth".

"The introduction of a central depositary [for settlement of trades] opens Russia up to the wider market," Ms Shaftan says.

"We don't know how far the reforms will go, but so far it has certainly made people stop and think. To diversify away from oil, Russia needs to attract foreign investment, and it needs to demonstrate the environment is safe."

Russian authorities are also seeking to change the rules on how pension funds invest. These funds do not have high levels of equity investment, as the managers are forced to make good any losses experienced through investing in risk assets.

But proposed reforms to this law aim to drastically increase Russian pension funds' investment in the domestic markets, which would create what Ms Shaftan refers to as a "cushion of domestic liquidity". As much as $50bn could come in to the market in the next few years if the reforms go ahead as planned, she says.

President Putin's reforms also include a requirement for Russian companies to pay 25 per cent of their profits as dividends to shareholders.

Ms Shaftan's bias towards small and mid-cap companies has hurt the performance of her fund in recent years. In the five-year period to March 25, the fund lost 16 per cent, according to FE Analytics, while its benchmark, the MSCI EM Europe 10/40 index, gained 6 per cent.

Shorter-term performance, however, has been much stronger, and Ms Shaftan says she has seen "signs of change" in the past three months. In the six months to March 25, the fund gained 12.9 per cent compared with the index's gain of 7.9 per cent.

"When people look for safety, they look for index-hugging and liquidity, for example exchange traded funds," Ms Shaftan says.

"When risk appetite improves, investors start to look at fundamentals, and that often leads them towards small and mid-cap companies."

Nick Reeve is a senior reporter at Investment Adviser

Special
The Experts: What Lessons Can Investors Learn From Warren Buffett?

2,381 words
7 April 2013
21:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

What lessons can investors learn from Warren Buffett's investment strategy and history? The Wall Street Journal put this question to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics from the print Report. This question relates to a "http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887324735304578354753422652608.html" on the increasing difficulty of getting some personal time with Warren Buffett at Berkshire Hathaway's annual shareholders' meeting and formed the basis of a discussion in The Experts stream on Friday, April 5.

The Experts will discuss topics raised in this month's Investing in Funds & ETFs Report and other Wall Street Journal Reports. Find the finance Experts online at "http://online.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-wealth.html?mg=inert-wsj"

Also "http://on.wsj.com/14CDLEj" three of The Experts Christian Magoon ("https://twitter.com/ChristianMagoon") of Magoon Capital, Rick Ferri ("https://twitter.com/Rick_Ferri") of Portfolio Solutions and Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") of IndexUniverse speak about investing in index funds and ETFs in a live video chat that aired on Monday, April 8 at 3 p.m. EDT.

Matt Hougan: Invest in Things. Don't Rent.

Don't sell. Invest in things; don't rent them for speculative returns.

There are caveats to that, of course: If you're tax-loss harvesting, or if the investment landscape shifts, or if your initial thesis (that you wrote down when you first invested) breaks down.

But holding periods for stocks now average less than a year. Fifty years ago, they were about seven years. We were better off then.

Matt Hougan ("https://twitter.com/Matt_Hougan") is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

Sheryl Garrett: Keep It Real

Don't try to be fancy. Keep it real. Focus on the business aspects of any investment. Research thoroughly. Trust few. Avoid leverage, and be patient and willing to invest forever. And, if you don't have the time or interest to do all of this with a great deal of confidence, Warren Buffett himself would recommend that we simply invest in index funds.

Sheryl Garrett ("https://twitter.com/SherylGarrett") is founder of the Garrett Planning Network Inc.

Eleanor Blayney: Invest Like a Woman

According to a recent book on the Oracle of Omaha, Warren Buffett "invests like a girl." While it would be more politically correct to say that Mr. Buffett "invests like a woman," the point remains: His investment style and method share many characteristics that research has shown are more prevalent among women investors. These include patience, ability to learn from mistakes and investing only in what he understands.

There's a take-away here particularly for women, who tend to be reluctant, anxious investors or think they do not know enough to invest. You've got what it takes! You are more like Warren Buffett than you might think! It is imperative for you to get investing to ensure your financial security over your longer lives. (This is a message for all Americans, but women in particular need the support and encouragement to become confident investors.)

Eleanor Blayney ("https://twitter.com/EleanorBlayney") is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards.

Tom Brakke: Don't Get Swept Up By the Crowd

No name gets dropped quite as much as Warren Buffett's by those writing about investments, including me. As an indication, The Experts were given a question that referenced him just last month.

The infatuation with Mr. Buffett is understandable given his success and his folksy manner. But investors should be aware that he plays a different game than the rest of us. He gets the first call on deals and he gets attractive terms on his investments. We can't replicate those advantages.

Another thing to keep in mind is how much Mr. Buffett's strategy has changed over time. Whereas most investors are urged to adopt a plan and stick to it, Mr. Buffett showed that different times and different circumstances mean that core principles should be examined and adapted as necessary.

Charlie Munger was the key person in Mr. Buffett's evolution. There are good lessons from that: It's important to listen to other perspectives, and a dialogue like Mr. Buffett and Mr. Munger have had over the years has gotten them to places that neither could have gone on his own.

Mr. Buffett has said that his favorite holding period for an investment is "forever." That's a welcome departure from the short-term thinking that pervades much of the investment business. You should not, however, make the mistake of thinking that anything that Mr. Buffett holds is worth owning. Many investors did that in the '90s, buying Coca-Cola and other stocks because Buffett owned them, even though they were trading at historic levels of overvaluation. The results weren't good. So don't cherry-pick Mr. Buffett's ideas and expect to do well. That's not a great way to emulate him.

The most important lesson that Mr. Buffett can offer anyone: Don't get swept up by the crowd.

For example, he has been willing to sit on a mountain of cash, even though interest rates are extremely low. Most financial advisers would scoff at that, but Mr. Buffett will wait in cash if there aren't compelling values available elsewhere.

And he is willing to be out of step for long periods in order to do well in the end. Most of us have a very hard time doing that.

Tom Brakke ("https://twitter.com/researchpuzzler") is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

Michelle Perry Higgins: Be Fearful When Others Are Greedy, and Greedy Only When Others Are Fearful

The best lesson to me is to not be a part of the herd when it comes to investing. You see the herd mentality when an investor becomes greedy and wants to invest when the particular stock they are watching is at an all-time high or they begin pouring money into the stock market when it's at peak. Then, as soon as the indicator starts to fall, they become fearful and quickly sell at a loss. You would never see Mr. Buffett do that. A patient, long-term value investor is the one that sees the big picture and understands stock-market cycles. He or she will likely not fall into the greed or fear trap. Warren Buffett is a brilliant man and has many lessons that investors should emulate. One of his most valued quotes for me is, "We (must) simply attempt to be fearful when others are greedy and to be greedy only when others are fearful." That is the epitome of breaking away from the herd.

Michelle Perry Higgins ("https://twitter.com/RetirementMPH") is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

Meir Statman: Buffett Teaches Us the Importance of Being a "Mensch"

The most important lessons we can learn from Warren Buffett are about life.

We tend to seek investment lessons from Mr. Buffett. How can we make the most money from our investments? Should we choose value stocks? Or perhaps growth stocks with dominating brands? But what is the money for?

Money offers three kinds of benefits: utilitarian, expressive and emotional. The utilitarian benefits of money are in the financial security it provides to us and our families. The expressive benefits are about what it says about us. Does it say that we drive a Lamborghini or does it say that we can afford a Lamborghini but choose to drive a Toyota? The emotional benefits are about how it makes us feel. Does a pile of money make us feel tall as we stand atop it, or does it make us proud as we contribute it for the benefit of others? Mr. Buffett enjoys the utilitarian benefits of money, providing well for himself and his family, and he enjoys the expressive and emotional benefits of money by giving it away.

Mr. Buffett teaches us that the most important part of being a self-made man is being a "mensch," a decent man. He recognizes the role of community efforts and luck in his success. He also recognizes the role of government in helping people who have not been successful, and the fairness of sharing the fruits of success. Mr. Buffett "believes in fairness," wrote Rana Foroohar following an interview with Mr. Buffett. "He believes in the ability of government to make people's lives better. But most of all, he believes in luck." Mr. Buffett's good fortune, he says, "starts with being born in this country." When asked by Andrew Ross Sorkin if there are any private equity investors he admires, Mr. Buffett "flatly replied: 'No' &When asked if he followed any hedge-fund managers, he struggled to name any &"

Meir Statman is the Glenn Klimek professor of finance at Santa Clara University, and visiting professor at Tilburg University in the Netherlands.

Larry Zimpleman: Take the Long-Term View

I think Warren Buffett and other very successful investors offer the average investor a lot of good tips for their own investment strategy. Some of the concepts I think Mr. Buffett teaches us include:

A. Invest like you want to own the company. If you don't understand what a company does or how it makes money, it's probably not a good investment.

B. Don't outguess yourself if the fundamentals behind the motivation for your investment are still in place, don't worry if the returns aren't performing over the short term. But if the circumstances do change, be willing to act.

C. Probably the most important one is to invest for the long term. Even Warren Buffett doesn't know whether the markets will go up or down over the next month, or even the next year. But the odds that markets will go up over five or 10 years is very high so take the long-term view.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

Rick Ferri: Buffett Hasn't Been Wrong on Index Funds

It would be nice if we could all pick stocks like Warren Buffett. A handful of investors have, but most have not. I don't need to because I own Berkshire Hathaway stock. It's the only individual stock in my portfolio. All other investments are primarily in low-cost index funds.

Warren Buffett would approve of my strategy. He has been a longtime advocate of index-fund investing. In 1996, he wrote in the Berkshire Hathaway annual report that, "Most investors, both institutional and individual, will find that the best way to own common stocks is through an index fund that charges minimal fees. Those following this path are sure to beat the net results of the great majority of investment professionals."

Each year, Mr. Buffet repeats his index-fund endorsement in some fashion or other. "A very low-cost index is going to beat a majority of the amateur-managed money or professionally-managed money," Mr. Buffett said at the 2007 Berkshire Hathaway annual meeting. He hasn't been wrong.

Rick Ferri is founder of "http://www.portfoliosolutions.com/" LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is "http://www.rickferri.com/blog/".

Greg McBride: You Own a Business, Not a Piece of Paper

There are several lessons investors can learn from Warren Buffett. Mr. Buffett espouses the "buy and hold" that many investors have dismissed as out of fashion. Mr. Buffett is also a great example of the importance of minimizing trading to keep investment expenses low and to preserve returns. Investors should also follow Mr. Buffett's lead and recognize equity ownership as owning part of a business and not just owning a piece of paper. Other concepts such as recognizing value, buying what you know and the power of compounding are well illustrated by Mr. Buffett.

Greg McBride ("https://twitter.com/BankrateGreg") is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

Charles Rotblut: Never Panic and Stick to Your Strategy

I recently asked Carol Loomis, who wrote a book on Warren Buffett last year and edits his annual Berkshire Hathaway shareholder letter, a similar question. She noted that in a previous annual report, Mr. Buffett said those people with the aptitude, the interest and the time to decide what securities to invest in can do well. Most others should simply invest in index funds.

I would add two other things.

The first is the importance of never panicking. A large part of Mr. Buffett's success has come from his ability to stay calm and be willing to increase his stockholdings when everyone else is selling. Everyone has heard they should "buy low and sell high," but few people have shown the ability to truly buy low as Mr. Buffett has.

The second is the importance of having a rational, well-thought-out investment strategy and sticking to it. Mr. Buffett has built a fortune by investing in bargain stocks good quality companies that trade at attractive prices. Despite everything else that has evolved over his career, he has kept a laser focus and only tweaked his strategy when there was an overriding reason to do so.

Charles Rotblut ("https://twitter.com/charlesrotblut") is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

Scott Adams: How Hard Could It Be?

When I watch LeBron James jump from the free-throw line, rotate 360 degrees in the air, and dunk a basketball, I always say to myself "Now I know how to do that, too!" It's the same feeling I get from watching Warren Buffett invest. I mean, how hard could it be? As far as I can tell, all you do is make folksy pronouncements and neglect trimming your eyebrows for decades. Mr. Buffett says the market is inefficient and he has opportunities and information not available to the average person, but I haven't been average since LeBron taught me to dunk so that's not an issue for me.

Scott Adams is the creator of the Dilbert comic strip that appears in thousands of newspapers world-wide and "http://www.dilbert.com/".

The Apps Fall Far From Apple's Tree

Google is eating Apple's iPhone from the inside out. Facebook is hugely popular on mobile, but faces a threat from companies that are building their own mobile social networks. Oh, and smartphone users love games. Really love them.

These are just some of the themes that emerge from studying the app charts. These are real-time scoreboards of the top apps by number of downloads and by revenue that are published by the makers of the dominant mobile operating systems, Google and Apple.

The Billboard of the mobile era, the app charts are something all technology investors should follow to understand who is up and who is down in the smartphone age.

There can be no doubt that smartphones have become the dominant computing device. Last year, nearly 700 million of them were shipped world-wide, according to Strategy Analytics, a research firm. That was nearly double IDC's estimate for PC shipments of 352 million. As recently as 2010, PCs were still the bigger market.

No wonder a war is being waged among tech's biggest players to control the mobile experience. On Thursday, Facebook is due to open another front when, together with Taiwan-based handset maker HTC, it is expected to roll out a smartphone with a special Facebook-themed home screen. The hope is to grab even more of mobile users' time. Controlling that will be the key to billions of dollars of advertising and other revenue in the near future.

But Facebook is just one of many players competing for attention. Google and Apple are others. Meanwhile, there are thousands of smaller app developers taking on these giants, and having great success in some cases.

Luckily, investors can see who is winning and losing.

While Apple's App Store and Google's equivalent Google Play store don't disclose the specific number of downloads or the precise amount of revenue for each app, they do provide country-by-country lists of how apps rank on both scores. A useful tool to track this is provided by research firm App Annie, which collects all the data and presents it in ways that otherwise wouldn't be visible, particularly for Google Play.

Important trends emerge when you study the charts. First, people love games: In the U.S., by revenue, nine of the top 10 apps on Google Play are games as are eight of the top 10 apps on iPhone. Candy Crush Saga, where players must quickly arrange brightly colored candies, is No. 1 for both.

Excluding games, Google is actually the most popular publisher of apps on Apple's mobile devices these days. Apps like Google Maps and YouTube are downloaded more frequently than even the apps Apple encourages iPhone users to download when they first buy their device.

Together with popular apps like Chrome, Search and Gmail, it is clear that Google is building a strong connection directly with the users of Apple products. The clear risk for Apple is that as iPhone users grow accustomed to Google's mobile apps, they may more-readily defect to rival devices running Google's Android.

Apple is the No. 2 nongame publisher of apps for iPhones in terms of downloads. But it isn't clear that many are actually using its apps, including Find My Friends, iTunes U and iBooks.

This is a key limitation of the app charts. While Apple and Google publish data on downloads and revenue, they don't disclose how much time users are actually spending inside individual apps.

One app publisher that is having no trouble with user engagement, though, is Facebook. Though its app ranks 60th for the iPhone currently, it sits atop the Google Play store. The reason may be that most iPhone users long ago already downloaded the app, while the Android equivalent, which hasn't been out as long, is still being rapidly adopted by users.

In any case, research firm comScore tracks app usage and says that about a fifth of the time that U.S. smartphone users spent consuming media on their device in February was in Facebook's app. That doesn't include Facebook Messenger, a separate service that handles texting. Nor does it include Instagram, the photo-sharing app that Facebook recently bought. Both of those rank very highly on the U.S. app charts.

Even Facebook's domination of mobile usage is hardly total. Newer apps are grabbing a bigger share of mobile content creation and sharing. Snapchat, which allows users to send photo messages that disappear after a brief time, is currently ranked seventh for iPhone and 20th for Android in the U.S., according to App Annie.

Another app that is growing in popularity is Vine. Published by Twitter, Vine captures six-second videos to share with friends and is currently ranked third in the U.S. for iPhone. Twitter itself was ranked 36th for the iPhone and 10th for Google Play in the U.S. In terms of usage, though, it does quite well, grabbing 1.7% of U.S. smartphone users' time spent in apps in February, according to comScore, good for 11th in their rankings.

Another threat to Facebook could come from apps like WhatsApp Messenger. Ranked among the top paid apps in the U.S. for iPhone, WhatsApp is a popular alternative to standard text-messaging apps that come with smartphones. It is a threat to Facebook because it is rapidly recreating Facebook's key asset, a social network, largely based on users' smartphone address books.

Abroad, you can see how similar messaging apps are turning this into a big business. Developers in Asia are building their games on top of the social networks built by messaging apps. In Korea, for both iPhone and Google Play, nearly all of the top-downloaded and top-grossing apps are built on top of a messenger app called Kakao. The same is true in Japan for an app called Line.

Facebook is capitalizing on the popularity of games: Because it has so much usage within its core service, game publishers pay to promote downloads via the social network's app install ads. Meanwhile, Google's suite of popular apps are also used extensively: Together, its apps accounted for around 7% of app usage in the U.S. in February, according to comScore.

In a sense, the odd-man out here is Apple. Despite the fact that its sleek devices have done so much to make the word "app" commonplace, it has just one truly popular app of its own: iTunes. Yes, having all of your music tied up in one Apple-owned service is a strong tether. But as the sheer diversity of the app rankings suggest, Apple could use more than one popular service to keep customers tied to its mobile devices over the long term.

-- Rolfe Winkler

BOJ Puts Substance in 'Abenomics'

Shinzo Abe and Haruhiko Kuroda want to make believers of us all.

Prime Minister Abe says his bold economic plan, focused on a 2% inflation target, would require a certain amount of audience participation. People would have to, ahem, buy into it for it to work.

Now Mr. Kuroda, at his first meeting as Bank of Japan governor, has provided more solid reasons to believe Japan's leaders are going to do whatever it takes to get the economy humming again.

First, the BOJ has committed to hitting the inflation target in "about two years." Where previously vagueness had prevailed, now there is a clear timeline with which to judge the effectiveness of "Abenomics."

Second, Mr. Kuroda has delivered on high expectations for monetary easing -- and then some. The BOJ said it would double the monetary base and its holdings of Japanese government bonds in two years, and buy JGBs in much longer durations than previously, up to 40 years. The bank also surprised the market by increasing purchases of equity funds and real-estate securities.

With these moves, the BOJ has officially made "qualitative and quantitative easing," or QQE, part of the financial lexicon.

In a symbolic move, it also temporarily scrapped a self-imposed rule that theoretically limited government-bond purchases to the amount of bank notes in circulation.

The immediate response to the BOJ's announcement saw the yen continue its weakening trend and the Nikkei resume its upward march.

But skeptics still will be concerned.

The BOJ's actions are meant to boost asset prices and depress interest rates. That, according to Abenomics, will boost economic activity and demand, spurring growth.

But the risk is that Japan's borrowing costs will rise faster than overall prices. That is a big concern as total government debt is more than 220% of annual gross domestic product. Japan's banks, major holders of JGBs, could find themselves in a precarious position indeed.

Also, though doubts over whether the BOJ can successfully engineer an end to deflation seem misplaced given the plan to print seemingly unlimited amounts of money, much still rests on the need for important structural changes to raise the real, underlying rate of Japan's economic growth.

To that end, a package of pro-growth measures is expected in June. These must include, for instance, policies to boost the labor force, deregulate potential growth sectors such as health care, and improve corporate governance by forcing more independent directors on corporate boards.

The BOJ's announcement has put some concrete measures behind the government's ambitious targets. But until Mr. Abe delivers on the important structural reform leg of his plans, Abenomics remains a leap into the unknown.

-- Aaron Back and Duncan Mavin

Telefonica's Hang-Ups

On Debt Are Receding

Telefonica shareholders can sleep a little easier. The Spanish telecom firm's net debt, which peaked at 56.3 billion euros ($72.3 billion) in 2011, is expected to fall below 47 billion euros this year. That should bring leverage to under a targeted 2.35 times Ebitda, close to an average of two times for its big European peers. But there is renewed talk of asset sales that, if they materialize, could reduce Telefonica's leverage even further.

Business Technology: CIO Journal

633 words
4 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Major League Baseball

Tests Seat Upgrades

Major League Baseball franchises are taking a page from the airline industry playbook, testing a mobile app that allows their most loyal customers to upgrade their seats during the middle of a game.

As the season kicks off, the Atlanta Braves, Oakland Athletics, Minnesota Twins and Arizona Diamondbacks will begin sending fans mobile offers at the start of games, pitching empty seats for a fee or as a reward for customer loyalty.

Franchises are concerned that last-minute upgrades could discourage advance sales of those higher-priced seats. "You'd hate to give a first-class upgrade to someone who might otherwise buy a first-class ticket," said Joe Inzerillo, chief technology officer for Major League Baseball Advanced Media.

The franchises already collect a wealth of data on fans attending games through the app "At the Ballpark," the platform for the new upgrade program. Since last season, fans have been able to use the app to "check in" at games.

Beyond filling empty seats, MLB hopes that offering someone a chance to sit in better seats will give them a taste of the experience and push them to buy pricier tickets in the future.

-- Joel Schectman

Rosetta Stone to Steer

Consumers to the Cloud

Language learning company Rosetta Stone Inc. on Tuesday said it acquired Livemocha for $8.5 million. While Rosetta Stone has made its mark selling language courses through CDs offered at yellow kiosks in malls and airports around the world, Livemocha's language courses are only offered online.

Much as Oracle Corp. last year acquired RightNow Technologies Inc. to ease its entry into cloud computing, Rosetta Stone is making this acquisition as much for the technology underpinning its smaller online learning rival as for the customer base it hopes to monetize in the near future.

Rosetta Stone CEO Steve Swad has been "on a mission for the last 18 months to accelerate our transition from being a language learning company to being a learning company, and from a CD-based company to cloud-based company. This transaction is foundational in moving faster in that direction."

While 99% of Rosetta Stone's business customers access the company's products over the cloud, the majority of its customers on the consumer side still buy CD versions of its products.

In addition to providing improved functionality, the acquisition will allow Rosetta Stone to cull usage and socio-demographic data from Livemocha, helping to identify other courses which the company could begin offering.

-- Michael Hickins

Amid Euro-Zone Crisis,

ING Tries Building Trust

With European consumers rattled by the continuing Euro-Zone Crisis, Dutch banking giant ING Groep NV is using technology to build trust with its customers.

The bank is harnessing traditional analytics and database software, and experimenting with Big Data, to do simple things that aren't all that simple to accomplish, but which could translate to higher customer loyalty -- such as helping customers avoid overdraft fees by suggesting fund transfers from one account to another.

The bank is also using predictive analytics based on historical transaction records, tracking the frequency of customer contact, the relevance of each contact, as well as emotional relevance to get a sense of what customers value.

After ING discovered through analytics that customers dropped out of the online loan application process because they didn't want to print the completed form, the bank created a system that alerts an ING agent, who calls the customer and offers to print the form and send it to them.

-- Clint Boulton

Abreast of the Market
U.S. Stock Rally Hits Brakes --- Weaker-Than-Expected Economic Data Damp Investor Sentiment

By Alexandra Scaggs 
732 words
4 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
21
English
Copyright  2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Stocks slipped, as investors hit pause after a pair of disappointing economic readings from the U.S. and ahead of two key global monetary-policy announcements.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average lost 58 points, or 0.4%, to 14605.

The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index shed nine points, or 0.6%, to 1561, and the Nasdaq Composite Index pulled back 15 points, or 0.5%, to 3239.

On Tuesday, the Dow and the S&P 500 both rose to record highs for the second time in three sessions.

"The rally's leadership is limited to a smaller and smaller number of names," said Julius Ridgway, investment adviser at Medley & Brown LLC, in Jackson, Miss. "It seems to be getting a little narrower."

The Russell 2000, an index of small stocks, slid more steeply than other benchmarks, pulling back 1%.

Stocks extended losses after the Institute for Supply Management's reading on the U.S. nonmanufacturing sector came in worse than expected -- slipping to its lowest level since August 2012 -- and employment slowed as well.

A disappointing report on private-sector jobs growth for March also cast a pall on Tuesday's optimism. Data compiled by Automatic Data Processing and Moody'sAnalytics showed an increase of 158,000 jobs on the month, well below the 192,000 expected.

The measure is seen as a preview to Friday's closely watched employment report from the Bureau of Labor Statistics.

"Investors are trying to interpret all of this economic data. At the same time, they're looking for the opportunity to take a bit of a breather" after the latest rally, said Joseph Tanious, global market strategist at J.P. Morgan Funds.

But Mr. Tanious remains positive on the U.S. economy. "We would view the selloff to be a buying opportunity," he said.

Next on investors' radars will be monetary-policy decisions abroad. The European Central Bank and the Bank of Japan are both slated to announce policy moves on Thursday.

"All eyes will be focused on what both of those central banks are going to do in their efforts to stimulate economic growth . . . their actions may dominate" market action, Mr. Tanious said.

Zynga rallied 14% ahead of the rollout of two real-money casino and poker games in the next few days.

U.S.-listed shares of Vodafone pulled back 4% after Verizon Communications said it doesn't plan to merge with or make a bid for the U.K.-based telecommunications company. Verizon shares pulled back 1.3%.

Treasury prices got a boost from the weaker-than-expected economic reports. The benchmark 10-year note gained 9/32 in price to yield 1.830%.

European Stocks

Shares slid in the wake of the soft U.S. ISM and jobs figures and as investors took to the sidelines ahead of monetary-policy announcements by the ECB and the Bank of England.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index slid 0.9% to close at 294.80, partly erasing a 1.3% jump from Tuesday.

Delhaize Group tumbled 4.9% after HSBC cut the supermarket operator to "underweight" from "neutral."

Telecom firms were also in the downgrading spotlight, with Telecom Italia off 5.4% after UBS cut the firm to "sell" from "buy." France Telecom lost 4.4% after the bank reduced the rating to "sell" from "neutral."

Royal Bank of Scotland Group lost 4.4% on news the bank and its former executives are to be sued by 12,000 individuals and institutions. The group alleges that they were misled about the bank's financial health during a 2008 share sale.

Mining firms dropped in the U.K. after UBS downgraded the materials sector to "neutral" from "overweight." Shares of BHP Billiton and Rio Tinto both declined 2%. Metals prices were also lower across the board.

AXA retreated 1.9% in Paris as HSBC Holdings said that it agreed to sell its Singapore insurance business to the French insurer. HSBC fell 1.4%.

U.K. utility SSE managed to close 0.3% higher, despite being fined GBP 10.5 million ($15.9 million) for misselling products. This was the largest ever punishment issued by the U.K.'s energy-industry regulator.

Markets
Fewer IPO Deals, but a Bigger Take

By Chris Dieterich 
761 words
3 April 2013
17:57
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Companies going public on U.S. exchanges raised 29% more funds in the first quarter than in the year-ago period, and most of those that launched managed big gains, a trend that bankers said bodes well for deal activity in the months ahead.

The first quarter ushered in 25 IPOs that together raised $7.5 billion, according to data provider Dealogic. But the soaring stock market hasn't set off a burst in the number of initial public offerings, with the quarter seeing 14 fewer deals than last year.

Several factors, including a sluggish U.S. economy and Washington's fiscal uncertainty, helped keep a lid on the number of IPOs. Also, bankers said companies have watched for sustained appetite on the part of investors for new deals before putting IPO plans in gear.

"One might think IPOs would be more robust with the relatively strong market performance and low volatility," said Scott Cutler, head of global listings at NYSE Euronext. "Conditions have been stronger than the reality, but the window is open," Mr. Cutler said.

The first quarter's largest deal was Zoetis Inc., the animal-health unit of drug maker Pfizer Inc., which raised $2.57 billion in the largest deal to hit U.S. exchanges since Facebook's $16 billion debut in May. Leisure-cruise operator Norwegian Cruise Line Holdings Ltd., frozen-foods purveyor Pinnacle Foods Inc., and master limited partnership CVR Refining LP all raised more than $500 million apiece. All fetched prices at the high end of expectations and notched double-digit-percentage stock gains.

"We've seen a fewer number of IPOs than last year, but the deals are larger and experiencing more favorable pricing outcomes and strong aftermarket performance," said Liz Myers, head of global equity capital markets at J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.

Many other IPOs were well-received. Building-products company Boise Cascade Co., three-dimensional printer maker ExOne Co., child-care-service provider Bright Horizons Family Solutions Inc. and international money-transfer service Xoom Corp. all climbed at least 40%.

More than half of the quarter's IPO volume derived from offshoots of existing companies or large firms taken to market by private-equity owners. That trend is likely to continue, bankers said, with the largest potential IPOs likely to come from private-equity firms, including biopharmaceutical-analytics provider Quintiles Transnational Holdings Inc., tech hardware and software seller CDW Corp. and builder Taylor Morrison Home Corp. Each is pegged to launch deals valued at upward of $500 million. Also circling the runway to go public is Blackhawk Network Holdings Inc., Safeway Inc.'s gift-card unit.

"There's been success with sponsor-backed companies going public, particularly those focused on the housing recovery and consumer spending," said Frank Maturo, vice chairman for equity capital markets at Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch. "And things are starting to pick up with this trend of IPOs from carve-outs and spinoffs."

Noticeably absent in the first quarter were technology IPOs, the sector that tends to generate the highest proportion of deals. Just five tech IPOs launched in the first period, raising a total of $765 million, according to Ipreo, a market research firm. In the first quarter of 2012, 14 tech deals made up nearly a quarter of offerings and raised $1.88 billion. The U.S. maintained its position atop the global IPO leader board for total deal volume, after taking in the most in 2012.

In Asia, a quiet start to the year saw 10 new listings raise $1.05 billion in the first quarter in Hong Kong, the top global venue for IPOs from 2009 to 2011. Hong Kong has a heavy backlog, however, with companies including brokerage China Galaxy Securities Co. eyeing a $1.5 billion IPO in the coming quarter.

Europe nearly doubled the amount of funds raised through IPOs in the first quarter compared with last year, with 19 deals valued at $4.9 billion. The biggest deal came from German real-estate company LEG Immobilien AG, a $1.6 billion debut in Frankfurt. This week, upscale Italian notebook maker Moleskine SpA is slated to raise about $650 million with an offering in Milan.

Telis Demos contributed to this article.

Corrections & Amplifications Blackhawk Network Holdings Inc., Safeway Inc.'s gift-card unit, recently registered for an initial public offering. A previous version of this article incorrectly referred to the company as Nighthawk Network Holdings Inc.

 Big data  co SiSense raises $10m; SiSense said that it would use the proceeds "to meet intense demand for its solution." The company's Prism solution operates in the area of 0.5-100 terabytes of data.

Big data analytics company SiSense Ltd. has raised $10 million in its second financing round. Battery Ventures led the round, joined by current investors, Genesis Partners and Opus Capital. SiSense said that it would use the proceeds "to meet intense demand for its solution."

SiSense has raised $23 million since it was founded in 2004 by CPO Elad Israeli and CTO Eldad Farkash. SiSense says that its subscription revenue rose 520% in the past year, and that it has won customers in 49 countries.

SiSense CEO Amit Bendov said, "We are experiencing exponential sales growth and incredible buzz in the market now it's time to add oil to the fire. Our biggest challenge right now is growing the sales force and the support teams quickly enough to keep up with the demand."

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Custodians respond to stronger focus on price; Controlling costs

By Brian Bollen 
823 words
1 April 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
15
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Market forces as much as legal actions have made their mark, writes Brian Bollen

Transaction cost analysis (TCA) is going the same way in the foreign exchange markets as it did in the equities market. This will mean greater transparency, increased efficiencies in trading and greater competitiveness in pricing.

This forecast comes from Jim Cochrane, who started his professional life flying US military intelligence aircraft and is now director of analytics at New York-based Investment Technology Group.

TCA will assist clients beyond the requirements of regulatory compliance. Their attention will turn away from the question of whether they have benefited from the best execution available and towards implementation shortfall and process improvement, he adds.

TCA has been the topic of much discussion in recent years, not least because of the launch of a series of legal actions against custodian banks by US pension funds.

In the vanguard was Edmund G Brown Jr, attorney-general of California at the time. In 2009, he alleged that State Street had made excessive profits from FX transactions arising from the day-to-day servicing needs of assets belonging to the California Public Employees' Retirement System . State Street says it is vigorously defending the litigation.

Virginia and Florida filed similar suits against Bank of New York Mellon. Virginia and BNYM settled out of court on undisclosed terms in November 2012 after a judge had earlier dismissed their claim while leaving the door open for the state to refile. Virginia and BNYM subsequently renewed their relationship for another five years, the bank insisting that areas of dispute should be resolved through commercial negotiations rather than through litigation of baseless claims. "We will continue to defend ourselves where appropriate," BNYM said.

These actions traced their roots to 2003, when Calpers, the world's largest pension fund, appointed currency risk specialist Record Currency Management to analyse foreign exchange transactions. A later paper by Record, FX Transaction Costs - Plugging the Leakage in Returns , said the extent to which custodian banks profited from foreign exchange activity seemed excessive to most, especially when they failed to provide proper disclosure of actual profits. The substance of the paper remains relevant today, roughly 21 months since it was first published, says James Wood-Collins, chief executive of Record.

A year or two later something seems to have changed for the better in terms of custodian behaviour in pricing foreign exchange transactions, if anecdotal evidence can be relied upon. Mr Cochrane, for example, describes custodians as "having come inside the daily rate", whereas previously they operated some distance outside it.

This could, it must be said, owe as much to market forces as to legal actions. Torben Kaaber, chief executive of Saxo Capital Markets UK, observes that there has always been a premium associated with institutional FX trading and that continues to be the case.

However, whereas a decade ago there were very few platforms available to handle institutional requirements and going through a prime broker was a Hobson's choice, now high speed and highly sophisticated platforms are available in the market and offer a more cost-effective solution. "As a result, we see outsourcing of trading requirements - particularly in the FX space - very much as the direction of travel," he says.

The markets have moved strongly to execute online, using single-dealer platforms, multi-dealer platforms and aggregators, adds Mr Wood-Collins. As these platforms represent an increasing share of the total market they are creating a rich data set of online prices, he says. This, in turn, highlights poor practice. It very quickly becomes clear who is charging more than they ought.

Custodians have, without exception, responded to the challenges they face, offering more models for execution. They are offering clients the option of, for example, accepting the market price at the time of trading or a predetermined spread over a predetermined benchmark, Mr Wood-Collins states. Toby Cole, managing director in the FX business at RBS Markets, says that a spread of one basis point is not unrealistic, depending on the currency involved and the volume of the trade.

The heightened focus on price has also been driven by the prevailing economic conditions of recent years. Investors who may have been accustomed to annual returns around or in excess of double digits will find, suggests Mr Cole, that cutting a few basis points off their foreign exchange transaction costs can improve their Sharpe ratio significantly.

Tips for Investors

ITG's Jim Cochrane lists a number of ways investors can ensure the institutions with which they are executing FX transactions deliver the best deal.  " Ask for a time stamp  " If the custodian refuses one, cap the rate; insist on nothing beyond the high and low of the day  " Instruct the custodian to stay within a certain spread above the market rate at the time of the trade
