ECB May Hold Key to Debt Labyrinth

The European Central Bank could help solve Greece's debt puzzle. Talks with private bondholders on a "voluntary" deal to cut 100 billion euros ($129.3 billion) off Greece's debt have faltered, with banks arguing they have made the best offer they possibly can. It is increasingly likely that euro-zone governments will need to take a loss, too, to make Greece's debt profile look sustainable. A move to restructure the ECB's estimated 45 billion euros-50 billion euros holding of Greek bonds could unlock the process.

The ECB isn't involved in the Greek private-sector debt-restructuring process, which covers some 200 billion euros of debt, as it is an official body. It bought bonds to try to stabilize the market rather than as an investment. It has powerful arguments for why it needs to avoid losses. First, it would make it impossible for the ECB to accept Greek paper as collateral for lending to banks, cutting the Greek banking system adrift and effectively ejecting Greece from the euro -- a potential disaster scenario. Second, it would put an end to any further ECB bond purchases, removing a key tool that has proved effective in damping the crisis in Spain and Italy.

But if the ECB insists its bonds are paid off in full, that could strengthen the hand of other investors in holding out against a voluntary deal. Greece might then have to introduce and use so-called collective-action clauses in the bonds to force holdouts to participate. In that case, it is difficult to see how the ECB could extricate itself. While creditors with differing claims can be treated separately, creditors holding the same bond should be treated equally.

One idea is for the ECB to swap its bonds at the price at which it bought them for paper issued by the European Financial Stability Facility. The EFSF could then tender these bonds into the swap, with the government guarantors of the EFSF bearing the losses. After all, these governments would need to recapitalize the ECB if it took a significant loss anyway. But this way, the ECB balance sheet would remain intact.

Greece would get extra debt relief of up to 25 billion euros, potentially smoothing the path of a second bailout. More private holders of bonds might prove willing to agree to the "voluntary" deal because if government entities took losses, it would be harder to argue they should be treated differently. Fears of higher losses for holders of bonds from other countries where the ECB has been buying -- Ireland, Italy, Portugal and Spain -- could also recede. That could encourage buyers to return to these markets.

Politically, euro-zone governments would have to countenance upfront losses -- a huge problem. But the cost of a Greek deal falling apart is likely to be much higher.

-- Richard Barley

BlackBerry Revival Requires Sure Touch

For Research In Motion, this weekend was all about research in demotion.

Unfortunately for investors, the departure of co-chief executives Mike Lazaridis and Jim Balsillie came with no obvious shift in the BlackBerry maker's strategy. Ultimately, to save the company, new CEO Thorsten Heins may have to exit from handsets completely.

With a change at the top, investors were excited the door might even be open for a possible sale of the company. Yet Mr. Heins said he doesn't plan a big break with the past.

Having stuck to keyboard-based BlackBerrys, RIM can't compete with touch-based devices from the likes of Apple and Samsung. As those two have separated from the pack, the smartphone business has become a two-horse race, says analyst Neil Mawston of Strategy Analytics.

Like his predecessors, Mr. Heins is staking the company's future on a new operating system. Originally expected in its smartphones early this year, RIM now says not to expect those phones until the end of 2012. That launch date would be nearly six years behind the first iPhone.

RIM hopes its new operating system will reinvigorate its products and perhaps appeal to other handset makers interested in licensing it. Yet two mobile operating systems, Apple's iOS and Google's Android, already dominate the market.

Even if RIM doesn't want to sell itself, there is still hope for shareholders. Network outages aside, the company tends to deliver email more efficiently than rivals through its proprietary servers.

Subscription fees to access this network generate a bit more than $3 a share of earnings per year, estimates Jefferies Research analyst Peter Misek, while handsets now contribute little to no profit. Of course, if Blackberry devices disappear, so will this revenue stream. The solution, argues Mr. Misek, could be to offer the BlackBerry email experience to iPhone and Android users for a fee.

In that scenario, RIM might have to scale back its handset business. It would be a risky path. Yet it may grow increasingly appealing if RIM continues to slide.

-- Rolfe Winkler

UniCredit

Has Started

To Right Ship

Fortune favors the brave -- or desperate. Two weeks ago, UniCredit's efforts to raise 7.5 billion euros ($9.7 billion) of new capital looked to be going nowhere. Despite pricing the new shares at a deep discount, shares in the Italian bank fell nearly 50% in the first week after the terms were announced. Yet they have since rallied, and investors are back in the black, allowing for the effect of the new shares. Barring a euro disaster, UniCredit should now raise its money. But don't expect its success to spark a wave of deals.

UniCredit shares are still down 35% since it first said it planned to raise capital in November after the European Banking Authority stress test revealed a large capital hole. Since the start of January, more than 20% of the share register has changed hands. But investors who bought the rights to buy new shares at their low point two weeks ago have made nearly five times their money. Given how far UniCredit has underperformed European banks, the rally could have further to run.

So this should be a golden moment for other banks to tap markets. The EBA stress test revealed a 115 billion euro capital shortfall across the European banking sector. But most banks say they can meet their capital-ratio targets via deleveraging -- or shedding assets. Investors don't want to put capital into banks that can't deliver an economic return on equity under new regulatory rules.

Besides, most banks are too busy putting together full-year results to pull together the up-to-date financial data for a rights issue. The next opportunity might be around the time of first-quarter results in May. At that point, bankers anticipate a number of institutions could be tempted to take their chances, including Deutsche Bank, Societe Generale and some of the medium-size Spanish and Italian groups. But that depends on markets retaining their current optimism -- and bank boards feeling bold.

-- Simon Nixon

Overheard: AIG Raps Earnings Hopes

In 2008, AIG's near-collapse hit financial markets like a hurricane. Four years later, it is providing a tailwind for earnings growth.

S&P 500 companies are on track to boost earnings 9.5% year-on-year in the fourth quarter, according to FactSet. While under recent double-digit improvements, that's hardly a catastrophe. But AIG juices the numbers. It is expected to post earnings of 62 cents a share, versus a year-ago loss of $16.20. Strip that out, and the S&P 500's earnings-growth rate falls to just 2.2%. Meanwhile, the share of firms missing earnings estimates is the highest this cycle. Analysts have gone from expecting 14% earnings growth for 2012 back in July to about 9.6% now. And that may still be too high. According to Barclays Capital, the high ratio of quarterly earnings misses so far points to a further slide in full-year forecasts. Stocks will need another handhold to continue their climb.

Cure for Japan Rests

With Europe's Healing

The gloom over Japan has been bad, and it should have been even gloomier.

The central-bank survey of business sentiment, the Tankan, shows that Japan is in worse shape than most expected. The strong yen -- and weak euro -- are hurting exports, as is the fallout from floods in Thailand, which has buckled supply chains barely recovered from disruption after the March earthquake.

Worse still, big corporations said they now plan to increase capital expenditures far less than most economists had expected in the fiscal year through March. Spending on plant and equipment accounts for about 15% of the economy, so a dip is bound to hurt. Companies small and large say profits will fall nearly 5% this year, while almost all expect prices for their goods and services to decline in the next three months, after worsening in the previous quarter, too.

All eyes are on the Bank of Japan and the government in Tokyo to see whether they can arrest the slide.

Certainly, the nation's $154 billion reconstruction package will support the economy early next year. The central bank can ease monetary policy a bit more, too, by increasing its asset purchases and upping buys of Japanese government bonds to lift liquidity in the market and keep borrowing rates low.

But there is little reason to think BOJ measures will have much impact. The bank's recent efforts have done little to limit the rise of the yen.

Getting Japan up and running will be possible only with a turnaround beyond the nation's borders. If the European Union can resolve its sovereign-debt crisis, that could stabilize the yen-euro exchange rate and also potentially drive demand for Japanese exports. That is a tall order. In the meantime, clouds over Japan's economy show little sign of lifting.

-- James Simms

Call It Research in Demotion

Research In Motion shareholders are hanging up on the stock. Perhaps for good reason.

The BlackBerry maker's shares have fallen about 74% this year to date and are off 19% since the company said two weeks ago that it was taking a $485 million provision against the value of its disastrous Playbook tablets. Investors are realizing that, even though the stock currently fetches just 3.5 times this fiscal year's expected earnings, it could easily go far lower.

History hasn't been kind to handset makers whose devices go out of style. Palm racked up operating losses of nearly $400 million the two years before Hewlett-Packard mercifully scooped it up for $1.2 billion. After the RAZR fad fizzled, Motorola's handset business had operating losses of over $5 billion from 2007 through 2010.

RIM seems on a similar path as it loses market share. In the third quarter of 2010, RIM had 15% share of the smartphone market, estimates Strategy Analytics. That fell to 10% in this year's third quarter.

And those share losses are set to continue. On top of handset problems, the firm's reputation for offering a bullet-proof corporate-email service took a hammering from October's widespread network outage. Poor sales of the Playbook mean the company isn't competitive in tablets. Morgan Stanley projects sales of recent smartphone models could disappoint. And, perhaps most worrisome, is the late release of its new BlackBerry 10 operating system.

It is hard to say how cheap is too cheap for the shares. Goldman Sachs analyst Simona Jankowski argued in a November note to clients that on a "sum-of-the-parts" basis the company is worth some $9.6 billion, about 21% above the current valuation.

But such analyses make aggressive assumptions. For starters, it is notoriously difficult to estimate the value of patents. Goldman estimates RIM's patents are worth a tidy $3.6 billion. Second, Goldman puts at $4.6 billion the present value of cash flows from RIM's highly profitable service business, which charges for routing messages through its own servers.

One hope is that a buyer will emerge. But it isn't clear who would step up.

One long-rumored acquirer is Microsoft, which has struggled to break into the mobile market. RIM is strong among enterprise customers, Microsoft's sweet spot. Plus, buying RIM would give Microsoft the ability to control both smartphone hardware and software in the future. Such a vertically integrated model has helped Apple leap ahead of its rivals.Google will have an opportunity to do the same once it completes its purchase of Motorola Mobility.

But Microsoft still would face the huge challenge of turning RIM around. The integration would be messy and Microsoft is instead likely focused on a recent deal that put its operating system inside Nokia's devices.

Nor does RIM seem cheap enough to attract interest from private-equity firms.

Meanwhile, co-CEOs Mike Lazaridis and Jim Balsillie have resisted change. With 10% of the stock between them, any takeover might have to be hostile, adding to risks.

Shareholders need to take a more active role to force change and stop the company's downward spiral. Barring drastic changes at the company, BlackBerry shares still deserve a raspberry.

-- Rolfe Winkler

China's Stability Landed in Trouble

A village of 20,000 in open rebellion against local government points to the challenge Beijing faces in maintaining social stability in a challenging economic environment.

Like many instances of social unrest in China, the uprising in Wukan, a fishing village in Guangdong, has land at its center. Villagers claim local officials illegally sold land to a developer, pocketing the proceeds without properly compensating the residents.

Yu Jianrong, an expert on social disorder at China's Academy of Social Science, estimates that 65% of the tens of thousands of disputes that occur in China's countryside each year are related to land.

There may be more to come. Local governments' borrowing spree in 2009-10 was underpinned by the promise of land sales to repay debts. According to a National Audit Office report published in June, local governments are on the hook for 10.7 trillion yuan ($1.7 trillion), and 2.5 trillion yuan of that is guaranteed by land sales.

To put that number in perspective, total local government revenue from land sales in 2010 came to 2.9 trillion yuan. In other words, local governments have to sell land equal to almost the entire amount sold in 2010 just to cover debts falling due over the next few years. If they want funds to continue paying for roads, schools and hospitals, they will have to sell even more.

Worse, prices are falling. Data from property agency Soufun show sales of residential land down 55% year over year in November and prices down 7%. That isn't a surprise as developers slow investment in preparation for a tough 2012.

In 2009, channeling the economic stimulus through local governments and bank loans looked like a smart option. Now there is a price to be paid.

-- Tom Orlik

Yanzhou Finds Keeping

A Vow Can Be a Burden

China's Yanzhou Coal made a promise to the Australian government in 2009. Now it must deliver without shooting itself in the foot.

When the Chinese coal miner bought Australia's Felix Resources two years ago for 3.5 billion Australian dollars (US$3.5 billion), it agreed to list at least 30% of its Australian assets by the end of 2012. The deal appeared to leave all parties happy.

With a year to go, however, Yanzhou's promise has left it with a dilemma. Australia's market for initial public offerings is in the doldrums. This year's biggest deal was for just US$217 million, and total IPO volumes are 87% below year-ago levels. The market could come back to life next year, but the chances of Yanzhou getting a good price for its assets look slim amid ongoing global volatility.

Yancoal -- the company's Australian subsidiary -- has spent around A$750 million on Australian assets on top of the Felix purchase, which means it would be looking at a listing of upward of A$1.3 billion. That would be one of the largest floats since late 2009. While coal assets are hot these days, investors know Yanzhou has a firm deadline for listing.

Breaking the promise isn't an option. Enter the possibility of a reverse takeover, which Yanzhou's deputy general manager said this week is on the table. This would involve using a smaller, listed company that would in essence buy the Yancoal assets. That would see Yanzhou come through on its deal with Canberra without having to go through the costly IPO process or risk unloading 30% of its Australian business into a tough equity market.

But the move wouldn't be without risks. The backdoor listing process is under intense scrutiny from regulators in the U.S. and Canada, where a spate of accounting scandals has been partly blamed on misuse of reverse takeovers by Chinese firms. In Australia, the practice is less controversial, but mostly because it isn't very common.

Still, a reverse takeover could land Yanzhou with cumbersome and possibly unpredictable regulatory red tape. In 2009, when Gloucester Coal and Whitehaven Coal proposed a merger in which shareholders of Gloucester, the smaller of the two, didn't get a chance to vote, the case ended up in a complicated appeals process involving the takeover panel.

In theory, either an IPO or a reverse takeover should satisfy Yanzhou's commitment to the government and fulfill the goal of subjecting Yancoal to Australian disclosure laws.

But the Chinese company must tread carefully. A forced IPO would see it shed value. But a contentious reverse takeover could hurt Australian-Chinese business relations for which the Felix deal was once feted.

-- Cynthia Koons

Euro Stress Indicators Are Flashing Red

Many weeks have been called the worst of the financial crisis. But this past one has as good a claim as any. Wild swings in euro-zone bond markets and a further deterioration in bank-funding conditions have raised many stress indicators to levels last seen after Lehman's 2008 collapse. The difference this time is that policy makers have fewer bullets to fire.

In government-bond markets, the gap between 10-year French and German debt rose above two percentage points Thursday, a level unseen since the early 1990s. That, together with moves in Dutch and Finnish debt, suggests investors are starting to see a real risk of a euro-zone breakup. Meanwhile, U.K. gilt yields have fallen to record lows.

Stresses in the banking system also are building. Long-term funding has evaporated. The cost of insuring 10 million euros ($13.46 million) of European bank and insurer debt for five years via the Markit iTraxx Senior Financials index was 300,000 euros Thursday, close to a record and far over March 2009's peak of 211,000 euros.

Short-term bank borrowing rates are failing to reflect declines in expected official interest rates, as measured by overnight index swaps. The three-month Euribor/OIS spread hit 0.91 percentage point this past week, the widest since March 2009, according to Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch. That implies any central-bank rate cuts won't help much.

And European banks are having to pay through the nose to get dollars via the markets: The euro-U.S. dollar cross-currency basis swap was negative 1.3 percentage points Friday, based on RBS. This indicator went below minus-two percentage points after Lehman's collapse, but quickly recovered. This time, it has been deteriorating since April.

In 2008, central banks were able to ease strains by cutting rates and pumping liquidity into the system. But this time, interest rates are low and liquidity is abundant: Banks have 230.9 billion euros deposited overnight at the European Central Bank. They bid to deposit another 260.5 billion euros for seven days this past week at an 187 billion euro auction designed to sterilize ECB government-bond purchases. The ECB has announced new one-year refinancing operations and pledged unlimited cash to banks until the middle of 2012. That suggests there may be worse weeks to come.

-- Richard Barley

Deutsche Boerse Throws Deal Dice Again

Deutsche Boerse and NYSE Euronext knew the European competition authorities wouldn't be a pushover. But they have encountered stiff antitrust objections to their planned $17.7 billion merger. In response, the exchanges have offered to open up their clearing business to third parties. But while the move is symbolically important, it is unlikely to satisfy the deal's critics.

Deutsche Boerse believes its offer to let third parties clear new equities-index and interest-rate derivatives on Eurex is a major concession that addresses concerns over future competition. Until now, it hasn't countenanced any breach of its integrated trading and clearing model.

As for current competition, the exchanges are confident Brussels accepts that Eurex and NYSE Liffe don't compete head on. Liquidity in a given product tends to concentrate in one exchange or another. Eurex and Liffe dominate, respectively, the markets for products based on longer-term and shorter-term rates. Deutsche Boerse and NYSE Euronext have also offered to sell their single stock-option trading and clearing activities that do overlap.

Even so, Eurex and Liffe are likely to retain a 90%-plus combined share of European exchange-traded contracts -- at least until new derivatives products emerge in Europe. Eurex won't clear rival versions of the exchanges' existing products. The exchanges' argument that the merger should be judged in the context of the broader market for derivatives, 80% of which are traded over the counter, has already been rejected.

But Deutsche Boerse and NYSE Euronext's fate will be determined by the full college of European Commissioners at a ruling set for Jan. 23. Their best hope may be to encourage the EC to look beyond immediate competition concerns to longer-term shifts in Europe's financial-markets. The merger could save hard-pressed banks and insurers $3 billion in collateral. Vertical integration is an industry trend. Take the London Stock Exchange's bid for LCH.Clearnet. Brussels-led reform is fostering competition and should ultimately diminish Deutsche Boerse's market share.

Ultimately, the prospect of a European-led exchanges champion may yet prove too politically tempting to resist.

-- Matthew Curtin

Glencore Stock

Needs More

Than a Squeeze

Glencore's share price could do with a fillip. The Swiss company's stock has fallen 25% since its hyped flotation in May, as investors struggle with its unusual mix of mining and trading. Late this week, more of its shares will become tradable, offering hope of a technical bounce. For now, such hope looks overrated.

Glencore's free float is now a measly 11.7% of its share base. Its employees hold most of the rest, including CEO Ivan Glasenberg's 15.7% stake. But Glencore gained quick entry into the FTSE 100 on the promise its free float would expand to meet the 50% minimum required of foreign companies within a year of its May 24 listing. Thursday, cornerstone investors in the IPO -- including Abu Dhabi's sovereign-wealth fund, BlackRock and a Chinese mining company -- become free to sell their stakes, totaling 5.2% of shares.

For index purposes, Glencore's increased 16.9% float will be treated as a 20% free float. In turn, Glencore's weighting in FTSE and MSCI indexes will rise. Funds tracking these benchmarks will have to buy more shares. Assuming FTSE and MSCI tracker funds now hold 10% and 5% of Glencore's free float, they could have to buy 83 million more shares, Credit Suisse estimates. That will represent 7% of the increased free float, a significant chunk. Still, the impact will be phased: The FTSE's reweighting occurs Dec. 16, the MSCI's, not until the end of February. Existing investors will have had plenty of time to position themselves ahead of the changes. And some cornerstone investors might decide to sell part of their holdings.

Besides, Glencore's share price is unlikely to become detached from fundamentals for long. In May, employees are free to sell their 36% stake in the company, triggering another index reweighting. Whether this causes a stock squeeze or a share-price fall will depend on how many of Mr. Glasenberg's colleagues share his optimism about Glencore's future.

-- Andrew Peaple

Overheard: Shedding Light on Nokia's Lumia

By one early measure, Windows Phone isn't going to stop Nokia's slide. Analyst Pierre Ferragu of Bernstein Research points to Google Trends data that show Nokia's newest smartphones aren't generating much buzz with consumers. Searches for "Nokia Lumia" -- its first high-end devices based on Microsoft's mobile operating system -- peaked just after the phones were announced. And at a level that trails the maker's 2010 device, the "N8," itself a bust.

That is bad news for Nokia, which has seen its share of the world-wide smartphone market collapse from 33% to 14% over the past year, according to Strategy Analytics. Dumping its own mobile operating system in favor of Windows Phone is CEO Stephen Elop's hail Mary to recapture momentum.

But Apple and others may be too far ahead. To wit: Searches for "iPhone 4S" peaked at a level 20 times that of the Lumia devices.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,318 words
10 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
32
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

China's Day of Reckoning Isn't Here Yet

It is too early to start betting on the collapse of China.

A shaky world economy has investors understandably nervous. For many, a hard landing for China, with GDP growth for 2012 dipping sharply below the 8% mark, has moved from outlying risk to central scenario.

One measure of bearish sentiment, the Hang Seng China Enterprises Index, has fallen 35% since the end of June.

Markets have gotten ahead of themselves. Yes, China heads into the possibility of a second global downturn with more problems than it did the first. Its main weaknesses are dependence on exports, a real-estate bubble and burgeoning public debt. All are real problems. But it has enough growth momentum and room to maneuver to make a soft landing the most likely outcome.

The importance of exports as a driver of China's growth is overstated. Exports as a percentage of China's 2010 gross domestic product were 27%, down from 35% in 2007. In value-added terms, the real share of exports is even smaller. UBS economist Wang Tao sees a base case in which falling exports shave a percentage point from China's 2012 GDP growth. With GDP growth expected to be around 9% in 2011, all else being equal, that is hardly a disaster.

Real-estate investment is a key driver of China's growth, equal to around 14% of GDP in 2010. Ghost towns of empty houses raise fears that construction might be set to slow sharply. But with millions moving from the countryside to the city each year, and rising wages driving demand for higher-quality accommodations for existing city dwellers, fundamental demand is strong.

Rising debt is another worry. An investment-based response to the 2008 crisis saw local governments rack up enormous debts -- 10.7 trillion yuan ($1.68 trillion), according to the National Audit Office. Investors fear that much of that will end up as bad loans on the banks' balance sheets.

There is another way to look at the problem, though. Research firm Dragonomics calculates that China's per-capita capital stock in 2010 was just 6% to 7% of that in the U.S. Starting from a very low capital base, even an investment splurge like that in 2009-2010 is likely to have produced more bridges to somewhere than bridges to nowhere.

That should also allay the worst fears about a surge in nonperforming loans. Assets on the other side of the balance sheet mean that in many cases, local governments are good for their debts. Even if all the borrowing by local governments goes bad, China is still not maxed out on debt. The range of analysts' estimates for the total of national, local, and other debts that might end up on the Ministry of Finance's balance sheet is 60% to 85% of 2010 GDP.

That is far below public debt to GDP of 94% in the U.S. or 220% in Japan, according to International Monetary Fund figures. Unlike many other countries, China still has fiscal firepower to support growth.

China is getting riskier. Without reform, there will be a day of reckoning for the economy, but this is not it.

-- Tom Orlik

The Consequences From QE Gone Wild

The mantra of lower for longer doesn't just apply to short-term interest rates; it holds for bond yields, too.

The Bank of England's new GBP 75 billion ($116 billion) quantitative-easing program and a gloomy economic outlook will likely push U.K. government bond yields lower, particularly for long-dated debt. That will cause complications and distortions.

The BOE's program is assuming epic scale. Once total purchases reach GBP 275 billion, it will have spent the equivalent of 19% of 2010 gross domestic product. It already holds GBP 177.7 billion of non-inflation-linked gilts, or 23% of the total once government holdings are excluded, says Morgan Stanley. Purchases over the next four months, added to redemptions and interest payments, will outstrip new issuance by some GBP 60 billion, UBS estimates.

The BOE is splitting its purchases evenly between three baskets: bonds maturing in three to 10 years, 10 to 25 years and 25 years or more. Thus GBP 50 billion of buying will be beyond 10 years. But heavy issuance in recent years due to the financial crisis has been weighted toward shorter maturities.

The BOE will be buying where paper is relatively scarce. There are only GBP 74 billion of bonds maturing in more than 25 years that the BOE could buy, versus GBP 125 billion in the three- to 10-year bucket, Barclays Capital calculates. The gap between 10-year and 50-year gilt yields narrowed 0.1 percentage point Thursday.

Lower yields pose a threat to pension funds, reducing returns and increasing deficits. Companies have to make larger payments, eating into profits.

Worse, pension funds and insurers have to invest more in bonds to meet new regulatory rules on matching assets and liabilities. BOE purchases will compete directly with them.

But they should expect no respite. QE may one day reach GBP 500 billion, Citigroup thinks. The unintended savings market consequences are only starting to emerge.

-- Richard Barley

Sony Makes

A Brave Call

On Handsets

A potential deal for Sony to take full ownership of its mobile-phone joint venture with Ericsson makes strategic sense. Whether Sony could pull it off successfully is less straightforward.

The electronics giant is in talks to buy the half of Sony Ericsson it doesn't already own, in a deal worth up to $1.7 billion, The Wall Street Journal reported Thursday. The deal isn't confirmed.

Certainly, a new approach would be welcome. Sony Ericsson returned to profit in the fiscal year ended in March, after a fairly dismal run, by focusing on higher-end smartphones using the popular Google-backed Android operating system. That has been at the expense of its lower-end handsets. Sony Ericsson's share of the global cellphone market halved to 2.1% in the second quarter from two years ago, estimates Strategy Analytics. But its share of global smartphones jumped to 4.1% from 0.6% over the period.

Even so, it remains subscale. It was the seventh-largest vendor behind such giants as Apple, with 18.5% and HTC with 10.9%. More scale is crucial for Sony Ericsson to lift operating margins.

Certainly, with a single owner in control, decisions on new phones and other matters would be taken faster. Sony may be able to better integrate the handset business with its tablet PCs and hand-held game consoles, and link up the phones to its nascent online music, movie and games business.

The question is whether Sony, grappling with red ink in the television unit and the yen's strength against the euro, has the resources necessary to focus on handsets.

While some recent Sony Ericsson smartphones have earned decent reviews, Sony's recent track record of producing must-have gadgets isn't good. Coming up with a winning smartphone strategy still looks like a tall order.

-- James Simms

Overheard

Louis Vuitton is hunting crocs -- and not the brightly-colored foam clogs that are staging something of a fashion comeback.

Rather the French luxury powerhouse is buying Heng Long International.

The Singapore-based business sources, tans and processes crocodilian leather.

It's a decent sized bite at $124 million.

And like, say, steelmakers buying iron ore mines, LVMH is employing classic vertical integration: It won't be dependent on suppliers if the supply of leather falls short of demand for fancy handbags and loafers.

It's anyone's guess which part of the supply chain will be gobbled up next.

How about an LVMH ostrich ranch?

-- overheard@wsj.com

A Fear Gauge Comes Up Short --- Analysis Shows Credit-Default Swaps, a Popular Indicator of Market Health, Are Thinly Traded

By Carrick Mollenkamp and Serena Ng 
890 words
28 September 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2011, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Corrections & Amplifications

CMA, a data analytics firm, is a unit of the CME Group. A Money & Investing article Wednesday on credit-default swaps incorrectly said CMA is a unit of CMA Group.

(WSJ September 29, 2011)

(END)

Wall Street is glued to a gauge of trading fear that has surprisingly few trades behind it.

In recent years, credit-default swaps -- contracts that give the buyer the right to collect a payment from the seller if a borrower defaults on its obligations -- have risen from obscurity to an avidly tracked barometer of the financial health of everything from Bank of America Corp. to Greece.

In some cases they have even come to serve as a stand-in for stock quotes when U.S. exchanges are closed.

Before stock markets opened in New York on Aug. 23, for example, the price quoted for the cost of insuring against a default on Bank of America rose sharply, hitting a record high. A Nomura Securities trader sent out an email alert citing the derivatives price: "Ugly out there this morning."

Yet a Wall Street Journal analysis shows that actual trades in these widely cited derivatives are few and far between -- and the quotes that market observers bandy about often aren't based on actual trades at all.

While the swaps can help investors hedge risks and bet on market trends, the thin trading underlines a key shortcoming of an instrument that has a huge influence on risk perceptions. During periods of stress, the actions of a few traders can have an outsize impact on delicate market psychology.

"The market does not fully understand the limitations in trading, or the lack of liquidity, as CDS spreads are often quoted as readily as the DJ industrial average nowadays," said Hong Yan, a professor of finance at Shanghai Advanced Institute of Finance who is on leave from the University of South Carolina. "This could be potentially dangerous in a very volatile and uncertain market since CDS spreads are used much more frequently and prevalently."

The Journal analyzed data compiled by Depository Trust & Clearing Corp., a central warehouse that collects swaps trading information from investment banks. That analysis shows that even for the most popular credit-default swaps, such as those for Bank of America debt, daily trading is dwarfed by that in the stock market and often, too, in bonds.

For the week ending Sept. 16, the most recent data available, the gross notional value of swaps referencing Bank of America -- that is, the value of the contracts outstanding -- rose from the prior week by $700 million, according to Depository Trust.

By comparison, some $8.5 billion of the Charlotte, N.C., company's stock traded during the same week.

Despite the thin trading, investment banks and media outlets frequently point to swaps pricing as an indicator of the health of the global financial system's constituent parts.

On Tuesday, the limited trading was further highlighted in a Federal Reserve Bank of New York paper that analyzed three months of swaps data for both single-name corporate bonds and baskets of swaps. New York Fed officials sought to better understand how frequently the derivatives trade amid regulatory efforts to require investment banks to report real-time transaction data.

The study found little actual trading.

"A majority of the single-name reference entities traded less than once a day, whereas the most active traded over 20 times per day," the New York Fed paper said.

"The CDS market, in general, is not like the fast-trading stock market," said Darrell Duffie, a derivatives expert and finance professor at Stanford University.

That isn't to say there is no money at stake in the credit-default swap market. The net notional value of swaps outstanding on Bank of America, for instance, was $5.8 billion at Sept. 23, a similar level from the start of the year, according to Depository Trust. That is the maximum amount of money that would be exchanged in the event of a default.

The contracts have been used for years by banks to unload risks they don't want and by hedge funds and other investors to bet on changing market trends. But since the financial crisis of 2008, they have been best known as a measure of market stress.

In the case of credit-default swaps for corporate bonds, a buyer pays a sum quarterly for the derivative. If a bond defaults, the losses are covered by the seller of the contract.

Costs of the protection can rise if investors believe the underlying debt is getting riskier. Those trading the swaps often are big banks or, to a lesser degree, hedge funds.

Data vendors such as Markit Group Ltd. and CMA, a unit of CMA Group, use computer systems to extract swaps prices -- including quotes -- from electronic messages between investment banks and investors.

The companies aggregate that pricing data, which makes its way to a wider audience seeking a gauge of default risk -- an audience that may not recognize the limitations of the data they are looking at and the nature of how the derivatives trade.
